on appeals from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[December 10, 2003]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Rehnquist delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to BCRA Titles III and IV.*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This opinion addresses issues involving miscellaneous Title III and IV provisions of the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act of 2002 (BCRA), 116 Stat. 81. For the reasons discussed below, we affirm the judgment of the District Court with respect to these provisions.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7305

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7305 amends the federal Communications Act of 1934 (Communications Act) \xc2\xa7315(b), 48 Stat. 1088, as amended, 86 Stat. 4, which requires that, 45 days before a primary or 60 days before a general election, broadcast stations must sell a qualified candidate the \xe2\x80\x9clowest unit charge of the station for the same class and amount of time for the same period,\xe2\x80\x9d 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7315(b). Section 305\xe2\x80\x99s amendment, in turn, denies a candidate the benefit of that lowest unit charge unless the candidate \xe2\x80\x9cprovides written certification to the broadcast station that the candidate (and any authorized committee of the candidate) shall not make any direct reference to another candidate for the same office,\xe2\x80\x9d or the candidate, in the manner prescribed in BCRA \xc2\xa7305(a)(3), clearly identifies herself at the end of the broadcast and states that she approves of the broadcast. 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7315(b)(2)(A), (C) (Supp. 2003).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The McConnell plaintiffs challenge \xc2\xa7305. They argue that Senator McConnell\xe2\x80\x99s testimony that he plans to run advertisements critical of his opponents in the future and that he had run them in the past is sufficient to establish standing. We think not.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Article III of the Constitution limits the \xe2\x80\x9cjudicial power\xe2\x80\x9d to the resolution of \xe2\x80\x9ccases\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9ccontroversies.\xe2\x80\x9d One element of the \xe2\x80\x9cbedrock\xe2\x80\x9d case-or-controversy requirement is that plaintiffs must establish that they have standing to sue. Raines v. Byrd, 521 U. S. 811, 818 (1997). On many occasions, we have reiterated the three requirements that constitute the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98irreducible constitutional minimum\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d of standing. Vermont Agency of Natural Resources v. United States ex rel. Stevens, 529 U. S. 765, 771 (2000). First, a plaintiff must demonstrate an \xe2\x80\x9cinjury in fact,\xe2\x80\x9d which is \xe2\x80\x9cconcrete,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cdistinct and palpable,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cactual or imminent.\xe2\x80\x9d Whitmore v. Arkansas, 495 U. S. 149, 155 (1990) (internal quotation marks and citation omitted). Second, a plaintiff must establish \xe2\x80\x9ca causal connection between the injury and the conduct complained of\xe2\x80\x94the injury has to be \xe2\x80\x98fairly trace[able] to the challenged action of the defendant, and not \xe2\x80\xa6 th[e] result [of] some third party not before the court.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Lujan v. Defenders of Wildlife, 504 U. S. 555, 560\xe2\x80\x93561 (1992) (quoting Simon v. Eastern Ky. Welfare Rights Organization, 426 U. S. 26, 41\xe2\x80\x9342 (1976)). Third, a plaintiff must show the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98substantial likelihood\xe2\x80\x99 that the requested relief will remedy the alleged injury in fact.\xe2\x80\x9d Stevens, supra, at 771.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As noted above, \xc2\xa7305 amended the Communication Act\xe2\x80\x99s requirements with respect to the lowest unit charge for broadcasting time. But this price is not available to qualified candidates until 45 days before a primary election or 60 days before a general election. Because Senator McConnell\xe2\x80\x99s current term does not expire until 2009, the earliest day he could be affected by \xc2\xa7305 is 45 days before the Republican primary election in 2008. This alleged injury in fact is too remote temporally to satisfy Article III standing. See Whitmore, supra, at 158 (\xe2\x80\x9cA threatened injury must be certainly impending to constitute injury in fact\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks and citations omitted)); see also Los Angeles v. Lyons, 461 U. S. 95, 102 (1983) (A plaintiff seeking injunctive relief must show he is \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98immediately in danger of sustaining some direct injury\xe2\x80\x99 as [a] result\xe2\x80\x9d of the challenged conduct). Because we hold that the McConnell plaintiffs lack standing to challenge \xc2\xa7305, we affirm the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s dismissal of the challenge to BCRA \xc2\xa7305.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7307

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7307, which amends \xc2\xa7315(a)(1) of the Federal Election Campaign Act of 1971 (FECA), 86 Stat. 3, as added, 90 Stat. 487, increases and indexes for inflation certain FECA contribution limits. The Adams and Paul plaintiffs challenge \xc2\xa7307 in this Court. Both groups contend that they have standing to sue. Again, we disagree.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Adams plaintiffs, a group consisting of voters, organizations representing voters, and candidates, allege two injuries, and argue each is legally cognizable, \xe2\x80\x9cas established by case law outlawing electoral discrimination based on economic status \xe2\x80\xa6 and upholding the right to an equally meaningful vote \xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Appellants Adams et al. in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931740, p.\xc2\xa031.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, they assert that the increases in hard money limits enacted by \xc2\xa7307 deprive them of an equal ability to participate in the election process based on their economic status. But, to satisfy our standing requirements, a plaintiff\xe2\x80\x99s alleged injury must be an invasion of a concrete and particularized legally protected interest. Lujan, supra, at 560. We have noted that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]lthough standing in no way depends on the merits of the plaintiff\xe2\x80\x99s contention that particular conduct is illegal, \xe2\x80\xa6 it often turns on the nature and source of the claim asserted.\xe2\x80\x9d Warth v. Seldin, 422 U. S. 490, 500 (1975) (internal quotation marks and citations omitted). We have never recognized a legal right comparable to the broad and diffuse injury asserted by the Adams plaintiffs. Their reliance on this Court\xe2\x80\x99s voting rights cases is misplaced. They rely on cases requiring nondiscriminatory access to the ballot and a single, equal vote for each voter. See, e.g., Lubin v. Panish, 415 U. S. 709 (1974) (invalidating a statute requiring a ballot-access fee fixed at a percentage of the salary for the office sought because it unconstitutionally burdened the right to vote); Harper v. Virginia Bd. of Elections, 383 U. S. 663, 666\xe2\x80\x93668 (1966) (invalidating a state poll tax because it effectively denied the right to vote).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0None of these plaintiffs claims a denial of equal access to the ballot or the right to vote. Instead, the plaintiffs allege a curtailment of the scope of their participation in the electoral process. But we have noted that \xe2\x80\x9c[p]olitical \xe2\x80\x98free trade\xe2\x80\x99 does not necessarily require that all who participate in the political marketplace do so with exactly equal resources.\xe2\x80\x9d Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Massachusetts Citizens for Life, Inc., 479 U. S. 238, 257 (1986); see also Buckley v. Valeo, 424 U. S. 1, 48 (1976) (per curiam) (rejecting the asserted government interest of \xe2\x80\x9cequalizing the relative ability of individuals and groups to influence the outcome of elections\xe2\x80\x9d to justify the burden on speech presented by expenditure limits). This claim of injury by the Adams plaintiffs is, therefore, not to a legally cognizable right.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, the Adams plaintiffs-candidates contend that they have suffered a competitive injury. Their candidates \xe2\x80\x9cdo not wish to solicit or accept large campaign contributions as permitted by BCRA\xe2\x80\x9d because \xe2\x80\x9c[t]hey believe such contributions create the appearance of unequal access and influence.\xe2\x80\x9d Adams Complaint \xc2\xb6 ;53. As a result, they claim that BCRA \xc2\xa7307 puts them at a \xe2\x80\x9cfundraising disadvantage,\xe2\x80\x9d making it more difficult for them to compete in elections. See id., \xc2\xb6 ;56.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The second claimed injury is based on the same premise as the first: BCRA \xc2\xa7307\xe2\x80\x99s increased hard money limits allow plaintiffs-candidates\xe2\x80\x99 opponents to raise more money, and, consequently, the plaintiffs-candidates\xe2\x80\x99 ability to compete or participate in the electoral process is diminished. But they cannot show that their alleged injury is \xe2\x80\x9cfairly traceable\xe2\x80\x9d to BCRA \xc2\xa7307. See Lujan, supra, at 562. Their alleged inability to compete stems not from the operation of \xc2\xa7307, but from their own personal \xe2\x80\x9cwish\xe2\x80\x9d not to solicit or accept large contributions, i.e., their personal choice. Accordingly, the Adams plaintiffs fail here to allege an injury in fact that is \xe2\x80\x9cfairly traceable\xe2\x80\x9d to BCRA.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Paul plaintiffs maintain that BCRA \xc2\xa7307 violates the Freedom of Press Clause of the First Amendment. They contend that their political campaigns and public interest advocacy involve traditional press activities and that, therefore, they are protected by the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s guarantee of the freedom of press. The Paul plaintiffs argue that the contribution limits imposed by BCRA \xc2\xa7307, together with the individual and political action committee contribution limitations of FECA \xc2\xa7315, impose unconstitutional editorial control upon candidates and their campaigns. The Paul plaintiffs argue that by imposing economic burdens upon them, but not upon the institutional media, see 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7431(9)(B)(i) (exempting \xe2\x80\x9cany news story, commentary, or editorial distributed through the facilities of any broadcasting station, newspaper, magazine, or other periodical publication, unless such facilities are owned or controlled by any political party, political committee, or candidate\xe2\x80\x9d from the definition of expenditure), BCRA \xc2\xa7307 and FECA \xc2\xa7315 violate the freedom of the press.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Paul plaintiffs cannot show the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98substantial likelihood\xe2\x80\x99 that the requested relief will remedy [their] alleged injury in fact,\xe2\x80\x9d Stevens, 529 U. S., at 771. The relief the Paul plaintiffs seek is for this Court to strike down the contribution limits, removing the alleged disparate editorial controls and economic burdens imposed on them. But \xc2\xa7307 merely increased and indexed for inflation certain FECA contribution limits. This Court has no power to adjudicate a challenge to the FECA limits in this case because challenges to the constitutionality of FECA provisions are subject to direct review before an appropriate en banc court of appeals, as provided in 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7437h, not in the three-judge District Court convened pursuant to BCRA \xc2\xa7403(a). Although the Court has jurisdiction to hear a challenge to \xc2\xa7307, if the Court were to strike down the increases and indexes established by BCRA \xc2\xa7307, it would not remedy the Paul plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 alleged injury because both the limitations imposed by FECA and the exemption for news media would remain unchanged. A ruling in the Paul plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 favor, therefore, would not redress their alleged injury, and they accordingly lack standing. See Steel Co. v. Citizens for Better Environment, 523 U. S. 83, 105\xe2\x80\x93110 (1998).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the reasons above, we affirm the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s dismissal of the Adams and Paul plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenges to BCRA \xc2\xa7307 for lack of standing.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7304, 316, and 319

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7304 and 316, which amend FECA \xc2\xa7315, and BCRA \xc2\xa7319, which adds FECA \xc2\xa7315A, collectively known as the \xe2\x80\x9cmillionaire provisions,\xe2\x80\x9d provide for a series of staggered increases in otherwise applicable contribution-to-candidate limits if the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s opponent spends a triggering amount of his personal funds.[Footnote 1] The provisions also eliminate the coordinated expenditure limits in certain circumstances.[Footnote 2]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In their challenge to the millionaire provisions, the Adams plaintiffs allege the same injuries that they alleged with regard to BCRA \xc2\xa7307. For the reasons discussed above, they fail to allege a cognizable injury that is \xe2\x80\x9cfairly traceable\xe2\x80\x9d to BCRA. Additionally, as the District Court noted, \xe2\x80\x9cnone of the Adams plaintiffs is a candidate in an election affected by the millionaire provisions\xe2\x80\x94i.e., one in which an opponent chooses to spend the triggering amount in his own funds\xe2\x80\x94and it would be purely \xe2\x80\x98conjectural\xe2\x80\x99 for the court to assume that any plaintiff ever will be.\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 176, 431 (DC 2003) (case below) (Henderson, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part) (quoting Lujan, 504 U. S., at 560). We affirm the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s dismissal of the Adams plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenge to the millionaire provisions for lack of standing.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7311

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0FECA \xc2\xa7318 requires that certain communications \xe2\x80\x9cauthorized\xe2\x80\x9d by a candidate or his political committee clearly identify the candidate or committee or, if not so authorized, identify the payor and announce the lack of authorization. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441d (main ed. and Supp. 2003). BCRA \xc2\xa7311 makes several amendments to FECA \xc2\xa7318, among them the expansion of this identification regime to include disbursements for \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications\xe2\x80\x9d as defined in BCRA \xc2\xa7201.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The McConnell and Chamber of Commerce plaintiffs challenge BCRA \xc2\xa7311 by simply noting that \xc2\xa7311, along with all of the \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications\xe2\x80\x9d provisions of BCRA, is unconstitutional. We disagree. We think BCRA \xc2\xa7311\xe2\x80\x99s inclusion of electioneering communications in the FECA \xc2\xa7318 disclosure regime bears a sufficient relationship to the important governmental interest of \xe2\x80\x9cshed[ding] the light of publicity\xe2\x80\x9d on campaign financing. Buckley, 424 U. S., at 81. Assuming as we must that FECA \xc2\xa7318 is valid to begin with, and that FECA \xc2\xa7318 is valid as amended by BCRA \xc2\xa7311\xe2\x80\x99s amendments other than the inclusion of electioneering communications, the challenged inclusion of electioneering communications is not itself unconstitutional. We affirm the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision upholding \xc2\xa7311\xe2\x80\x99s expansion of FECA \xc2\xa7318(a) to include disclosure of disbursements for electioneering communications.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7318

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7318, which adds FECA \xc2\xa7324, prohibits individuals \xe2\x80\x9c17 years old or younger\xe2\x80\x9d from making contributions to candidates and contributions or donations to political parties. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441k (Supp. 2003). The McConnell and Echols plaintiffs challenge the provision; they argue that \xc2\xa7318 violates the First Amendment rights of minors. We agree.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Minors enjoy the protection of the First Amendment. See, e.g., Tinker v. Des Moines Independent Community School Dist., 393 U. S. 503, 511\xe2\x80\x93513 (1969). Limitations on the amount that an individual may contribute to a candidate or political committee impinge on the protected freedoms of expression and association. See Buckley, supra, at 20\xe2\x80\x9322. When the Government burdens the right to contribute, we apply heightened scrutiny. See ante, at 25\xe2\x80\x9326 (joint opinion of Stevens and O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, JJ.) (\xe2\x80\x9c[A] contribution limit involving even \xe2\x80\x98significant interference\xe2\x80\x99 with associational rights is nevertheless valid if it satisfies the \xe2\x80\x98lesser demand\xe2\x80\x99 of being \xe2\x80\x98closely drawn\xe2\x80\x99 to match a \xe2\x80\x98sufficiently important interest.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Beaumont, 539 U. S. ___, ___ (2003) (slip op., at 15)). We ask whether there is a \xe2\x80\x9csufficiently important interest\xe2\x80\x9d and whether the statute is \xe2\x80\x9cclosely drawn\xe2\x80\x9d to avoid unnecessary abridgment of First Amendment freedoms. Ante, at 25\xe2\x80\x9326; Buckley, 424 U. S., at 25. The Government asserts that the provision protects against corruption by conduit; that is, donations by parents through their minor children to circumvent contribution limits applicable to the parents. But the Government offers scant evidence of this form of evasion.[Footnote 3] Perhaps the Government\xe2\x80\x99s slim evidence results from sufficient deterrence of such activities by \xc2\xa7320 of FECA, which prohibits any person from \xe2\x80\x9cmak[ing] a contribution in the name of another person\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cknowingly accept[ing] a contribution made by one person in the name of another,\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441f. Absent a more convincing case of the claimed evil, this interest is simply too attenuated for \xc2\xa7318 to withstand heightened scrutiny. See Nixon v. Shrink Missouri Government PAC, 528 U. S. 377, 391 (2000) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe quantum of empirical evidence needed to satisfy heightened judicial scrutiny of legislative judgments will vary up or down with the novelty and plausibility of the justification raised\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even assuming, arguendo, the Government advances an important interest, the provision is overinclusive. The States have adopted a variety of more tailored approaches\xe2\x80\x94e.g., counting contributions by minors against the total permitted for a parent or family unit, imposing a lower cap on contributions by minors, and prohibiting contributions by very young children. Without deciding whether any of these alternatives is sufficiently tailored, we hold that the provision here sweeps too broadly. We therefore affirm the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision striking down \xc2\xa7318 as unconstitutional.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7403(b)

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The National Right to Life plaintiffs argue that the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s grant of intervention to the intervenor-defendants, pursuant to Federal Rule of Civil Procedure 24(a) and BCRA \xc2\xa7403(b), must be reversed because the intervenor-defendants lack Article III standing. It is clear, however, that the Federal Election Commission (FEC) has standing, and therefore we need not address the standing of the intervenor-defendants, whose position here is identical to the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s. See, e.g., Clinton v. City of New York, 524 U. S. 417, 431\xe2\x80\x93432, n.\xc2\xa019 (1998); Bowsher v. Synar, 478 U. S. 714, 721 (1986). Cf. Diamond v. Charles, 476 U. S. 54, 68\xe2\x80\x9369, n.\xc2\xa021 (1986) (reserving the question for another day).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the foregoing reasons, we affirm the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s judgment finding the plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenges to BCRA \xc2\xa7305, \xc2\xa7307, and the millionaire provisions nonjusticiable, striking down as unconstitutional BCRA \xc2\xa7318, and upholding BCRA \xc2\xa7311. The judgment of the District Court is

\n

Affirmed.

\n

*\xc2\xa0Justice O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, Justice Scalia, Justice Kennedy, and Justice Souter join this opinion in its entirety. Justice Stevens, Justice Ginsburg, and Justice Breyer join this opinion, except with respect to BCRA \xc2\xa7305. Justice Thomas joins this opinion with respect to BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7304, 305, 307, 316, 319, and 403(b).

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0To qualify for increased candidate contribution limits, the \xe2\x80\x9copposition personal funds amount,\xe2\x80\x9d which depends on expenditures by a candidate and her self-financed opponent, must exceed a \xe2\x80\x9cthreshold amount.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441a(i)(1)(D), 441a\xe2\x80\x931(a)(2)(A) (Supp. 2003).

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0If the \xe2\x80\x9copposition personal funds amount\xe2\x80\x9d is at least 10 times the \xe2\x80\x9cthreshold amount\xe2\x80\x9d in a Senate race, or exceeds $350,000 in a House of Representatives race, the coordinated party expenditure limits do not apply. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441a(i)(1)(C)(iii), 441a\xe2\x80\x931(a)(1)(C).

\nFootnote 3

\xc2\xa0Although some examples were presented to the District Court, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 176, 588\xe2\x80\x93590 (2003) (Kollar-Kotelly, J.), none were offered to this Court.

\nFEDERAL ELECTION COMMISSION, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on appeals from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[December 10, 2003]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Stevens and Justice O\xe2\x80\x99Connor delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to BCRA Titles I and II.*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act of 2002 (BCRA), 116 Stat. 81, contains a series of amendments to the Federal Election Campaign Act of 1971 (FECA), 86 Stat. 11, as amended, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7431 et seq. (main ed. and Supp. 2003), the Communications Act of 1934, 48 Stat. 1088, as amended, 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315, and other portions of the United States Code, 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7607 (Supp. 2003), 36 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7510\xe2\x80\x93511, that are challenged in these cases.[Footnote 1] In this opinion we discuss Titles I and II of BCRA. The opinion of the Court delivered by The Chief Justice, post, p.\xc2\xa0___, discusses Titles III and IV, and the opinion of the Court delivered by Justice Breyer, post, p. ___, discusses Title V.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0More than a century ago the \xe2\x80\x9csober-minded Elihu Root\xe2\x80\x9d advocated legislation that would prohibit political contributions by corporations in order to prevent \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the great aggregations of wealth, from using their corporate funds, directly or indirectly,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to elect legislators who would \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98vote for their protection and the advancement of their interests as against those of the public.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Automobile Workers, 352 U. S. 567, 571 (1957) (quoting E. Root, Addresses on Government and Citizenship 143 (R. Bacon & J. Scott eds. 1916)). In Root\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, such legislation would \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98strik[e] at a constantly growing evil which has done more to shake the confidence of the plain people of small means of this country in our political institutions than any other practice which has ever obtained since the foundation of our Government.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 352 U. S., at 571. The Congress of the United States has repeatedly enacted legislation endorsing Root\xe2\x80\x99s judgment.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA is the most recent federal enactment designed \xe2\x80\x9cto purge national politics of what was conceived to be the pernicious influence of \xe2\x80\x98big money\xe2\x80\x99 campaign contributions.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 572. As Justice Frankfurter explained in his opinion for the Court in Automobile Workers, the first such enactment responded to President Theodore Roosevelt\xe2\x80\x99s call for legislation forbidding all contributions by corporations \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98to any political committee or for any political purpose.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (quoting 40 Cong. Rec. 96 (1906)). In his annual message to Congress in December 1905, President Roosevelt stated that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98directors should not be permitted to use stockholders\xe2\x80\x99 money\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d for political purposes, and he recommended that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98a prohibition\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d on corporate political contributions \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98would be, as far as it went, an effective method of stopping the evils aimed at in corrupt practices acts.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 352 U. S., at 352. The resulting 1907 statute completely banned corporate contributions of \xe2\x80\x9cmoney \xe2\x80\xa6 in connection with\xe2\x80\x9d any federal election. Tillman Act, ch. 420, 34 Stat. 864. Congress soon amended the statute to require the public disclosure of certain contributions and expenditures and to place \xe2\x80\x9cmaximum limits on the amounts that congressional candidates could spend in seeking nomination and election.\xe2\x80\x9d Automobile Workers, supra, at 575\xe2\x80\x93576.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In 1925 Congress extended the prohibition of \xe2\x80\x9ccontributions\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cto include \xe2\x80\x98anything of value,\xe2\x80\x99 and made acceptance of a corporate contribution as well as the giving of such a contribution a crime.\xe2\x80\x9d Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. National Right to Work Comm., 459 U. S. 197, 209 (1982) (citing Federal Corrupt Practices Act, 1925, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7301, 313, 43 Stat. 1070, 1074). During the debates preceding that amendment, a leading Senator characterized \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the apparent hold on political parties which business interests and certain organizations seek and sometimes obtain by reason of liberal campaign contributions\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d as \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98one of the great political evils of the time.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Automobile Workers, supra, at 576 (quoting 65 Cong. Rec. 9507\xe2\x80\x939508 (1924)). We upheld the amended statute against a constitutional challenge, observing that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he power of Congress to protect the election of President and Vice President from corruption being clear, the choice of means to that end presents a question primarily addressed to the judgment of Congress.\xe2\x80\x9d Burroughs v. United States, 290 U. S. 534, 547 (1934).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Congress\xe2\x80\x99 historical concern with the \xe2\x80\x9cpolitical potentialities of wealth\xe2\x80\x9d and their \xe2\x80\x9cuntoward consequences for the democratic process,\xe2\x80\x9d Automobile Workers, supra, at 577\xe2\x80\x93578, has long reached beyond corporate money. During and shortly after World War II, Congress reacted to the \xe2\x80\x9cenormous financial outlays\xe2\x80\x9d made by some unions in connection with national elections. 352 U. S., at 579. Congress first restricted union contributions in the Hatch Act, 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7610,[Footnote 2] and it later prohibited \xe2\x80\x9cunion contributions in connection with federal elections \xe2\x80\xa6 altogether.\xe2\x80\x9d National Right to Work, supra, at 209 (citing War Labor Disputes Act (Smith-Connally Anti-Strike Act), ch. 144, \xc2\xa79, 57 Stat. 167). Congress subsequently extended that prohibition to cover unions\xe2\x80\x99 election-related expenditures as well as contributions, and it broadened the coverage of federal campaigns to include both primary and general elections. Labor Management Relations Act, 1947 (Taft-Hartley Act), 61 Stat. 136. See Automobile Workers, supra, at 578\xe2\x80\x93584. During the consideration of those measures, legislators repeatedly voiced their concerns regarding the pernicious influence of large campaign contributions. See 93 Cong. Rec. 3428, 3522 (1947); H.\xc2\xa0R. Rep. No. 245, 80th Cong., 1st Sess. (1947); S.\xc2\xa0Rep. No. 1, 80th Cong., 1st Sess., pt. 2 (1947); H.\xc2\xa0R. Rep. No. 2093, 78th Cong., 2d Sess. (1945). As we noted in a unanimous opinion recalling this history, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9ccareful legislative adjustment of the federal election laws, in a \xe2\x80\x98cautious advance, step by step,\xe2\x80\x99 to account for the particular legal and economic attributes of corporations and labor organizations warrants considerable deference.\xe2\x80\x9d National Right to Work, 352 U. S., at 209 (citations omitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In early 1972 Congress continued its steady improvement of the national election laws by enacting FECA, 86 Stat. 3. As first enacted, that statute required disclosure of all contributions exceeding $100 and of expenditures by candidates and political committees that spent more than $1,000 per year. Id., at 11\xe2\x80\x9319. It also prohibited contributions made in the name of another person, id., at 19, and by Government contractors, id., at 10. The law ratified the earlier prohibition on the use of corporate and union general treasury funds for political contributions and expenditures, but it expressly permitted corporations and unions to establish and administer separate segregated funds (commonly known as political action committees, or PACs) for election-related contributions and expenditures. Id., at 12\xe2\x80\x9313.[Footnote 3] See Pipefitters v. United States, 407 U. S. 385, 409\xe2\x80\x93410 (1972).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As the 1972 presidential elections made clear, however, FECA\xe2\x80\x99s passage did not deter unseemly fundraising and campaign practices. Evidence of those practices persuaded Congress to enact the Federal Election Campaign Act Amendments of 1974, 88 Stat. 1263. Reviewing a constitutional challenge to the amendments, the Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit described them as \xe2\x80\x9cby far the most comprehensive \xe2\x80\xa6 reform legislation [ever] passed by Congress concerning the election of the President, Vice-President and members of Congress.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley v. Valeo, 519 F.\xc2\xa02d 821, 831 (1975) (en banc) (per curiam).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The 1974 amendments closed the loophole that had allowed candidates to use an unlimited number of political committees for fundraising purposes and thereby to circumvent the limits on individual committees\xe2\x80\x99 receipts and disbursements. They also limited individual political contributions to any single candidate to $1,000 per election, with an overall annual limitation of $25,000 by any contributor; imposed ceilings on spending by candidates and political parties for national conventions; required reporting and public disclosure of contributions and expenditures exceeding certain limits; and established the Federal Election Commission (FEC) to administer and enforce the legislation. Id., at 831\xe2\x80\x93834.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court of Appeals upheld the 1974 amendments almost in their entirety.[Footnote 4] It concluded that the clear and compelling interest in preserving the integrity of the electoral process provided a sufficient basis for sustaining the substantive provisions of the Act. Id., at 841. The court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion relied heavily on findings that large contributions facilitated access to public officials[Footnote 5] and described methods of evading the contribution limits that had enabled contributors of massive sums to avoid disclosure. Id., at 837\xe2\x80\x93841.[Footnote 6]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court of Appeals upheld the provisions establishing contribution and expenditure limitations on the theory that they should be viewed as regulations of conduct rather than speech. Id., at 840\xe2\x80\x93841 (citing United States v. O\xe2\x80\x99Brien, 391 U. S. 367, 376\xe2\x80\x93377 (1968)). This Court, however, concluded that each set of limitations raised serious\xe2\x80\x94though different\xe2\x80\x94concerns under the First Amendment. Buckley v. Valeo, 424 U. S. 1, 14\xe2\x80\x9323 (1976) (per curiam). We treated the limitations on candidate and individual expenditures as direct restraints on speech, but we observed that the contribution limitations, in contrast, imposed only \xe2\x80\x9ca marginal restriction upon the contributor\xe2\x80\x99s ability to engage in free communication.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 20\xe2\x80\x9321. Considering the \xe2\x80\x9cdeeply disturbing examples\xe2\x80\x9d of corruption related to candidate contributions discussed in the Court of Appeals\xe2\x80\x99 opinion, we determined that limiting contributions served an interest in protecting \xe2\x80\x9cthe integrity of our system of representative democracy.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 26\xe2\x80\x9327. In the end, the Act\xe2\x80\x99s primary purpose\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cto limit the actuality and appearance of corruption resulting from large individual financial contributions\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94provided \xe2\x80\x9ca constitutionally sufficient justification for the $1,000 contribution limitation.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 26.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We prefaced our analysis of the $1,000 limitation on expenditures by observing that it broadly encompassed every expenditure \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98relative to a clearly identified candidate.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 39 (quoting 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7608(e)(1) (1970 ed., Supp. IV)). To avoid vagueness concerns we construed that phrase to apply only to \xe2\x80\x9ccommunications that in express terms advocate the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate for federal office.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 42\xe2\x80\x93\n44. We concluded, however, that as so narrowed, the provision would not provide effective protection against the dangers of quid pro quo arrangements, because persons and groups could eschew expenditures that expressly advocated the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate while remaining \xe2\x80\x9cfree to spend as much as they want to promote the candidate and his views.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 45. We also rejected the argument that the expenditure limits were necessary to prevent attempts to circumvent the Act\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limits, because FECA already treated expenditures controlled by or coordinated with the candidate as contributions, and we were not persuaded that independent expenditures posed the same risk of real or apparent corruption as coordinated expenditures. Id., at 46\xe2\x80\x9347. We therefore held that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 interest in preventing real or apparent corruption was inadequate to justify the heavy burdens on the freedoms of expression and association that the expenditure limits imposed.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We upheld all of the disclosure and reporting requirements in the Act that were challenged on appeal to this Court after finding that they vindicated three important interests: providing the electorate with relevant information about the candidates and their supporters; deterring actual corruption and discouraging the use of money for improper purposes; and facilitating enforcement of the prohibitions in the Act. Id., at 66\xe2\x80\x9368. In order to avoid an overbreadth problem, however, we placed the same narrowing construction on the term \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditure\xe2\x80\x9d in the disclosure context that we had adopted in the context of the expenditure limitations. Thus, we construed the reporting requirement for persons making expenditures of more than $100 in a year \xe2\x80\x9cto reach only funds used for communications that expressly advocate the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 80 (footnote omitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Our opinion in Buckley addressed issues that primarily related to contributions and expenditures by individuals, since none of the parties challenged the prohibition on contributions by corporations and labor unions. We noted, however, that the statute authorized the use of corporate and union resources to form and administer segregated funds that could be used for political purposes. Id., at 28\xe2\x80\x9329, n.\xc2\xa031; see also n.\xc2\xa03, supra.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Three important developments in the years after our decision in Buckley persuaded Congress that further legislation was necessary to regulate the role that corporations, unions, and wealthy contributors play in the electoral process. As a preface to our discussion of the specific provisions of BCRA, we comment briefly on the increased importance of \xe2\x80\x9csoft money,\xe2\x80\x9d the proliferation of \xe2\x80\x9cissue ads,\xe2\x80\x9d and the disturbing findings of a Senate investigation into campaign practices related to the 1996 federal elections.

\n

Soft Money

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Under FECA, \xe2\x80\x9ccontributions\xe2\x80\x9d must be made with funds that are subject to the Act\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements and source and amount limitations. Such funds are known as \xe2\x80\x9cfederal\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9chard\xe2\x80\x9d money. FECA defines the term \xe2\x80\x9ccontribution,\xe2\x80\x9d however, to include only the gift or advance of anything of value \xe2\x80\x9cmade by any person for the purpose of influencing any election for Federal office.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7431(8)(A)(i) (emphasis added). Donations made solely for the purpose of influencing state or local elections are therefore unaffected by FECA\xe2\x80\x99s requirements and prohibitions. As a result, prior to the enactment of BCRA, federal law permitted corporations and unions, as well as individuals who had already made the maximum permissible contributions to federal candidates, to contribute \xe2\x80\x9cnonfederal money\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94also known as \xe2\x80\x9csoft money\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94to political parties for activities intended to influence state or local elections.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Shortly after Buckley was decided, questions arose concerning the treatment of contributions intended to influence both federal and state elections. Although a literal reading of FECA\xe2\x80\x99s definition of \xe2\x80\x9ccontribution\xe2\x80\x9d would have required such activities to be funded with hard money, the FEC ruled that political parties could fund mixed-purpose activities\xe2\x80\x94including get-out-the-vote drives and generic party advertising\xe2\x80\x94in part with soft money.[Footnote 7] In 1995 the FEC concluded that the parties could also use soft money to defray the costs of \xe2\x80\x9clegislative advocacy media advertisements,\xe2\x80\x9d even if the ads mentioned the name of a federal candidate, so long as they did not expressly advocate the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s election or defeat. FEC Advisory Op. 1995\xe2\x80\x9325.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As the permissible uses of soft money expanded, the amount of soft money raised and spent by the national political parties increased exponentially. Of the two major parties\xe2\x80\x99 total spending, soft money accounted for 5% ($21.6 million) in 1984, 11% ($45 million) in 1988, 16% ($80 million) in 1992, 30% ($272 million) in 1996, and 42% ($498 million) in 2000.[Footnote 8] The national parties transferred large amounts of their soft money to the state parties, which were allowed to use a larger percentage of soft money to finance mixed-purpose activities under FEC rules.[Footnote 9] In the year 2000, for example, the national parties diverted $280 million\xe2\x80\x94more than half of their soft money\xe2\x80\x94to state parties.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Many contributions of soft money were dramatically larger than the contributions of hard money permitted by FECA. For example, in 1996 the top five corporate soft-money donors gave, in total, more than $9 million in nonfederal funds to the two national party committees.[Footnote 10] In the most recent election cycle the political parties raised almost $300 million\xe2\x80\x9460% of their total soft-money fundraising\xe2\x80\x94from just 800 donors, each of which contributed a minimum of $120,000.[Footnote 11] Moreover, the largest corporate donors often made substantial contributions to both parties.[Footnote 12] Such practices corroborate evidence indicating that many corporate contributions were motivated by a desire for access to candidates and a fear of being placed at a disadvantage in the legislative process relative to other contributors, rather than by ideological support for the candidates and parties.[Footnote 13]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Not only were such soft-money contributions often designed to gain access to federal candidates, but they were in many cases solicited by the candidates themselves. Candidates often directed potential donors to party committees and tax-exempt organizations that could legally accept soft money. For example, a federal legislator running for reelection solicited soft money from a supporter by advising him that even though he had already \xe2\x80\x9ccontributed the legal maximum\xe2\x80\x9d to the campaign committee, he could still make an additional contribution to a joint program supporting federal, state, and local candidates of his party.[Footnote 14] Such solicitations were not uncommon.[Footnote 15]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The solicitation, transfer, and use of soft money thus enabled parties and candidates to circumvent FECA\xe2\x80\x99s limitations on the source and amount of contributions in connection with federal elections.

\n

Issue Advertising

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In Buckley we construed FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure and reporting requirements, as well as its expenditure limitations, \xe2\x80\x9cto reach only funds used for communications that expressly advocate the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 80 (footnote omitted). As a result of that strict reading of the statute, the use or omission of \xe2\x80\x9cmagic words\xe2\x80\x9d such as \xe2\x80\x9cElect John Smith\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cVote Against Jane Doe\xe2\x80\x9d marked a bright statutory line separating \xe2\x80\x9cexpress advocacy\xe2\x80\x9d from \xe2\x80\x9cissue advocacy.\xe2\x80\x9d See id., at 44, n. 52. Express advocacy was subject to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s limitations and could be financed only using hard money. The political parties, in other words, could not use soft money to sponsor ads that used any magic words, and corporations and unions could not fund such ads out of their general treasuries. So-called issue ads, on the other hand, not only could be financed with soft money, but could be aired without disclosing the identity of, or any other information about, their sponsors.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0While the distinction between \xe2\x80\x9cissue\xe2\x80\x9d and express advocacy seemed neat in theory, the two categories of advertisements proved functionally identical in important respects. Both were used to advocate the election or defeat of clearly identified federal candidates, even though the so-called issue ads eschewed the use of magic words.[Footnote 16] Little difference existed, for example, between an ad that urged viewers to \xe2\x80\x9cvote against Jane Doe\xe2\x80\x9d and one that condemned Jane Doe\xe2\x80\x99s record on a particular issue before exhorting viewers to \xe2\x80\x9ccall Jane Doe and tell her what you think.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 17] Indeed, campaign professionals testified that the most effective campaign ads, like the most effective commercials for products such as Coca-Cola, should, and did, avoid the use of the magic words.[Footnote 18] Moreover, the conclusion that such ads were specifically intended to affect election results was confirmed by the fact that almost all of them aired in the 60 days immediately preceding a federal election.[Footnote 19] Corporations and unions spent hundreds of millions of dollars of their general funds to pay for these ads,[Footnote 20] and those expenditures, like soft-money donations to the political parties, were unregulated under FECA. Indeed, the ads were attractive to organizations and candidates precisely because they were beyond FECA\xe2\x80\x99s reach, enabling candidates and their parties to work closely with friendly interest groups to sponsor so-called issue ads when the candidates themselves were running out of money.[Footnote 21]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Because FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements did not apply to so-called issue ads, sponsors of such ads often used misleading names to conceal their identity. \xe2\x80\x9cCitizens for Better Medicare,\xe2\x80\x9d for instance, was not a grassroots organization of citizens, as its name might suggest, but was instead a platform for an association of drug manufacturers.[Footnote 22] And \xe2\x80\x9cRepublicans for Clean Air,\xe2\x80\x9d which ran ads in the 2000 Republican Presidential primary, was actually an organization consisting of just two individuals\xe2\x80\x94brothers who together spent $25 million on ads supporting their favored candidate.[Footnote 23]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0While the public may not have been fully informed about the sponsorship of so-called issue ads, the record indicates that candidates and officeholders often were. A former Senator confirmed that candidates and officials knew who their friends were and \xe2\x80\x9csometimes suggest[ed] that corporations or individuals make donations to interest groups that run \xe2\x80\x98issue ads.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 24] As with soft-money contributions, political parties and candidates used the availability of so-called issue ads to circumvent FECA\xe2\x80\x99s limitations, asking donors who contributed their permitted quota of hard money to give money to nonprofit corporations to spend on \xe2\x80\x9cissue\xe2\x80\x9d advocacy.[Footnote 25]

\n

Senate Committee Investigation

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In 1998 the Senate Committee on Governmental Affairs issued a six-volume report summarizing the results of an extensive investigation into the campaign practices in the 1996 federal elections. The report gave particular attention to the effect of soft money on the American political system, including elected officials\xe2\x80\x99 practice of granting special access in return for political contributions.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The committee\xe2\x80\x99s principal findings relating to Democratic Party fundraising were set forth in the majority\xe2\x80\x99s report, while the minority report primarily described Republican practices. The two reports reached consensus, however, on certain central propositions. They agreed that the \xe2\x80\x9csoft money loophole\xe2\x80\x9d had led to a \xe2\x80\x9cmeltdown\xe2\x80\x9d of the campaign finance system that had been intended \xe2\x80\x9cto keep corporate, union and large individual contributions from influencing the electoral process.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 26] One Senator stated that \xe2\x80\x9cthe hearings provided overwhelming evi-\ndence that the twin loopholes of soft money and bogus issue advertising have virtually destroyed our campaign finance laws, leaving us with little more than a pile of legal rubble.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 27]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The report was critical of both parties\xe2\x80\x99 methods of raising soft money, as well as their use of those funds. It concluded that both parties promised and provided special access to candidates and senior Government officials in exchange for large soft-money contributions. The Committee majority described the White House coffees that rewarded major donors with access to President Clinton,[Footnote 28] and the courtesies extended to an international businessman named Roger Tamraz, who candidly acknowledged that his donations of about $300,000 to the DNC and to state parties were motivated by his interest in gaining the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s support for an oil-line project in the Caucasus.[Footnote 29] The minority described the promotional materials used by the RNC\xe2\x80\x99s two principal donor programs, \xe2\x80\x9cTeam 100\xe2\x80\x9d and the \xe2\x80\x9cRepublican Eagles,\xe2\x80\x9d which promised \xe2\x80\x9cspecial access to high-ranking Republican elected officials, including governors, senators, and representatives.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 30] One fundraising letter recited that the chairman of the RNC had personally escorted a donor on appointments that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98turned out to be very significant in legislation affecting public utility holding companies\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and made the donor \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98a hero in his industry.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 31]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In 1996 both parties began to use large amounts of soft money to pay for issue advertising designed to influence federal elections. The Committee found such ads highly problematic for two reasons. Since they accomplished the same purposes as express advocacy (which could lawfully be funded only with hard money), the ads enabled unions, corporations, and wealthy contributors to circumvent protections that FECA was intended to provide. Moreover, though ostensibly independent of the candidates, the ads were often actually coordinated with, and controlled by, the campaigns.[Footnote 32] The ads thus provided a means for evading FECA\xe2\x80\x99s candidate contribution limits.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The report also emphasized the role of state and local parties. While the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s allocation regime permitted national parties to use soft money to pay for up to 40% of the costs of both generic voter activities and issue advertising, they allowed state and local parties to use larger percentages of soft money for those purposes.[Footnote 33] For that reason, national parties often made substantial transfers of soft money to \xe2\x80\x9cstate and local political parties for \xe2\x80\x98generic voter activities\xe2\x80\x99 that in fact ultimately benefit[ed] federal candidates because the funds for all practical purposes remain[ed] under the control of the national committees.\xe2\x80\x9d The report concluded that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he use of such soft money thus allow[ed] more corporate, union treasury, and large contributions from wealthy individuals into the system.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 34]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The report discussed potential reforms, including a ban on soft money at the national and state party levels and restrictions on sham issue advocacy by nonparty groups.[Footnote 35] The majority expressed the view that a ban on the raising of soft money by national party committees would effectively address the use of union and corporate general treasury funds in the federal political process only if it required that candidate-specific ads be funded with hard money.[Footnote 36] The minority similarly recommended the elimination of soft-money contributions to political parties from individuals, corporations, and unions, as well as \xe2\x80\x9creforms addressing candidate advertisements masquerading as issue ads.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 37]

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In BCRA, Congress enacted many of the committee\xe2\x80\x99s proposed reforms. BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s central provisions are designed to address Congress\xe2\x80\x99 concerns about the increasing use of soft money and issue advertising to influence federal elections. Title I regulates the use of soft money by political parties, officeholders, and candidates. Title II primarily prohibits corporations and labor unions from using general treasury funds for communications that are intended to, or have the effect of, influencing the outcome of federal elections.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 403 of BCRA provides special rules for actions challenging the constitutionality of any of the Act\xe2\x80\x99s provisions. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. A. \xc2\xa7437h note (Supp. 2003). Eleven such actions were filed promptly after the statute went into effect in March 2002. As required by \xc2\xa7403, those actions were filed in the District Court for the District of Columbia and heard by a three-judge court. Section 403 directed the District Court to advance the cases on the docket and to expedite their disposition \xe2\x80\x9cto the greatest possible extent.\xe2\x80\x9d The court received a voluminous record compiled by the parties and ultimately delivered a decision embodied in a two-judge per curiam opinion and three separate, lengthy opinions, each of which contained extensive commentary on the facts and a careful analysis of the legal issues. 251 F. Supp. 2d 176 (2003). The three judges reached unanimity on certain issues but differed on many. Their judgment, entered on May 1, 2003, held some parts of BCRA unconstitutional and upheld others. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 948.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As authorized by \xc2\xa7403, all of the losing parties filed direct appeals to this Court within 10 days. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7437h note. On June 5, 2003, we noted probable jurisdiction and ordered the parties to comply with an expedited briefing schedule and present their oral arguments at a special hearing on September 8, 2003. 539 U. S. ___. To simplify the presentation, we directed the parties challenging provisions of BCRA to proceed first on all issues, whether or not they prevailed on any issue in the District Court. Ibid. Mindful of \xc2\xa7403\xe2\x80\x99s instruction that we expedite our disposition of these appeals to the greatest extent possible, we also consider each of the issues in order. Accordingly, we first turn our attention to Title I of BCRA.

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Title I is Congress\xe2\x80\x99 effort to plug the soft-money loophole. The cornerstone of Title I is new FECA \xc2\xa7323(a), which prohibits national party committees and their agents from soliciting, receiving, directing, or spending any soft money. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(a) (Supp. 2003).[Footnote 38] In short, \xc2\xa7323(a) takes national parties out of the soft-money business.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The remaining provisions of new FECA \xc2\xa7323 largely reinforce the restrictions in \xc2\xa7323(a). New FECA \xc2\xa7323(b) prevents the wholesale shift of soft-money influence from national to state party committees by prohibiting state and local party committees from using such funds for activities that affect federal elections. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(b). These \xe2\x80\x9cFederal election activit[ies],\xe2\x80\x9d defined in new FECA \xc2\xa7301(20)(A), are almost identical to the mixed-purpose activities that have long been regulated under the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s pre-BCRA allocation regime. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7431(20)(A). New FECA \xc2\xa7323(d) reinforces these soft-money restrictions by prohibiting political parties from soliciting and donating funds to tax-exempt organizations that engage in electioneering activities. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(d). New FECA \xc2\xa7323(e) restricts federal candidates and officeholders from receiving, spending, or soliciting soft money in connection with federal elections and limits their ability to do so in connection with state and local elections. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(e). Finally, new FECA \xc2\xa7323(f) prevents circumvention of the restrictions on national, state, and local party committees by prohibiting state and local candidates from raising and spending soft money to fund advertisements and other public communications that promote or attack federal candidates. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(f).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs mount a facial First Amendment challenge to new FECA \xc2\xa7323, as well as challenges based on the Elections Clause, U. S. Const., Art. I, \xc2\xa74, principles of federalism, and the equal protection component of the Due Process Clause. We address these challenges in turn.

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In Buckley and subsequent cases, we have subjected restrictions on campaign expenditures to closer scrutiny than limits on campaign contributions. See, e.g., Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Beaumont, 539 U. S. ___, ___ (2003) (slip op., at 14); see also Nixon v. Shrink Missouri Government PAC, 528 U. S. 377, 387\xe2\x80\x93388 (2000); Buckley, 424 U. S., at 19. In these cases we have recognized that contribution limits, unlike limits on expenditures, \xe2\x80\x9centai[l] only a marginal restriction upon the contributor\xe2\x80\x99s ability to engage in free communication.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 20; see also, e.g., Beaumont, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 14); Shrink Missouri, supra, at 386\xe2\x80\x93388. In Buckley we said that:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cA contribution serves as a general expression of support for the candidate and his views, but does not communicate the underlying basis for the support. The quantity of communication by the contributor does not increase perceptibly with the size of the contribution, since the expression rests solely on the undifferentiated, symbolic act of contributing. At most, the size of the contribution provides a very rough index of the intensity of the contributor\xe2\x80\x99s support for the candidate. A limitation on the amount of money a person may give to a candidate or campaign organization thus involves little direct restraint on his political communication, for it permits the symbolic expression of support evidenced by a contribution but does not in any way infringe the contributor\xe2\x80\x99s freedom to discuss candidates and issues. While contributions may result in political expression if spent by a candidate or an association to present views to the voters, the transformation of contributions into political debate involves speech by someone other than the contributor.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 21 (footnote omitted).

\n

\nBecause the communicative value of large contributions inheres mainly in their ability to facilitate the speech of their recipients, we have said that contribution limits impose serious burdens on free speech only if they are so low as to \xe2\x80\x9cpreven[t] candidates and political committees from amassing the resources necessary for effective advocacy.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We have recognized that contribution limits may bear \xe2\x80\x9cmore heavily on the associational right than on freedom to speak,\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, supra, at 388, since contributions serve \xe2\x80\x9cto affiliate a person with a candidate\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cenabl[e] like-minded persons to pool their resources,\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 22. Unlike expenditure limits, however, which \xe2\x80\x9cpreclud[e] most associations from effectively amplifying the voice of their adherents,\xe2\x80\x9d contribution limits both \xe2\x80\x9cleave the contributor free to become a member of any political association and to assist personally in the association\xe2\x80\x99s efforts on behalf of candidates,\xe2\x80\x9d and allow associations \xe2\x80\x9cto aggregate large sums of money to promote effective advocacy.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. The \xe2\x80\x9coverall effect\xe2\x80\x9d of dollar limits on contributions is \xe2\x80\x9cmerely to require candidates and political committees to raise funds from a greater number of persons.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 21\xe2\x80\x9322. Thus, a contribution limit involving even \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98significant interference\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d with associational rights is nevertheless valid if it satisfies the \xe2\x80\x9clesser demand\xe2\x80\x9d of being \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98closely drawn\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to match a \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98sufficiently important interest.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Beaumont, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 15) (quoting Shrink Missouri, supra, at 387\xe2\x80\x93388).[Footnote 39]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Our treatment of contribution restrictions reflects more than the limited burdens they impose on First Amendment freedoms. It also reflects the importance of the interests that underlie contribution limits\xe2\x80\x94interests in preventing \xe2\x80\x9cboth the actual corruption threatened by large financial contributions and the eroding of public confidence in the electoral process through the appearance of corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d National Right to Work, 459 U. S., at 208; see also Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Colorado Republican Federal Campaign Comm., 533 U. S. 431, 440\xe2\x80\x93441 (2001) (Colorado II). We have said that these interests directly implicate \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the integrity of our electoral process, and, not less, the responsibility of the individual citizen for the successful functioning of that process.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d National Right to Work, supra, at 208 (quoting Automobile Workers, 352 U. S., at 570). Because the electoral process is the very \xe2\x80\x9cmeans through which a free society democratically translates political speech into concrete governmental action,\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 401 (Breyer, J., concurring), contribution limits, like other measures aimed at protecting the integrity of the process, tangibly benefit public participation in political debate. For that reason, when reviewing Congress\xe2\x80\x99 decision to enact contribution limits, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no place for a strong presumption against constitutionality, of the sort often thought to accompany the words \xe2\x80\x98strict scrutiny.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 400 (Breyer, J., concurring). The less rigorous standard of review we have applied to contribution limits (Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cclosely drawn\xe2\x80\x9d scrutiny) shows proper deference to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 ability to weigh competing constitutional interests in an area in which it enjoys particular expertise. It also provides Congress with sufficient room to anticipate and respond to concerns about circumvention of regulations designed to protect the integrity of the political process.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Our application of this less rigorous degree of scrutiny has given rise to significant criticism in the past from our dissenting colleagues. See, e.g., Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 405\xe2\x80\x93410 (Kennedy, J., dissenting); id., at 410\xe2\x80\x93420 (Thomas, J., dissenting); Colorado Republican Federal Campaign Comm. v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 518 U. S. 604, 635\xe2\x80\x93644 (1996) (Colorado I) (Thomas, J., dissenting). We have rejected such criticism in previous cases for the reasons identified above. We are also mindful of the fact that in its lengthy deliberations leading to the enactment of BCRA, Congress properly relied on the recognition of its authority contained in Buckley and its progeny. Considerations of stare decisis, buttressed by the respect that the Legislative and Judicial Branches owe to one another, provide additional powerful reasons for adhering to the analysis of contribution limits that the Court has consistently followed since Buckley was decided. See Hilton v. South Carolina Public Railways Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 502 U. S. 197, 202 (1991).[Footnote 40]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Like the contribution limits we upheld in Buckley, \xc2\xa7323\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions have only a marginal impact on the ability of contributors, candidates, officeholders, and parties to engage in effective political speech. Beaumont, 539 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 14). Complex as its provisions may be, \xc2\xa7323, in the main, does little more than regulate the ability of wealthy individuals, corporations, and unions to contribute large sums of money to influence federal elections, federal candidates, and federal officeholders.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs contend that we must apply strict scrutiny to \xc2\xa7323 because many of its provisions restrict not only contributions but also the spending and solicitation of funds raised outside of FECA\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limits. But for purposes of determining the level of scrutiny, it is irrelevant that Congress chose in \xc2\xa7323 to regulate contributions on the demand rather than the supply side. See, e.g., National Right to Work, supra, at 206\xe2\x80\x93211 (upholding a provision restricting PACs\xe2\x80\x99 ability to solicit funds). The relevant inquiry is whether the mechanism adopted to implement the contribution limit, or to prevent circumvention of that limit, burdens speech in a way that a direct restriction on the contribution itself would not. That is not the case here.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For example, while \xc2\xa7323(a) prohibits national parties from receiving or spending nonfederal money, and \xc2\xa7323(b) prohibits state party committees from spending nonfederal money on federal election activities, neither provision in any way limits the total amount of money parties can spend. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441i(a), (b) (Supp. 2003). Rather, they simply limit the source and individual amount of donations. That they do so by prohibiting the spending of soft money does not render them expenditure limitations.[Footnote 41]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Similarly, the solicitation provisions of \xc2\xa7323(a) and \xc2\xa7323(e), which restrict the ability of national party committees, federal candidates, and federal officeholders to solicit nonfederal funds, leave open ample opportunities for soliciting federal funds on behalf of entities subject to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s source and amount restrictions. Even \xc2\xa7323(d), which on its face enacts a blanket ban on party solicitations of funds to certain tax-exempt organizations, nevertheless allows parties to solicit funds to the organizations\xe2\x80\x99 federal PACs. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(d). As for those organizations that cannot or do not administer PACs, parties remain free to donate federal funds directly to such organizations, and may solicit funds expressly for that purpose. See infra, at 72\xe2\x80\x9373 (construing \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s restriction on donations by parties to apply only to donations from a party committee\xe2\x80\x99s nonfederal or soft-money account). And as with \xc2\xa7323(a), \xc2\xa7323(d) places no limits on other means of endorsing tax-exempt organizations or any restrictions on solicitations by party officers acting in their individual capacities. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441i(a), (d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323 thus shows \xe2\x80\x9cdue regard for the reality that solicitation is characteristically intertwined with informative and perhaps persuasive speech seeking support for particular causes or for particular views.\xe2\x80\x9d Schaumburg v. Citizens for a Better Environment, 444 U. S. 620, 632 (1980). The fact that party committees and federal candidates and officeholders must now ask only for limited dollar amounts or request that a corporation or union contribute money through its PAC in no way alters or impairs the political message \xe2\x80\x9cintertwined\xe2\x80\x9d with the solicitation. Cf. Riley v. National Federation of Blind of N. C., Inc., 487 U. S. 781, 795 (1988) (treating solicitation restriction that required fundraisers to disclose particular information as a content-based regulation subject to strict scrutiny because it \xe2\x80\x9cnecessarily alter[ed] the content of the speech\xe2\x80\x9d). And rather than chill such solicitations, as was the case in Schaumburg, the restriction here tends to increase the dissemination of information by forcing parties, candidates, and officeholders to solicit from a wider array of potential donors. As with direct limits on contributions, therefore, \xc2\xa7323\xe2\x80\x99s spending and solicitation restrictions have only a marginal impact on political speech.[Footnote 42]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, plaintiffs contend that the type of associational burdens that \xc2\xa7323 imposes are fundamentally different from the burdens that accompanied Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limits, and merit the type of strict scrutiny we have applied to attempts to regulate the internal processes of political parties. E.g., California Democratic Party v. Jones, 530 U. S. 567, 573\xe2\x80\x93574 (2000). In making this argument, plaintiffs greatly exaggerate the effect of \xc2\xa7323, contending that it precludes any collaboration among national, state, and local committees of the same party in fundraising and electioneering activities. We do not read the provisions in that way. See infra, at 51\xe2\x80\x9352. Section 323 merely subjects a greater percentage of contributions to parties and candidates to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s source and amount limitations. Buckley has already acknowledged that such limitations \xe2\x80\x9cleave the contributor free to become a member of any political association and to assist personally in the association\xe2\x80\x99s efforts on behalf of candidates.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 22. The modest impact that \xc2\xa7323 has on the ability of committees within a party to associate with each other does not independently occasion strict scrutiny. None of this is to suggest that the alleged associational burdens imposed on parties by \xc2\xa7323 have no place in the First Amendment analysis; it is only that we account for them in the application, rather than the choice, of the appropriate level of scrutiny.[Footnote 43]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0With these principles in mind, we apply the less rigorous scrutiny applicable to contribution limits to evaluate the constitutionality of new FECA \xc2\xa7323. Because the five challenged provisions of \xc2\xa7323 implicate different First Amendment concerns, we discuss them separately. We are mindful, however, that Congress enacted \xc2\xa7323 as an integrated whole to vindicate the Government\xe2\x80\x99s important interest in preventing corruption and the appearance of corruption.

\n

New FECA \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s Restrictions on National Party Committees

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The core of Title I is new FECA \xc2\xa7323(a), which provides that \xe2\x80\x9cnational committee[s] of a political party \xe2\x80\xa6 may not solicit, receive, or direct to another person a contribution, donation, or transfer of funds or any other thing of value, or spend any funds, that are not subject to the limitations, prohibitions, and reporting requirements of this Act.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(a)(1) (Supp. 2003). The prohibition extends to \xe2\x80\x9cany officer or agent acting on behalf of such a national committee, and any entity that is directly or indirectly established, financed, or maintained, or controlled by such a national committee.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7441(a)(2).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The main goal of \xc2\xa7323(a) is modest. In large part, it simply effects a return to the scheme that was approved in Buckley and that was subverted by the creation of the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s allocation regime, which permitted the political parties to fund federal electioneering efforts with a combination of hard and soft money. See supra, at 11\xe2\x80\x9313, and n.\xc2\xa07. Under that allocation regime, national parties were able to use vast amounts of soft money in their efforts to elect federal candidates. Consequently, as long as they directed the money to the political parties, donors could contribute large amounts of soft money for use in activities designed to influence federal elections.[Footnote 44] New \xc2\xa7323(a) is designed to put a stop to that practice.

\n

1.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Governmental Interests Underlying New FECA\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa7323(a)

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government defends \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s ban on national parties\xe2\x80\x99 involvement with soft money as necessary to prevent the actual and apparent corruption of federal candidates and officeholders. Our cases have made clear that the prevention of corruption or its appearance constitutes a sufficiently important interest to justify political contribution limits. We have not limited that interest to the elimination of cash-for-votes exchanges. In Buckley, we expressly rejected the argument that antibribery laws provided a less restrictive alternative to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limits, noting that such laws \xe2\x80\x9cdeal[t] with only the most blatant and specific attempts of those with money to influence government action.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 28. Thus, \xe2\x80\x9c[i]n speaking of \xe2\x80\x98improper influence\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98opportunities for abuse\xe2\x80\x99 in addition to \xe2\x80\x98quid pro quo arrangements,\xe2\x80\x99 we [have] recognized a concern not confined to bribery of public officials, but extending to the broader threat from politicians too compliant with the wishes of large contributors.\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 389; see also Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 441 (acknowledging that corruption extends beyond explicit cash-for-votes agreements to \xe2\x80\x9cundue influence on an officeholder\xe2\x80\x99s judgment\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Of \xe2\x80\x9calmost equal\xe2\x80\x9d importance has been the Government\xe2\x80\x99s interest in combating the appearance or perception of corruption engendered by large campaign contributions. Buckley, supra, at 27; see also Shrink Missouri, supra, at 390; Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. National Conservative Political Action Comm., 470 U. S. 480, 496\xe2\x80\x93497 (1985). Take away Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to regulate the appearance of undue influence and \xe2\x80\x9cthe cynical assumption that large donors call the tune could jeopardize the willingness of voters to take part in democratic governance.\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 390; see also id., at 401 (Breyer, J., concurring). And because the First Amendment does not require Congress to ignore the fact that \xe2\x80\x9ccandidates, donors, and parties test the limits of the current law,\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 457, these interests have been sufficient to justify not only contribution limits themselves, but laws preventing the circumvention of such limits, id., at 456 (\xe2\x80\x9c[A]ll Members of the Court agree that circumvention is a valid theory of corruption\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe quantum of empirical evidence needed to satisfy heightened judicial scrutiny of legislative judgments will vary up or down with the novelty or the plausibility of the justification raised.\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, supra, at 391. The idea that large contributions to a national party can corrupt or, at the very least, create the appearance of corruption of federal candidates and officeholders is neither novel nor implausible. For nearly 30 years, FECA has placed strict dollar limits and source restrictions on contributions that individuals and other entities can give to national, state, and local party committees for the purpose of influencing a federal election. The premise behind these restrictions has been, and continues to be, that contributions to a federal candidate\xe2\x80\x99s party in aid of that candidate\xe2\x80\x99s campaign threaten to create\xe2\x80\x94no less than would a direct contribution to the candidate\xe2\x80\x94a sense of obligation. See Buckley, supra, at 38 (upholding FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $25,000 limit on aggregate yearly contributions to a candidate, political committee, and political party committee as a \xe2\x80\x9cquite modest restraint \xe2\x80\xa6 to prevent evasion of the $1,000 contribution limitation\xe2\x80\x9d by, among other things, \xe2\x80\x9chuge contributions to the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s political party\xe2\x80\x9d). This is particularly true of contributions to national parties, with which federal candidates and officeholders enjoy a special relationship and unity of interest. This close affiliation has placed national parties in a unique position, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether they like it or not,\xe2\x80\x9d to serve as \xe2\x80\x9cagents for spending on behalf of those who seek to produce obligated officeholders.\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado II, supra, at 452; see also Shrink Missouri, supra, at 406 (Kennedy, J., dissenting) (\xe2\x80\x9c[Respondent] asks us to evaluate his speech claim in the context of a system which favors candidates and officeholders whose campaigns are supported by soft money, usually funneled through political parties\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)). As discussed below, rather than resist that role, the national parties have actively embraced it.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The question for present purposes is whether large soft-money contributions to national party committees have a corrupting influence or give rise to the appearance of corruption. Both common sense and the ample record in these cases confirm Congress\xe2\x80\x99 belief that they do. As set forth above, supra, at 11\xe2\x80\x9313, and n.\xc2\xa07, the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s allocation regime has invited widespread circumvention of FECA\xe2\x80\x99s limits on contributions to parties for the purpose of influencing federal elections. Under this system, corporate, union, and wealthy individual donors have been free to contribute substantial sums of soft money to the national parties, which the parties can spend for the specific purpose of influencing a particular candidate\xe2\x80\x99s federal election. It is not only plausible, but likely, that candidates would feel grateful for such donations and that donors would seek to exploit that gratitude.[Footnote 45]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The evidence in the record shows that candidates and donors alike have in fact exploited the soft-money loophole, the former to increase their prospects of election and the latter to create debt on the part of officeholders, with the national parties serving as willing intermediaries. Thus, despite FECA\xe2\x80\x99s hard-money limits on direct contributions to candidates, federal officeholders have commonly asked donors to make soft-money donations to national and state committees \xe2\x80\x9csolely in order to assist federal campaigns,\xe2\x80\x9d including the officeholder\xe2\x80\x99s own. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 472 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting declaration of Wade Randlett, CEO, Dashboard Technology \xc2\xb6 ;\xc2\xb6 ;6\xe2\x80\x939 (hereinafter Randlett Decl.), App. 713\xe2\x80\x93714); see also 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 471\xe2\x80\x93473, 478\xe2\x80\x93479 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 842\xe2\x80\x93843 (Leon, J.). Parties kept tallies of the amounts of soft money raised by each officeholder, and \xe2\x80\x9cthe amount of money a Member of Congress raise[d] for the national political committees often affect[ed] the amount the committees g[a]ve to assist the Member\xe2\x80\x99s campaign.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 474\xe2\x80\x93475 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.). Donors often asked that their contributions be credited to particular candidates, and the parties obliged, irrespective of whether the funds were hard or soft.
