Id., at 477\xe2\x80\x93478 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 824, 847 (Leon, J.). National party committees often teamed with individual candidates\xe2\x80\x99 campaign committees to create joint fundraising committees, which enabled the candidates to take advantage of the party\xe2\x80\x99s higher contribution limits while still allowing donors to give to their preferred candidate. Id., at 478 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 847\xe2\x80\x93848 (Leon, J.); see also App. 1286 (Krasno & Sorauf Expert Report (characterizing the joint fundraising committee as one \xe2\x80\x9cin which Senate candidates in effect rais[e] soft money for use in their own races\xe2\x80\x9d)). Even when not participating directly in the fundraising, federal officeholders were well aware of the identities of the donors: National party committees would distribute lists of potential or actual donors, or donors themselves would report their generosity to officeholders. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 487\xe2\x80\x93488 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (\xe2\x80\x9c[F]or a Member not to know the identities of these donors, he or she must actively avoid such knowledge, as it is provided by the national political parties and the donors themselves\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 853\xe2\x80\x93855 (Leon, J.).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For their part, lobbyists, CEOs, and wealthy individuals alike all have candidly admitted donating substantial sums of soft money to national committees not on ideological grounds, but for the express purpose of securing influence over federal officials. For example, a former lobbyist and partner at a lobbying firm in Washington, D.\xc2\xa0C., stated in his declaration:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98You are doing a favor for somebody by making a large [soft-money] donation and they appreciate it. Ordinarily, people feel inclined to reciprocate favors. Do a bigger favor for someone\xe2\x80\x94that is, write a larger check\xe2\x80\x94and they feel even more compelled to reciprocate. In my experience, overt words are rarely exchanged about contributions, but people do have understandings.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 493 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting declaration of Robert Rozen, partner, Ernst & Young \xc2\xb6 ;14; see 8\xe2\x80\x93R Defs. Exhs., Tab 33).[Footnote 46]

\n

\nParticularly telling is the fact that, in 1996 and 2000, more than half of the top 50 soft-money donors gave substantial sums to both major national parties, leaving room for no other conclusion but that these donors were seeking influence, or avoiding retaliation, rather than promoting any particular ideology. See, e.g., 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 508\xe2\x80\x93510 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (citing Mann Expert Report Tbls. 5\xe2\x80\x936); 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 509 (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98Giving soft money to both parties, the Republicans and the Democrats, makes no sense at all unless the donor feels that he or she is buying access.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting declaration of former Sen. Dale Bumpers \xc2\xb6 ;15, App. 175)).[Footnote 47]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The evidence from the federal officeholders\xe2\x80\x99 perspective is similar. For example, one former Senator described the influence purchased by nonfederal donations as follows:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98Too often, Members\xe2\x80\x99 first thought is not what is right or what they believe, but how it will affect fundraising. Who, after all, can seriously contend that a $100,000 donation does not alter the way one thinks about\xe2\x80\x94and quite possibly votes on\xe2\x80\x94an issue?\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\xa6 When you don\xe2\x80\x99t pay the piper that finances your campaigns, you will never get any more money from that piper. Since money is the mother\xe2\x80\x99s milk of politics, you never want to be in that situation.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 481 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting declaration of former Sen. Alan Simpson \xc2\xb6 ;10 (hereinafter Simpson Decl.), App. 811); 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 851 (Leon, J.) (same).

\n

\nSee also id., at 489 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98The majority of those who contribute to political parties do so for business reasons, to gain access to influential Members of Congress and to get to know new Members.\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Hickmott Decl., Exh. A, \xc2\xb6 ;46)). By bringing soft-money donors and federal candidates and officeholders together, \xe2\x80\x9c[p]arties are thus necessarily the instruments of some contributors whose object is not to support the party\xe2\x80\x99s message or to elect party candidates across the board, but rather to support a specific candidate for the sake of a position on one narrow issue, or even to support any candidate who will be obliged to the contributors.\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 451\xe2\x80\x93452.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs argue that without concrete evidence of an instance in which a federal officeholder has actually switched a vote (or, presumably, evidence of a specific instance where the public believes a vote was switched), Congress has not shown that there exists real or apparent corruption. But the record is to the contrary. The evidence connects soft money to manipulations of the legislative calendar, leading to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 failure to enact, among other things, generic drug legislation, tort reform, and tobacco legislation. See, e.g., 251 F. Supp. 2d, at 482 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 852 (Leon, J.); App. 390\xe2\x80\x93394 (declaration of Sen. John McCain \xc2\xb6 ;\xc2\xb6 ;5, 8\xe2\x80\x9311 (hereinafter McCain Decl.)); App. 811 (Simpson Decl. \xc2\xb6 ;10) (\xe2\x80\x9cDonations from the tobacco industry to Republicans scuttled tobacco legislation, just as contributions from the trial lawyers to Democrats stopped tort reform\xe2\x80\x9d); App. 805 (declaration of former Sen. Paul Simon \xc2\xb6 ;\xc2\xb6 ;13\xe2\x80\x9314). To claim that such actions do not change legislative outcomes surely misunderstands the legislative process.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0More importantly, plaintiffs conceive of corruption too narrowly. Our cases have firmly established that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 legitimate interest extends beyond preventing simple cash-for-votes corruption to curbing \xe2\x80\x9cundue influence on an officeholder\xe2\x80\x99s judgment, and the appearance of such influence.\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado II, supra, at 441. Many of the \xe2\x80\x9cdeeply disturbing examples\xe2\x80\x9d of corruption cited by this Court in Buckley, 424 U. S., at 27, to justify FECA\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limits were not episodes of vote buying, but evidence that various corporate interests had given substantial donations to gain access to high-level government officials. See Buckley, 519 F.\xc2\xa02d, at 821, 839\xe2\x80\x93840, n.\xc2\xa036; nn. 5\xe2\x80\x936, supra. Even if that access did not secure actual influence, it certainly gave the \xe2\x80\x9cappearance of such influence.\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado II, supra, at 441; see also 519 F. 2d, at 838.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The record in the present case is replete with similar examples of national party committees peddling access to federal candidates and officeholders in exchange for large soft-money donations. See 251 F. Supp. 2d, at 492\xe2\x80\x93506 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.). As one former Senator put it:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98Special interests who give large amounts of soft money to political parties do in fact achieve their objectives. They do get special access. Sitting Senators and House Members have limited amounts of time, but they make time available in their schedules to meet with representatives of business and unions and wealthy individuals who gave large sums to their parties. These are not idle chit-chats about the philosophy of democracy\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Senators are pressed by their benefactors to introduce legislation, to amend legislation, to block legislation, and to vote on legislation in a certain way.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 496 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting declaration of former Sen. Warren Rudman \xc2\xb6 ;7 (hereinafter Rudman Decl.), App. 742); 251 F. Supp. 2d, at 858 (Leon, J.) (same).

\n

\nSo pervasive is this practice that the six national party committees actually furnish their own menus of opportunities for access to would-be soft-money donors, with increased prices reflecting an increased level of access. For example, the DCCC offers a range of donor options, starting with the $10,000-per-year Business Forum program, and going up to the $100,000-per-year National Finance Board program. The latter entitles the donor to bimonthly conference calls with the Democratic House leadership and chair of the DCCC, complimentary invitations to all DCCC fundraising events, two private dinners with the Democratic House leadership and ranking members, and two retreats with the Democratic House leader and DCCC chair in Telluride, Colorado, and Hyannisport, Massachusetts. Id., at 504\xe2\x80\x93505 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); see also id., at 506 (describing records indicating that DNC offered meetings with President in return for large donations); id., at 502\xe2\x80\x93503 (describing RNC\xe2\x80\x99s various donor programs); id., at 503\xe2\x80\x93504 (same for NRSC); id., at 500\xe2\x80\x93503 (same for DSCC); id., at 504 (same for NRCC). Similarly, \xe2\x80\x9cthe RNC\xe2\x80\x99s donor programs offer greater access to federal office holders as the donations grow larger, with the highest level and most personal access offered to the largest soft money donors.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 500\xe2\x80\x93503 (finding, further, that the RNC holds out the prospect of access to officeholders to attract soft-money donations and encourages officeholders to meet with large soft-money donors); accord, id., at 860\xe2\x80\x93861 (Leon, J.).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Despite this evidence and the close ties that candidates and officeholders have with their parties, Justice Kennedy would limit Congress\xe2\x80\x99 regulatory interest only to the prevention of the actual or apparent quid pro quo corruption \xe2\x80\x9cinherent in\xe2\x80\x9d contributions made directly to, contributions made at the express behest of, and expenditures made in coordination with, a federal officeholder or candidate. Post, at 8\xe2\x80\x9310, 15. Regulation of any other donation or expenditure\xe2\x80\x94regardless of its size, the recipient\xe2\x80\x99s relationship to the candidate or officeholder, its potential impact on a candidate\xe2\x80\x99s election, its value to the candidate, or its unabashed and explicit intent to purchase influence\xe2\x80\x94would, according to Justice Kennedy, simply be out of bounds. This crabbed view of corruption, and particularly of the appearance of corruption, ignores precedent, common sense, and the realities of political fundraising exposed by the record in this litigation.[Footnote 48]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s interpretation of the First Amendment would render Congress powerless to address more subtle but equally dispiriting forms of corruption. Just as troubling to a functioning democracy as classic quid pro quo corruption is the danger that officeholders will decide issues not on the merits or the desires of their constituencies, but according to the wishes of those who have made large financial contributions valued by the officeholder. Even if it occurs only occasionally, the potential for such undue influence is manifest. And unlike straight cash-for-votes transactions, such corruption is neither easily detected nor practical to criminalize. The best means of prevention is to identify and to remove the temptation. The evidence set forth above, which is but a sampling of the reams of disquieting evidence contained in the record, convincingly demonstrates that soft-money contributions to political parties carry with them just such temptation.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy likewise takes too narrow a view of the appearance of corruption. He asserts that only those transactions with \xe2\x80\x9cinherent corruption potential,\xe2\x80\x9d which he again limits to contributions directly to candidates, justify the inference \xe2\x80\x9cthat regulating the conduct will stem the appearance of real corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 14.[Footnote 49] In our view, however, Congress is not required to ignore historical evidence regarding a particular practice or to view conduct in isolation from its context. To be sure, mere political favoritism or opportunity for influence alone is insufficient to justify regulation. Post, at 12\xe2\x80\x9314. As the record demonstrates, it is the manner in which parties have sold access to federal candidates and officeholders that has given rise to the appearance of undue influence. Implicit (and, as the record shows, sometimes explicit) in the sale of access is the suggestion that money buys influence. It is no surprise then that purchasers of such access unabashedly admit that they are seeking to purchase just such influence. It was not unwarranted for Congress to conclude that the selling of access gives rise to the appearance of corruption.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, there is substantial evidence to support Congress\xe2\x80\x99 determination that large soft-money contributions to national political parties give rise to corruption and the appearance of corruption.

\n

2.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s Restriction on Spending and \n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Receiving Soft Money

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs and The Chief Justice contend that \xc2\xa7323(a) is impermissibly overbroad because it subjects all funds raised and spent by national parties to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s hard-money source and amount limits, including, for example, funds spent on purely state and local elections in which no federal office is at stake.[Footnote 50] Post, 2\xe2\x80\x935 (Rehnquist, C.\xc2\xa0J., dissenting). Such activities, The Chief Justice asserts, pose \xe2\x80\x9clittle or no potential to corrupt \xe2\x80\xa6 federal candidates or officeholders.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 5 (dissenting opinion). This observation is beside the point. Section 323(a), like the remainder of \xc2\xa7323, regulates contributions, not activities. As the record demonstrates, it is the close relationship between federal officeholders and the national parties, as well as the means by which parties have traded on that relationship, that have made all large soft-money contributions to national parties suspect.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As one expert noted, \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[t]here is no meaningful distinction between the national party committees and the public officials who control them.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 468\xe2\x80\x93469 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (quoting Mann Expert Report 29). The national committees of the two major parties are both run by, and largely composed of, federal officeholders and candidates. Indeed, of the six national committees of the two major parties, four are composed entirely of federal officeholders. Ibid. The nexus between national parties and federal officeholders prompted one of Title I\xe2\x80\x99s framers to conclude:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cBecause the national parties operate at the national level, and are inextricably intertwined with federal officeholders and candidates, who raise the money for the national party committees, there is a close connection between the funding of the national parties and the corrupting dangers of soft money on the federal political process. The only effective way to address this [soft-money] problem of corruption is to ban entirely all raising and spending of soft money by the national parties.\xe2\x80\x9d 148 Cong. Rec. H409 (Feb. 13, 2002) (statement of Rep. Shays).

\n

\nGiven this close connection and alignment of interests, large soft-money contributions to national parties are likely to create actual or apparent indebtedness on the part of federal officeholders, regardless of how those funds are ultimately used.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This close affiliation has also placed national parties in a position to sell access to federal officeholders in exchange for soft-money contributions that the party can then use for its own purposes. Access to federal officeholders is the most valuable favor the national party committees are able to give in exchange for large donations. The fact that officeholders comply by donating their valuable time indicates either that officeholders place substantial value on the soft-money contribution themselves, without regard to their end use, or that national committees are able to exert considerable control over federal officeholders. See, e.g., App. 1196\xe2\x80\x931198 (Expert Report of Donald P. Green, Yale University) (\xe2\x80\x9cOnce elected to legislative office, public officials enter an environment in which political parties-in-government control the resources crucial to subsequent electoral success and legislative power. Political parties organize the legislative caucuses that make committee assignments\xe2\x80\x9d); App. 1298 (Krasno & Sorauf Expert Report) (indicating that officeholders\xe2\x80\x99 re-election prospects are significantly influenced by attitudes of party leadership). Either way, large soft-money donations to national party committees are likely to buy donors preferential access to federal officeholders no matter the ends to which their contributions are eventually put. As discussed above, Congress had sufficient grounds to regulate the appearance of undue influence associated with this practice. The Government\xe2\x80\x99s strong interests in preventing corruption, and in particular the appearance of corruption, are thus sufficient to justify subjecting all donations to national parties to the source, amount, and disclosure limitations of FECA.[Footnote 51]

\n

3.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s Restriction on Soliciting or \n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Directing Soft Money

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs also contend that \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s prohibition on national parties\xe2\x80\x99 soliciting or directing soft-money contributions is substantially overbroad. The reach of the solicitation prohibition, however, is limited. It bars only solicitations of soft money by national party committees and by party officers in their official capacities. The committees remain free to solicit hard money on their own behalf, as well as to solicit hard money on behalf of state committees and state and local candidates.[Footnote 52] They also can contribute hard money to state committees and to candidates. In accordance with FEC regulations, furthermore, officers of national parties are free to solicit soft money in their individual capacities, or, if they are also officials of state parties, in that capacity. See 67 Fed. Reg. 49083 (2002).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This limited restriction on solicitation follows sensibly from the prohibition on national committees\xe2\x80\x99 receiving soft money. The same observations that led us to approve the latter compel us to reach the same conclusion regarding the former. A national committee is likely to respond favorably to a donation made at its request regardless of whether the recipient is the committee itself or another entity. This principle accords with common sense and appears elsewhere in federal laws. E.g., 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7201(b)(2) (prohibition on public officials \xe2\x80\x9cdemand[ing] [or] seek[ing] \xe2\x80\xa6 anything of value personally or for any other person or entity \xe2\x80\xa6\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)); 5 CFR \xc2\xa72635.203(f)(2) (2003) (restriction on gifts to federal employees encompasses gifts \xe2\x80\x9c[g]iven to any other person, including any charitable organization, on the basis of designation, recommendation, or other specification by the employee\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs argue that BCRA itself demonstrates the overbreadth of \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s solicitation ban. They point in particular to \xc2\xa7323(e), which allows federal candidates and officeholders to solicit limited amounts of soft money from individual donors under certain circumstances. Compare 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A \xc2\xa7441i(a) with \xc2\xa7441i(e) (Supp. 2003). The differences between \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(a) and 323(e), however, are without constitutional significance. We have recognized that \xe2\x80\x9cthe \xe2\x80\x98differing structures and purposes\xe2\x80\x99 of different entities \xe2\x80\x98may require different forms of regulation in order to protect the integrity of the electoral process,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d National Right to Work, 459 U. S., at 210, and we respect Congress\xe2\x80\x99 decision to proceed in incremental steps in the area of campaign finance regulation, see Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Massachusetts Citizens for Life, Inc., 479 U. S. 238, 258, n.\xc2\xa011 (1986) (MCFL); Buckley, 424 U. S., at 105. The differences between the two provisions reflect Congress\xe2\x80\x99 reasonable judgments about the function played by national committees and the interactions between committees and officeholders, subjects about which Members of Congress have vastly superior knowledge.

\n

4.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s Application to Minor Parties

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The McConnell and political party plaintiffs contend that \xc2\xa7323(a) is substantially overbroad and must be stricken on its face because it impermissibly infringes the speech and associational rights of minor parties such as the Libertarian National Committee, which, owing to their slim prospects for electoral success and the fact that they receive few large soft-money contributions from corporate sources, pose no threat of corruption comparable to that posed by the RNC and DNC. In Buckley, we rejected a similar argument concerning limits on contributions to minor-party candidates, noting that \xe2\x80\x9cany attempt to exclude minor parties and independents en masse from the Act\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limitations overlooks the fact that minor-party candidates may win elective office or have a substantial impact on the outcome of an election.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 34\xe2\x80\x9335. We have thus recognized that the relevance of the interest in avoiding actual or apparent corruption is not a function of the number of legislators a given party manages to elect. It applies as much to a minor party that manages to elect only one of its members to federal office as it does to a major party whose members make up a majority of Congress. It is therefore reasonable to require that all parties and all candidates follow the same set of rules designed to protect the integrity of the electoral process.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We add that nothing in \xc2\xa7323(a) prevents individuals from pooling resources to start a new national party. Post, at 5 (Kennedy, J., dissenting). Only when an organization has gained official status, which carries with it significant benefits for its members, will the proscriptions of \xc2\xa7323(a) apply. Even then, a nascent or struggling minor party can bring an as-applied challenge if \xc2\xa7323(a) prevents it from \xe2\x80\x9camassing the resources necessary for effective advocacy.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, supra, at 21.

\n

5.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s Associational Burdens

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, plaintiffs assert that \xc2\xa7323(a) is unconstitutional because it impermissibly interferes with the ability of national committees to associate with state and local committees. By way of example, plaintiffs point to the Republican Victory Plans, whereby the RNC acts in concert with the state and local committees of a given State to plan and implement joint, full-ticket fundraising and electioneering programs. See App. 693, 694\xe2\x80\x93697 (declaration of John Peschong, RNC Western Reg. Political Dir. (describing the Republican Victory Plans)). The political parties assert that \xc2\xa7323(a) outlaws any participation in Victory Plans by RNC officers, including merely sitting down at a table and engaging in collective decisionmaking about how soft money will be solicited, received, and spent. Such associational burdens, they argue, are too great for the First Amendment to bear.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We are not persuaded by this argument because it hinges on an unnaturally broad reading of the terms \xe2\x80\x9cspend,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9creceive,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cdirect,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9csolicit.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(a) (Supp. 2003). Nothing on the face of \xc2\xa7323(a) prohibits national party officers, whether acting in their official or individual capacities, from sitting down with state and local party committees or candidates to plan and advise how to raise and spend soft money. As long as the national party officer does not personally spend, receive, direct, or solicit soft money, \xc2\xa7323(a) permits a wide range of joint planning and electioneering activity. Intervenor-defendants, the principal drafters and proponents of the legislation, concede as much. Brief for Intervenor-Defendants Sen. John McCain et\xc2\xa0al. in No. 02\xe2\x80\x93\n1674 et\xc2\xa0al., p.\xc2\xa022 (\xe2\x80\x9cBCRA leaves parties and candidates free to coordinate campaign plans and activities, political messages, and fundraising goals with one another\xe2\x80\x9d). The FEC\xe2\x80\x99s current definitions of \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s terms are consistent with that view. See, e.g., 11 CFR \xc2\xa7300.2(m) (2002) (defining \xe2\x80\x9csolicit\xe2\x80\x9d as \xe2\x80\x9cto ask \xe2\x80\xa6 another person\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)); \xc2\xa7300.2(n) (defining \xe2\x80\x9cdirect\xe2\x80\x9d as \xe2\x80\x9cto ask a person who has expressed an intent to make a contribution . . . to make that contribution \xe2\x80\xa6 including through a conduit or intermediary\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)); \xc2\xa7300.2(c) (laying out the factors that determine whether an entity will be considered to be controlled by a national committee).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Given the straightforward meaning of this provision, Justice Kennedy is incorrect that \xe2\x80\x9c[a] national party\xe2\x80\x99s mere involvement in the strategic planning of fundraising for a state ballot initiative\xe2\x80\x9d or its assistance in developing a state party\xe2\x80\x99s Levin-money fundraising efforts risks a finding that the officers are in \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98indirect control\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d of the state party and subject to criminal penalties. Post, at 5\xe2\x80\x936. Moreover, \xc2\xa7323(a) leaves national party committee officers entirely free to participate, in their official capacities, with state and local parties and candidates in soliciting and spending hard money; party officials may also solicit soft money in their unofficial capacities.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Accordingly, we reject the plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 First Amendment challenge to new FECA \xc2\xa7323(a).

\n

New FECA \xc2\xa7323(b)\xe2\x80\x99s Restrictions on State and Local Party Committees

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In constructing a coherent scheme of campaign finance regulation, Congress recognized that, given the close ties between federal candidates and state party committees, BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on national committee activity would rapidly become ineffective if state and local committees remained available as a conduit for soft-money donations.[Footnote 53] Section 323(b) is designed to foreclose wholesale evasion of \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s anticorruption measures by sharply curbing state committees\xe2\x80\x99 ability to use large soft-money contributions to influence federal elections. The core of \xc2\xa7323(b) is a straightforward contribution regulation: It prevents donors from contributing nonfederal funds to state and local party committees to help finance \xe2\x80\x9cFederal election activity.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(b)(1) (Supp. 2003). The term \xe2\x80\x9cFederal election activity\xe2\x80\x9d encompasses four distinct categories of electioneering: (1) voter registration activity during the 120 days preceding a regularly scheduled federal election; (2) voter identification, get-out-the-vote (GOTV), and generic campaign activity[Footnote 54] that is \xe2\x80\x9cconducted in connection with an election in which a candidate for Federal office appears on the ballot\xe2\x80\x9d; (3) any \xe2\x80\x9cpublic communication\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 55] that \xe2\x80\x9crefers to a clearly identified candidate for Federal office\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cpromotes,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9csupports,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cattacks,\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9copposes\xe2\x80\x9d a candidate for that office; and (4) the services provided by a state committee employee who dedicates more than 25% of his or her time to \xe2\x80\x9cactivities in connection with a Federal election.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7431(20)(A)(i)\xe2\x80\x93(iv). The Act explicitly excludes several categories of activity from this definition: public communications that refer solely to nonfederal candidates;[Footnote 56] contributions to nonfederal candidates;[Footnote 57] state and local political conventions; and the cost of grassroots campaign materials like bumper stickers that refer only to state candidates. \xc2\xa7431(20)(B). All activities that fall within the statutory definition must be funded with hard money. \xc2\xa7441i(b)(1).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(b)(2), the so-called Levin Amendment, carves out an exception to this general rule. A refinement on the pre-BCRA regime that permitted parties to pay for certain activities with a mix of federal and nonfederal funds, the Levin Amendment allows state and local party committees to pay for certain types of federal election activity with an allocated ratio of hard money and \xe2\x80\x9cLevin funds\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94that is, funds raised within an annual limit of $10,000 per person. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(b)(2). Except for the $10,000 cap and certain related restrictions to prevent circumvention of that limit, \xc2\xa7323(b)(2) leaves regulation of such contributions to the States.[Footnote 58]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The scope of the Levin Amendment is limited in two ways. First, state and local parties can use Levin money to fund only activities that fall within categories (1) and (2) of the statute\xe2\x80\x99s definition of federal election activity\xe2\x80\x94namely, voter registration activity, voter identification drives, GOTV drives, and generic campaign activities. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(b)(2)(A). And not all of these activities qualify: Levin funds cannot be used to pay for any activities that refer to \xe2\x80\x9ca clearly identified candidate for Federal office\xe2\x80\x9d; they likewise cannot be used to fund broadcast communications unless they refer \xe2\x80\x9csolely to a clearly identified candidate for State or local office.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441i(b)(2)(B)(i)\xe2\x80\x93(ii).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, both the Levin funds and the allocated portion of hard money used to pay for such activities must be raised entirely by the state or local committee that spends them. \xc2\xa7441i(b)(2)(B)(iv). This means that a state party committee cannot use Levin funds transferred from other party committees to cover the Levin funds portion of a Levin Amendment expenditure. It also means that a state party committee cannot use hard money transferred from other party committees to cover the hard-money portion of a Levin Amendment expenditure. Furthermore, national committees, federal candidates, and federal officeholders generally may not solicit Levin funds on behalf of\nstate committees, and state committees may not team up to raise Levin funds. \xc2\xa7441i(b)(2)(C). They can, however, jointly raise the hard money used to make Levin expenditures.

\n

1.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Governmental Interests Underlying New FECA\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa7323(b)

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We begin by noting that, in addressing the problem of soft-money contributions to state committees, Congress both drew a conclusion and made a prediction. Its conclusion, based on the evidence before it, was that the corrupting influence of soft money does not insinuate itself into the political process solely through national party committees. Rather, state committees function as an alternate avenue for precisely the same corrupting forces.[Footnote 59] Indeed, both candidates and parties already ask donors who have reached the limit on their direct contributions to donate to state committees.[Footnote 60] There is at least as much evidence as there was in Buckley that such donations have been made with the intent\xe2\x80\x94and in at least some cases the effect\xe2\x80\x94of gaining influence over federal officeholders.[Footnote 61] Section 323(b) thus promotes an important governmental interest by confronting the corrupting influence that soft-money donations to political parties already have.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Congress also made a prediction. Having been taught the hard lesson of circumvention by the entire history of campaign finance regulation, Congress knew that soft-money donors would react to \xc2\xa7323(a) by scrambling to find another way to purchase influence. It was \xe2\x80\x9cneither novel nor implausible,\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 391, for Congress to conclude that political parties would react to \xc2\xa7323(a) by directing soft-money contributors to the state committees, and that federal candidates would be just as indebted to these contributors as they had been to those who had formerly contributed to the national parties. We \xe2\x80\x9cmust accord substantial deference to the predictive judgments of Congress,\xe2\x80\x9d Turner Broadcasting System, Inc. v. FCC, 512 U. S. 622, 665 (1994), particularly when, as here, those predictions are so firmly rooted in relevant history and common sense. Preventing corrupting activity from shifting wholesale to state committees and thereby eviscerating FECA clearly qualifies as an important governmental interest.

\n

2.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(b)\xe2\x80\x99s Tailoring

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs argue that even if some legitimate interest might be served by \xc2\xa7323(b), the provision\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions are unjustifiably burdensome and therefore cannot be considered \xe2\x80\x9cclosely drawn\xe2\x80\x9d to match the Government\xe2\x80\x99s objectives. They advance three main contentions in support of this proposition. First, they argue that the provision is substantially overbroad because it federalizes activities that pose no conceivable risk of corrupting or appearing to corrupt federal officeholders. Second, they argue that the Levin Amendment imposes an unconstitutional burden on the associational rights of political parties. Finally, they argue that the provision prevents them from amassing the resources they need to engage in effective advocacy. We address these points in turn.

\n

a.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa7323(b)\xe2\x80\x99s Application to Federal Election Activity

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs assert that \xc2\xa7323(b) represents a new brand of pervasive federal regulation of state-focused electioneering activities that cannot possibly corrupt or appear to corrupt federal officeholders and thus goes well beyond Congress\xe2\x80\x99 concerns about the corruption of the federal electoral process. We disagree.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is true that \xc2\xa7323(b) captures some activities that affect state campaigns for nonfederal offices. But these are the same sorts of activities that already were covered by the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s pre-BCRA allocation rules, and thus had to be funded in part by hard money, because they affect federal as well as state elections. See 11 CFR \xc2\xa7106.5 (2002). As a practical matter, BCRA merely codifies the principles of the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s allocation regime while at the same time justifiably adjusting the formulas applicable to these activities in order to restore the efficacy of FECA\xe2\x80\x99s longtime statutory restriction\xe2\x80\x94approved by the Court and eroded by the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s allocation regime\xe2\x80\x94on contributions to state and local party committees for the purpose of influencing federal elections. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7431(8)(A), 441a(a)(1)(C); see also Buckley, 424 U. S., at 38 (upholding FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $25,000 limit on aggregate contributions to candidates and political committees); cf. California Medical Assn. v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 453 U. S. 182 (1981) (upholding FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $5,000 limit on contributions to multicandidate political committees).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Like the rest of Title I, \xc2\xa7323(b) is premised on Congress\xe2\x80\x99 judgment that if a large donation is capable of putting a federal candidate in the debt of the contributor, it poses a threat of corruption or the appearance of corruption. As we explain below, \xc2\xa7323(b) is narrowly focused on regulating contributions that pose the greatest risk of this kind of corruption: those contributions to state and local parties that can be used to benefit federal candidates directly. Further, these regulations all are reasonably tailored, with various temporal and substantive limitations designed to focus the regulations on the important anti-corruption interests to be served. We conclude that \xc2\xa7323(b) is a closely-drawn means of countering both corruption and the appearance of corruption.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The first two categories of \xe2\x80\x9cFederal election activity,\xe2\x80\x9d voter registration efforts, \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(i), and voter identification, GOTV, and generic campaign activities conducted in connection with a federal election, \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(ii), clearly capture activity that benefits federal candidates. Common sense dictates, and it was \xe2\x80\x9cundisputed\xe2\x80\x9d below, that a party\xe2\x80\x99s efforts to register voters sympathetic to that party directly assist the party\xe2\x80\x99s candidates for federal office. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 460 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.). It is equally clear that federal candidates reap substantial rewards from any efforts that increase the number of like-minded registered voters who actually go to the polls.[Footnote 62] See, e.g., id., at 459 (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[The evidence] shows quite clearly that a campaign that mobilizes residents of a highly Republican precinct will produce a harvest of votes for Republican candidates for both state and federal offices. A campaign need not mention federal candidates to have a direct effect on voting for such a candidate\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. [G]eneric campaign activity has a direct effect on federal elections\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Green Expert Report 14)). Representatives of the four major congressional campaign committees confirmed that they \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98transfe[r] federal and nonfederal money to state and/or local party committees for\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d both voter registration and get-out-the-vote activities, and that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[t]hese efforts have a significant effect on the election of federal candidates.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 459, 461 (citations\nomitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The record also makes quite clear that federal officeholders are grateful for contributions to state and local parties that can be converted into GOTV-type efforts. See id., at 459 (quoting a letter thanking a California Democratic Party donor and noting that CDP\xe2\x80\x99s voter registration and GOTV efforts would help \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98increase the number of Californian Democrats in the United States Congress\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98deliver California\xe2\x80\x99s 54 electoral votes\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to the Democratic presidential candidate).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Because voter registration, voter identification, GOTV, and generic campaign activity all confer substantial benefits on federal candidates, the funding of such activities creates a significant risk of actual and apparent corruption. Section 323(b) is a reasonable response to that risk. Its contribution limitations are focused on the subset of voter registration activity that is most likely to affect the election prospects of federal candidates: activity that occurs within 120 days before a federal election. And if the voter registration drive does not specifically\nmention a federal candidate, state committees can take advantage of the Levin Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s higher contribution limits and relaxed source restrictions. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441i(b)(2)(B)(i)\xe2\x80\x93(ii) (Supp. 2003). Similarly, the contribution limits applicable to \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(ii) activities target only those voter identification, GOTV, and generic campaign efforts that occur \xe2\x80\x9cin connection with an election in which a candidate for a Federal office appears on the ballot.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7431(20)(A)(ii). Appropriately, in implementing this subsection, the FEC has categorically excluded all activity that takes place during the run-up to elections when no federal office is at stake.[Footnote 63] Furthermore, state committees can take advantage of the Levin Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s higher contribution limits to fund any \xc2\xa7301(A)(20)(i) and \xc2\xa7301(A)(20)(ii) activities that do not specifically mention a federal candidate. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441i(b)(2)(B)(i)\xe2\x80\x93(ii). The prohibition on the use of soft money in connection with these activities is therefore closely drawn to meet the sufficiently important governmental interests of avoiding corruption and its appearance.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cPublic communications\xe2\x80\x9d that promote or attack a candidate for federal office\xe2\x80\x94the third category of \xe2\x80\x9cFederal election activity,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(iii)\xe2\x80\x94also undoubtedly have a dramatic effect on federal elections. Such ads were a prime motivating force behind BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s passage. See 3 1998 Senate Report 4535 (additional views of Sen. Collins) (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he hearings provided overwhelming evidence that the twin loopholes of soft money and bogus issue advertising have virtually destroyed our campaign finance laws, leaving us with little more than a pile of legal rubble\xe2\x80\x9d). As explained below, any public communication that promotes or attacks a clearly identified federal candidate directly affects the election in which he is participating. The record on this score could scarcely be more abundant. Given the overwhelming tendency of public communications, as carefully defined in \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(iii), to benefit directly federal candidates, we hold that application of \xc2\xa7323(b)\xe2\x80\x99s contribution caps to such communications is also closely drawn to the anticorruption interest it is intended to address.[Footnote 64]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As for the final category of \xe2\x80\x9cFederal election activity,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(iv), we find that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 interest in preventing circumvention of \xc2\xa7323(b)\xe2\x80\x99s other restrictions justifies the requirement that state and local parties spend federal funds to pay the salary of any employee spending more than 25% of his or her compensated time on activities in connection with a federal election. In the absence of this provision, a party might use soft money to pay for the equivalent of a full-time employee engaged in federal electioneering, by the simple expedient of dividing the federal workload among multiple employees. Plaintiffs have suggested no reason for us to strike down this provision. Accordingly, we give \xe2\x80\x9cdeference to [the] congressional determination of the need for [this] prophylactic rule.\xe2\x80\x9d National Conservative Political Action Comm., 470 U. S., at 500.

\n

b.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Associational Burdens Imposed by the Levin\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Amendment

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs also contend that \xc2\xa7323(b) is unconstitutional because the Levin Amendment unjustifiably burdens association among party committees by forbidding transfers of Levin funds among state parties, transfers of hard money to fund the allocable federal portion of Levin expenditures, and joint fundraising of Levin funds by state parties. We recognize, as we have in the past, the importance of preserving the associational freedom of parties. See, e.g., California Democratic Party v. Jones, 530 U. S. 567 (2000); Eu v. San Francisco County Democratic Central Comm., 489 U. S. 214 (1989). But not every minor restriction on parties\xe2\x80\x99 otherwise unrestrained ability to associate is of constitutional dimension. See Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 450, n. 11.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As an initial matter, we note that state and local parties can avoid these associational burdens altogether by forgoing the Levin Amendment option and electing to pay for federal election activities entirely with hard money. But in any event, the restrictions on the use, transfer, and raising of Levin funds are justifiable anticircumvention measures. Without the ban on transfers of Levin funds among state committees, donors could readily circumvent the $10,000 limit on contributions to a committee\xe2\x80\x99s Levin account by making multiple $10,000 donations to various committees that could then transfer the donations to the committee of choice.[Footnote 65] The same anticircumvention goal undergirds the ban on joint solicitation of Levin funds. Without this restriction, state and local committees could organize \xe2\x80\x9call hands\xe2\x80\x9d fundraisers at which individual, corporate, or union donors could make large soft-money donations to be divided between the committees. In that case, the purpose, if not the letter, of \xc2\xa7323(b)(2)\xe2\x80\x99s $10,000 limit would be thwarted: Donors could make large, visible contributions at fundraisers, which would provide ready means for corrupting federal officeholders. Given the delicate and interconnected regulatory scheme at issue here, any associational burdens imposed by the Levin Amendment restrictions are far outweighed by the need to prevent circumvention of the entire scheme.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(b)(2)(B)(iv)\xe2\x80\x99s apparent prohibition on the transfer of hard money by a national, state, or local committee to help fund the allocable hard-money portion of a separate state or local committee\xe2\x80\x99s Levin expenditures presents a closer question. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(b)(2)(B)(iv) (Supp. 2003). The Government defends the restriction as necessary to prevent the donor committee, particularly a national committee, from leveraging the transfer of federal money to wrest control over the spending of the recipient committee\xe2\x80\x99s Levin funds. This purported interest is weak, particularly given the fact that \xc2\xa7323(a) already polices attempts by national parties to engage in such behavior. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(a)(2) (extending \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions to entities controlled by national party committees). However, the associational burdens posed by the hard-money transfer restriction are so insubstantial as to be de minimis. Party committees, including national party committees, remain free to transfer unlimited hard money so long as it is not used to fund Levin expenditures. State and local party committees can thus dedicate all \xe2\x80\x9chomegrown\xe2\x80\x9d hard money to their Levin activities while relying on outside transfers to defray the costs of other hard-money expenditures. Given the strong anticircumvention interest vindicated by \xc2\xa7323(b)(2)(B)(iv)\xe2\x80\x99s restriction on the transfer of Levin funds, we will not strike down the entire provision based upon such an attenuated claim of associational infringement.

\n

c.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(b)\xe2\x80\x99s Impact on Parties\xe2\x80\x99 Ability\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0to Engage in Effective Advocacy

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, plaintiffs contend that \xc2\xa7323(b) is unconstitutional because its restrictions on soft-money contributions to state and local party committees will prevent them from engaging in effective advocacy. As Judge Kollar-Kotelly noted, the political parties\xe2\x80\x99 evidence regarding the impact of BCRA on their revenues is \xe2\x80\x9cspeculative and not based on any analysis.\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 524. If the history of campaign finance regulation discussed above proves anything, it is that political parties are extraordinarily flexible in adapting to new restrictions on their fundraising abilities. Moreover, the mere fact that \xc2\xa7323(b) may reduce the relative amount of money available to state and local parties to fund federal election activities is largely inconsequential. The question is not whether \xc2\xa7323(b) reduces the amount of funds available over previous election cycles, but whether it is \xe2\x80\x9cso radical in effect as to \xe2\x80\xa6 drive the sound of [the recipient\xe2\x80\x99s] voice below the level of notice.\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 397. If indeed state or local parties can make such a showing, as-applied challenges remain available.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We accordingly conclude that \xc2\xa7323(b), on its face, is closely drawn to match the important governmental interests of preventing corruption and the appearance of corruption.

\n

New FECA \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s Restrictions on Parties\xe2\x80\x99 Solicitations for, and Donations to, Tax-Exempt Organizations

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(d) prohibits national, state, and local party committees, and their agents or subsidiaries, from \xe2\x80\x9csolicit[ing] any funds for, or mak[ing] or direct[ing] any donations\xe2\x80\x9d to, any organization established under \xc2\xa7501(c) of the Internal Revenue Code[Footnote 66] that makes expenditures in connection with an election for federal office, and any political organizations established under \xc2\xa7527 \xe2\x80\x9cother than a political committee, a State, district, or local committee of a political party, or the authorized campaign committee of a candidate for State or local office.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 67] 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(d) (Supp. 2003). The District Court struck down the provision on its face. We reverse and uphold \xc2\xa7323(d), narrowly construing the section\xe2\x80\x99s ban on donations to apply only to the donation of funds not raised in compliance with FECA.

\n

1.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s Regulation of Solicitations

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government defends \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s ban on solicitations to tax-exempt organizations engaged in political activity as preventing circumvention of Title I\xe2\x80\x99s limits on contributions of soft money to national, state, and local party committees. That justification is entirely reasonable. The history of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 efforts at campaign finance reform well demonstrates that \xe2\x80\x9ccandidates, donors, and parties test the limits of the current law.\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 457. Absent the solicitation provision, national, state, and local party committees would have significant incentives to mobilize their formidable fundraising apparatuses, including the peddling of access to federal officeholders, into the service of like-minded tax-exempt organizations that conduct activities benefiting their candidates.[Footnote 68] All of the corruption and appearance of corruption attendant on the operation of those fundraising apparatuses would follow. Donations made at the behest of party committees would almost certainly be regarded by party officials, donors, and federal officeholders alike as benefiting the party as well as its candidates. Yet, by soliciting the donations to third-party organizations, the parties would avoid FECA\xe2\x80\x99s source-and-amount limitations, as well as its disclosure restrictions. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 348 (Henderson, J.) (citing various declarations demonstrating that, prior to BCRA, most tax-exempt organizations did not disclose the source or amount of contributions); id., at 521 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (same).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Experience under the current law demonstrates that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 concerns about circumvention are not merely hypothetical. Even without the added incentives created by Title I, national, state, and local parties already solicit unregulated soft-money donations to tax-exempt organizations for the purpose of supporting federal electioneering activity. See, e.g., 3 1998 Senate Report 4013 (\xe2\x80\x9cIn addition to direct contributions from the RNC to nonprofit groups, the senior leadership of the RNC helped to raise funds for many of the coalition\xe2\x80\x99s nonprofit organizations\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 5983 (minority views) (\xe2\x80\x9cTax-exempt \xe2\x80\x98issue advocacy\xe2\x80\x99 groups and other conduits were systematically used to circumvent federal campaign finance laws\xe2\x80\x9d); 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 517 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 848 (Leon, J.). Parties and candidates have also begun to take advantage of so-called \xe2\x80\x9cpolitician 527s,\xe2\x80\x9d which are little more than soft-money fronts for the promotion of particular federal officeholders and their interests. See id., at 519 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98Virtually every member of Congress in a formal leadership position has his or her own 527 group\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. In all, Public Citizen found 63 current members of Congress who have their own 527s\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Public Citizen Congress Watch, Congressional Leaders\xe2\x80\x99 Soft Money Accounts Show Need for Campaign Finance Reform Bills, Feb. 26, 2002, p. 6)); 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 849\xe2\x80\x93850 (Leon, J.). These 527s have been quite successful at raising substantial sums of soft money from corporate interests, as well as from the national parties themselves. See id., at 519\xe2\x80\x93520 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (finding that 27 industries had each donated over $100,000 in a single year to the top 25 politician 527 groups and that the DNC was the single largest contributor to politician 527 groups (citing Public Citizen Congress Watch, supra, at 10\xe2\x80\x9311)); 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 850 (Leon, J.) (same). Given BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s tighter restrictions on the raising and spending of soft money, the incentives for parties to exploit such organizations will only increase.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s solicitation restriction is closely drawn to prevent political parties from using tax-exempt organizations as soft-money surrogates. Though phrased as an absolute prohibition, the restriction does nothing more than subject contributions solicited by parties to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s regulatory regime, leaving open substantial opportunities for solicitation and other expressive activity in support of these organizations. First, and most obviously, \xc2\xa7323(d) restricts solicitations only to those \xc2\xa7501(c) groups \xe2\x80\x9cmak[ing] expenditures or disbursements in connection with an election for Federal office,\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(d)(1) (Supp. 2003), and to \xc2\xa7527 organizations, which by definition engage in partisan political activity, \xc2\xa7441i(d)(2); 26 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7527(e). Second, parties remain free to solicit hard-money contributions to a \xc2\xa7501(c)\xe2\x80\x99s federal PAC, as well as to \xc2\xa7527 organizations that already qualify as federal PACs.[Footnote 69] Third, \xc2\xa7323(d) allows parties to endorse qualifying organizations in ways other than direct solicitations of unregulated donations. For example, with respect to \xc2\xa7501(c) organizations that are prohibited from administering PACs, parties can solicit hard-money donations to themselves for the express purpose of donating to these organizations. See supra, at 72\xe2\x80\x9373. Finally, as with \xc2\xa7323(a), \xc2\xa7323(d) in no way restricts solicitations by party officers acting in their individual capacities. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(d) (extending restrictions to solicitations and donations made by \xe2\x80\x9can officer or agent acting on behalf of any such party committee\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In challenging \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s ban on solicitations, plaintiffs renew the argument they made with respect to \xc2\xa7323(a)\xe2\x80\x99s solicitation restrictions: that it cannot be squared with \xc2\xa7323(e), which allows federal candidates and officeholders to solicit limited donations of soft money to tax-exempt organizations that engage in federal election activities. Compare 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(d) with \xc2\xa7441i(e)(4). But if \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on solicitations are otherwise valid, they are not rendered unconstitutional by the mere fact that Congress chose not to regulate the activities of another group as stringently as it might have. See National Right to Work, 459 U. S., at 210; see also Katzenbach v. Morgan, 384 U. S. 641, 656\xe2\x80\x93657 (1966). In any event, the difference between the two provisions is fully explained by the fact that national party officers, unlike federal candidates and officeholders, are able to solicit soft money on behalf of nonprofit organizations in their individual capacities. Section 323(e), which is designed to accommodate the individual associational and speech interests of candidates and officeholders in lending personal support to nonprofit organizations, also places tight content, source, and amount restrictions on solicitations of soft money by federal candidates and officeholders. Given those limits, as well as the less rigorous standard of review, the greater allowances of \xc2\xa7323(e) do not render \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s solicitation restriction facially invalid.

\n

2.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s Regulation of Donations

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(d) also prohibits national, state, and local party committees from making or directing \xe2\x80\x9cany donatio[n]\xe2\x80\x9d to qualifying \xc2\xa7501(c) or \xc2\xa7527 organizations. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(d) (Supp. 2003). The Government again defends the restriction as an anticircumvention measure. We agree insofar as it prohibits the donation of soft money. Absent such a restriction, state and local party committees could accomplish directly what the antisolicitation restrictions prevent them from doing indirectly\xe2\x80\x94\nnamely, raising large sums of soft money to launder through tax-exempt organizations engaging in federal election activities. Because the party itself would be raising and collecting the funds, the potential for corruption would be that much greater. We will not disturb Congress\xe2\x80\x99 reasonable decision to close that loophole, particularly given a record demonstrating an already robust practice of parties\xe2\x80\x99 making such donations. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 517\xe2\x80\x93518 (Kollar-Kotelly); id., at 848\xe2\x80\x93849 (Leon, J.).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The prohibition does raise overbreadth concerns if read to restrict donations from a party\xe2\x80\x99s federal account\xe2\x80\x94i.e., funds that have already been raised in compliance with FECA\xe2\x80\x99s source, amount, and disclosure limitations. Parties have many valid reasons for giving to tax-exempt organizations, not the least of which is to associate themselves with certain causes and, in so doing, to demonstrate the values espoused by the party. A complete ban on donations prevents parties from making even the \xe2\x80\x9cgeneral expression of support\xe2\x80\x9d that a contribution represents. Buckley, 424 U. S., at 21. At the same time, prohibiting parties from donating funds already raised in compliance with FECA does little to further Congress\xe2\x80\x99 goal of preventing corruption or the appearance of corruption of federal candidates and officeholders.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government asserts that the restriction is necessary to prevent parties from leveraging their hard money to gain control over a tax-exempt group\xe2\x80\x99s soft money. Even if we accepted that rationale, it would at most justify a dollar limit, not a flat ban. Moreover, any legitimate concerns over capture are diminished by the fact that the restrictions set forth in \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(a) and (b) apply not only to party committees, but to entities under their control. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(a)(2) (extending prohibitions on national party committees to \xe2\x80\x9cany entity that is directly or indirectly established, financed, maintained, or controlled by such a national committee\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)); \xc2\xa7441i(b)(1) (same for state and local party committees).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These observations do not, however, require us to sustain plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 facial challenge to \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s donation restriction. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the validity of an act of the Congress is drawn in question, and \xe2\x80\xa6 a serious doubt of constitutionality is raised, it is a cardinal principle that this Court will first ascertain whether a construction of the statute is fairly possible by which the question may be avoided.\xe2\x80\x9d Crowell v. Benson, 285 U. S. 22, 62 (1932); see also Boos v. Barry, 485 U. S. 312, 331 (1988); New York v. Ferber, 458 U. S. 747, 769, n. 24 (1982). Given our obligation to avoid constitutional problems, we narrowly construe \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s ban to apply only to donations of funds not raised in compliance with FECA. This construction is consistent with the concerns animating Title I, whose purpose is to plug the soft-money loophole. Though there is little legislative history regarding BCRA generally, and almost nothing on \xc2\xa7323(d) specifically, the abuses identified in the 1998 Senate report regarding campaign finance practices involve the use of nonprofit organizations as conduits for large soft-money donations. See, e.g., 3 1998 Senate Report 4565 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe evidence indicates that the soft-money loophole is fueling many of the campaign abuses investigated by the Committee\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Soft money also supplied the funds parties used to make contributions to tax-exempt groups, which in turn used the funds to pay for election-related activities\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 4568\xe2\x80\x934569 (describing as an \xe2\x80\x9cegregious exampl[e]\xe2\x80\x9d of misuse a $4.6 million donation of nonfederal funds by the RNC to Americans for Tax Reform, which the organization spent on \xe2\x80\x9cdirect mail and phone bank operations to counter anti-Republican advertising\xe2\x80\x9d). We have found no evidence that Congress was concerned about, much less that it intended to prohibit, donations of money already fully regulated by FECA. Given Title I\xe2\x80\x99s exclusive focus on abuses related to soft money, we would expect that if Congress meant \xc2\xa7323(d)\xe2\x80\x99s restriction to have this dramatic and constitutionally questionable effect, it would say so explicitly. Because there is nothing that compels us to conclude that Congress intended \xe2\x80\x9cdonations\xe2\x80\x9d to include transfers of federal money, and because of the constitutional infirmities such an interpretation would raise, we decline to read \xc2\xa7323(d) in that way. Thus, political parties remain free to make or direct donations of money to any tax-exempt organization that has otherwise been raised in compliance with FECA.

\n

New FECA \xc2\xa7323(e)\xe2\x80\x99s Restrictions on Federal Candidates and Officeholders

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7323(e) regulates the raising and soliciting of soft money by federal candidates and officeholders. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(e) (Supp. 2003). It prohibits federal candidates and officeholders from \xe2\x80\x9csolicit[ing], receiv[ing], direct[ing], transfer[ing], or spend[ing]\xe2\x80\x9d any soft money in connection with federal elections. \xc2\xa7441i(e)(1)(A). It also limits the ability of federal candidates and officeholders to solicit, receive, direct, transfer, or spend soft money in connection with state and local elections. \xc2\xa7441i(e)(1)(B).[Footnote 70]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(e)\xe2\x80\x99s general prohibition on solicitations admits of a number of exceptions. For instance, federal candidates and officeholders are permitted to \xe2\x80\x9cattend, speak, or be a featured guest\xe2\x80\x9d at a state or local party fundraising event. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(e)(3). Section 323(e) specifically provides that federal candidates and officeholders may make solicitations of soft money to \xc2\xa7501(c) organizations whose primary purpose is not to engage in \xe2\x80\x9cFederal election activit[ies]\xe2\x80\x9d as long as the solicitation does not specify how the funds will be spent, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(e)(4)(A); to \xc2\xa7501(c) organizations whose primary purpose is to engage in \xe2\x80\x9cFederal election activit[ies]\xe2\x80\x9d as long as the solicitations are limited to individuals and the amount solicited does not exceed $20,000 per year per individual, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(e)(4)(B); and to \xc2\xa7501(c) organizations for the express purpose of carrying out such activities, again so long as the amount solicited does not exceed $20,000 per year per individual, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441(e)(4)(B).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0No party seriously questions the constitutionality of \xc2\xa7323(e)\xe2\x80\x99s general ban on donations of soft money made directly to federal candidates and officeholders, their agents, or entities established or controlled by them. Even on the narrowest reading of Buckley, a regulation restricting donations to a federal candidate, regardless of the ends to which those funds are ultimately put, qualifies as a contribution limit subject to less rigorous scrutiny. Such donations have only marginal speech and associational value, but at the same time pose a substantial threat of corruption. By severing the most direct link between the soft-money donor and the federal candidate, \xc2\xa7323(e)\xe2\x80\x99s ban on donations of soft money is closely drawn to prevent the corruption or the appearance of corruption of federal candidates and officeholders.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(e)\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on solicitations are justified as valid anticircumvention measures. Large soft-money donations at a candidate\xe2\x80\x99s or officeholder\xe2\x80\x99s behest give rise to all of the same corruption concerns posed by contributions made directly to the candidate or officeholder. Though the candidate may not ultimately control how the funds are spent, the value of the donation to the candidate or officeholder is evident from the fact of the solicitation itself. Without some restriction on solicitations, federal candidates and officeholders could easily avoid FECA\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limits by soliciting funds from large donors and restricted sources to like-minded organizations engaging in federal election activities. As the record demonstrates, even before the passage of BCRA, federal candidates and officeholders had already begun soliciting donations to state and local parties, as well as tax-exempt organizations, in order to help their own, as well as their party\xe2\x80\x99s, electoral cause. See Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 458 (quoting fundraising letter from a Congressman explaining to contributor that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98you are at the limit of what you can directly contribute to my campaign,\xe2\x80\x99 but \xe2\x80\x98you can further help my campaign by assisting the Colorado Republican Party\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d); 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 479\xe2\x80\x93480 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (surveying evidence of federal officeholders\xe2\x80\x99 soliciting funds to state and local parties); id., at 848 (Leon, J.) (same); id., at 518 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (surveying evidence of federal officeholders\xe2\x80\x99 soliciting funds for nonprofits for electioneering purposes); id., at 849 (Leon, J.) (same). The incentives to do so, at least with respect to solicitations to tax-exempt organizations, will only increase with Title I\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on the raising and spending of soft money by national, state, and local parties.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(e) addresses these concerns while accommodating the individual speech and associational rights of federal candidates and officeholders. Rather than place an outright ban on solicitations to tax-exempt organizations, \xc2\xa7323(e)(4) permits limited solicitations of soft money. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(e)(4). This allowance accommodates individuals who have long served as active members of nonprofit organizations in both their official and individual capacities. Similarly, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(e)(1)(B) and 323(e)(3) preserve the traditional fundraising role of federal officeholders by providing limited opportunities for federal candidates and officeholders to associate with their state and local colleagues through joint fundraising activities. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441i(e)(1)(B), 441i(e)(3). Given these many exceptions, as well as the substantial threat of corruption or its appearance posed by donations to or at the behest of federal candidates and officeholders, \xc2\xa7323(e) is clearly constitutional. We accordingly uphold \xc2\xa7323(e) against plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 First Amendment challenge.

\n

New FECA \xc2\xa7323(f)\xe2\x80\x99s Restrictions on State Candidates and Officeholders

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The final provision of Title I is new FECA \xc2\xa7323(f). 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(f) (Supp. 2003). Section 323(f) generally prohibits candidates for state or local office, or state or local officeholders, from spending soft money to fund \xe2\x80\x9cpublic communications\xe2\x80\x9d as defined in \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(iii)\xe2\x80\x94\ni.e., a communication that \xe2\x80\x9crefers to a clearly identified candidate for Federal office \xe2\x80\xa6 and that promotes or supports a candidate for that office, or attacks or opposes a candidate for that office.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(f)(1); \xc2\xa7431(20)(A)(iii). Exempted from this restriction are communications made in connection with an election for state or local office which refer only to the state or local candidate or officeholder making the expenditure or to any other candidate for the same state or local office. \xc2\xa7441i(f)(2).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 323(f) places no cap on the amount of money that state or local candidates can spend on any activity. Rather, like \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(a) and 323(b), it limits only the source and amount of contributions that state and local candidates can draw on to fund expenditures that directly impact federal elections. And, by regulating only contributions used to fund \xe2\x80\x9cpublic communications,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7323(f) focuses narrowly on those soft-money donations with the greatest potential to corrupt or give rise to the appearance of corruption of federal candidates and officeholders.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs advance two principal arguments against \xc2\xa7323(f). We have already rejected the first argument, that the definition of \xe2\x80\x9cpublic communications\xe2\x80\x9d in new FECA \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(iii) is unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. See supra, 62, n.\xc2\xa064. We add only that, plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 and Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s contrary reading notwithstanding, post, at 34, this provision does not prohibit a state or local candidate from advertising that he has received a federal officeholder\xe2\x80\x99s endorsement.[Footnote 71]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The second argument, that soft-money contributions to state and local candidates for \xe2\x80\x9cpublic communications\xe2\x80\x9d do not corrupt or appear to corrupt federal candidates, ignores both the record in this litigation and Congress\xe2\x80\x99 strong interest in preventing circumvention of otherwise valid contribution limits. The proliferation of sham issue ads has driven the soft-money explosion. Parties have sought out every possible way to fund and produce these ads with soft money: They have labored to bring them under the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s allocation regime; they have raised and transferred soft money from national to state party committees to take advantage of favorable allocation ratios; and they have transferred and solicited funds to tax-exempt organizations for production of such ads. We will not upset Congress\xe2\x80\x99 eminently reasonable prediction that, with these other avenues no longer available, state and local candidates and officeholders will become the next conduits for the soft-money funding of sham issue advertising. We therefore uphold \xc2\xa7323(f) against plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 First Amendment challenge.[Footnote 72]

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Several plaintiffs contend that Title I exceeds Congress\xe2\x80\x99 Election Clause authority to \xe2\x80\x9cmake or alter\xe2\x80\x9d rules governing federal elections, U. S. Const., Art. I, \xc2\xa74, and, by impairing the authority of the States to regulate their own elections, violates constitutional principles of federalism. In examining congressional enactments for infirmity under the Tenth Amendment, this Court has focused its attention on laws that commandeer the States and state officials in carrying out federal regulatory schemes. See Printz v. United States, 521 U. S. 898 (1997); New York v. United States, 505 U. S. 144 (1992). By contrast, Title I of BCRA only regulates the conduct of private parties. It imposes no requirements whatsoever upon States or state officials, and, because it does not expressly pre-empt state legislation, it leaves the States free to enforce their own restrictions on the financing of state electoral campaigns. It is true that Title I, as amended, prohibits some fundraising tactics that would otherwise be permitted under the laws of various States, and that it may therefore have an indirect effect on the financing of state electoral campaigns. But these indirect effects do not render BCRA unconstitutional. It is not uncommon for federal law to prohibit private conduct that is legal in some States. See, e.g., United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers\xe2\x80\x99 Cooperative, 532 U. S. 483 (2001). Indeed, such conflict is inevitable in areas of law that involve both state and federal concerns. It is not in and of itself a marker of constitutional infirmity. See Ex parte Siebold, 100 U. S. 371, 392 (1879).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Of course, in maintaining the federal system envisioned by the Founders, this Court has done more than just prevent Congress from commandeering the States. We have also policed the absolute boundaries of congressional power under Article I. See United States v. Morrison, 529 U. S. 598 (2000); United States v. Lopez, 514 U. S. 549 (1995). But plaintiffs offer no reason to believe that Congress has overstepped its Elections Clause power in enacting BCRA. Congress has a fully legitimate interest in maintaining the integrity of federal officeholders and preventing corruption of federal electoral processes through the means it has chosen. Indeed, our above analysis turns on our finding that those interests are sufficient to satisfy First Amendment scrutiny. Given that finding, we cannot conclude that those interests are insufficient to ground Congress\xe2\x80\x99 exercise of its Elections Clause power. See Morrison, supra, at 607 (respect owed to coordinate branches \xe2\x80\x9cdemands that we invalidate a congressional enactment only upon a plain showing that Congress has exceeded its constitutional bounds\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

C

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, plaintiffs argue that Title I violates the equal protection component of the Due Process Clause of the Fifth Amendment because it discriminates against political parties in favor of special interest groups such as the National Rifle Association (NRA), American Civil Liberties Union (ACLU), and Sierra Club. As explained earlier, BCRA imposes numerous restrictions on the fundraising abilities of political parties, of which the soft-money ban is only the most prominent. Interest groups, however, remain free to raise soft money to fund voter registration, GOTV activities, mailings, and broadcast advertising (other than electioneering communications). We conclude that this disparate treatment does not offend the Constitution.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As an initial matter, we note that BCRA actually favors political parties in many ways. Most obviously, party committees are entitled to receive individual contributions that substantially exceed FECA\xe2\x80\x99s limits on contributions to nonparty political committees; individuals can give $25,000 to political party committees whereas they can give a maximum of $5,000 to nonparty political committees. In addition, party committees are entitled in effect to contribute to candidates by making coordinated expenditures, and those expenditures may greatly exceed the contribution limits that apply to other donors. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(d) (Supp. 2003).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0More importantly, however, Congress is fully entitled to consider the real-world differences between political parties and interest groups when crafting a system of campaign finance regulation. See National Right to Work, 459 U. S., at 210. Interest groups do not select slates of candidates for elections. Interest groups do not determine who will serve on legislative committees, elect congressional leadership, or organize legislative caucuses. Political parties have influence and power in the legislature that vastly exceeds that of any interest group. As a result, it is hardly surprising that party affiliation is the primary way by which voters identify candidates, or that parties in turn have special access to and relationships with federal officeholders. Congress\xe2\x80\x99 efforts at campaign finance regulation may account for these salient differences.
