Taken seriously, appellants\xe2\x80\x99 equal protection arguments would call into question not just Title I of BCRA, but much of the pre-existing structure of FECA as well. We therefore reject those arguments.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Accordingly, we affirm the judgment of the District Court insofar as it upheld \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(e) and 323(f). We reverse the judgment of the District Court insofar as it invalidated \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(a), 323(b), and 323(d).

\n

IV

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Title II of BCRA, entitled \xe2\x80\x9cNoncandidate Campaign Expenditures,\xe2\x80\x9d is divided into two subtitles: \xe2\x80\x9cElectioneering Communications\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cIndependent and Coordinated Expenditures.\xe2\x80\x9d We consider each challenged section of these subtitles in turn.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7201\xe2\x80\x99s Definition of \xe2\x80\x9cElectioneering Communication\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The first section of Title II, \xc2\xa7201, comprehensively amends FECA \xc2\xa7304, which requires political committees to file detailed periodic financial reports with the FEC. The amendment coins a new term, \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication,\xe2\x80\x9d to replace the narrowing construction of FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure provisions adopted by this Court in Buckley. As discussed further below, that construction limited the coverage of FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirement to communications expressly advocating the election or defeat of particular candidates. By contrast, the term \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication\xe2\x80\x9d is not so limited, but is defined to encompass any \xe2\x80\x9cbroadcast, cable, or satellite communication\xe2\x80\x9d that

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c(I) refers to a clearly identified candidate for Federal office;

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c(II) is made within\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(aa) 60 days before a general, special, or runoff election for the office sought by the candidate; or

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(bb) 30 days before a primary or preference election, or a convention or caucus of a political party that has authority to nominate a candidate, for the office sought by the candidate; and

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c(III) in the case of a communication which refers to a candidate other than President or Vice President, is targeted to the relevant electorate.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434(f)(3)(A)(i) (Supp. 2003).[Footnote 73]

\n

\nNew FECA \xc2\xa7304(f)(3)(C) further provides that a communication is \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98targeted to the relevant electorate\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d if it \xe2\x80\x9ccan be received by 50,000 or more persons\xe2\x80\x9d in the district or State the candidate seeks to represent. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434(f\xc2\xa0)(3)(C).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition to setting forth this definition, BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s amendments to FECA \xc2\xa7304 specify significant disclosure requirements for persons who fund electioneering communications. BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s use of this new term is not, however, limited to the disclosure context: A later section of the Act (BCRA \xc2\xa7203, which amends FECA \xc2\xa7316(b)(2)) restricts corporations\xe2\x80\x99 and labor unions\xe2\x80\x99 funding of electioneering communications. Plaintiffs challenge the constitutionality of the new term as it applies in both the disclosure and the expenditure contexts.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The major premise of plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenge to BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s use of the term \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication\xe2\x80\x9d is that Buckley drew a constitutionally mandated line between express advocacy and so-called issue advocacy, and that speakers possess an inviolable First Amendment right to engage in the latter category of speech. Thus, plaintiffs maintain, Congress cannot constitutionally require disclosure of, or regulate expenditures for, \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications\xe2\x80\x9d without making an exception for those \xe2\x80\x9ccommunications\xe2\x80\x9d that do not meet Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s definition of express advocacy.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0That position misapprehends our prior decisions, for the express advocacy restriction was an endpoint of statutory interpretation, not a first principle of constitutional law. In Buckley we began by examining then-18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7608(e)(1) (1970 ed., Supp. IV), which restricted expenditures \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98relative to a clearly identified candidate,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and we found that the phrase \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98relative to\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d was impermissibly vague. 424 U. S., at 40\xe2\x80\x9342. We concluded that the vagueness deficiencies could \xe2\x80\x9cbe avoided only by reading \xc2\xa7608(e)(1) as limited to communications that include explicit words of advocacy of election or defeat of a candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 74] Id., at 43. We provided examples of words of express advocacy, such as \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98vote for,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98elect,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98support,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\xa6 \xe2\x80\x98defeat,\xe2\x80\x99 [and] \xe2\x80\x98reject,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 44, n. 52, and those examples eventually gave rise to what is now known as the \xe2\x80\x9cmagic words\xe2\x80\x9d requirement.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We then considered FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure provisions, including 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7431(f) (1970 ed., Supp. IV), which defined \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98expenditur[e]\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to include the use of money or other assets \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98for the purpose of \xe2\x80\xa6 influencing\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d a federal election. Buckley, 424 U. S., at 77. Finding that the \xe2\x80\x9cambiguity of this phrase\xe2\x80\x9d posed \xe2\x80\x9cconstitutional problems,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., we noted our \xe2\x80\x9cobligation to construe the statute, if that can be done consistent with the legislature\xe2\x80\x99s purpose, to avoid the shoals of vagueness,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 77\xe2\x80\x9378 (citations omitted). \xe2\x80\x9cTo insure that the reach\xe2\x80\x9d of the disclosure requirement was \xe2\x80\x9cnot impermissibly broad, we construe[d] \xe2\x80\x98expenditure\xe2\x80\x99 for purposes of that section in the same way we construed the terms of \xc2\xa7608(e)\xe2\x80\x94to reach only funds used for communications that expressly advocate the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 80 (footnote omitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Thus, a plain reading of Buckley makes clear that the express advocacy limitation, in both the expenditure and the disclosure contexts, was the product of statutory interpretation rather than a constitutional command.[Footnote 75] In narrowly reading the FECA provisions in Buckley to avoid problems of vagueness and overbreadth, we nowhere suggested that a statute that was neither vague nor overbroad would be required to toe the same express advocacy line. Nor did we suggest as much in MCFL, 479 U. S. 238 (1986), in which we addressed the scope of another FECA expenditure limitation and confirmed the understanding that Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s express advocacy category was a product of statutory construction.[Footnote 76]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In short, the concept of express advocacy and the concomitant class of magic words were born of an effort to avoid constitutional infirmities. See NLRB v. Catholic Bishop of Chicago, 440 U. S. 490, 500 (1979) (citing Murray v. Schooner Charming Betsy, 2 Cranch 64, 118 (1804)). We have long \xe2\x80\x9crigidly adhered\xe2\x80\x9d to the tenet \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98never to formulate a rule of constitutional law broader than is required by the precise facts to which it is to be applied,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Raines, 362 U. S. 17, 21 (1960) (citation omitted), for \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he nature of judicial review constrains us to consider the case that is actually before us,\xe2\x80\x9d James B. Beam Distilling Co. v. Georgia, 501 U. S. 529, 547 (1991) (Blackmun, J., dissenting). Consistent with that principle, our decisions in Buckley and MCFL were specific to the statutory language before us; they in no way drew a constitutional boundary that forever fixed the permissible scope of provisions regulating campaign-related speech.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nor are we persuaded, independent of our precedents, that the First Amendment erects a rigid barrier between express advocacy and so-called issue advocacy. That notion cannot be squared with our longstanding recognition that the presence or absence of magic words cannot meaningfully distinguish electioneering speech from a true issue ad. See Buckley, supra, at 45. Indeed, the unmistakable lesson from the record in this litigation, as all three judges on the District Court agreed, is that Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s magic-words requirement is functionally meaningless. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 303\xe2\x80\x93304 (Henderson, J.); id., at 534 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 875\xe2\x80\x93879 (Leon, J.). Not only can advertisers easily evade the line by eschewing the use of magic words, but they would seldom choose to use such words even if permitted.[Footnote 77] And although the resulting advertisements do not urge the viewer to vote for or against a candidate in so many words, they are no less clearly intended to influence the election.[Footnote 78] Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s express advocacy line, in short, has not aided the legislative effort to combat real or apparent corruption, and Congress enacted BCRA to correct the flaws it found in the existing system.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally we observe that new FECA \xc2\xa7304(f)(3)\xe2\x80\x99s definition of \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication\xe2\x80\x9d raises none of the vagueness concerns that drove our analysis in Buckley. The term \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication\xe2\x80\x9d applies only (1) to a broadcast (2) clearly identifying a candidate for federal office, (3) aired within a specific time period, and (4) targeted to an identified audience of at least 50,000 viewers or listeners. These components are both easily understood and objectively determinable. See Grayned v. City of Rockford, 408 U. S. 104, 108\xe2\x80\x93114 (1972). Thus, the constitutional objection that persuaded the Court in Buckley to limit FECA\xe2\x80\x99s reach to express advocacy is simply inapposite here.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7201\xe2\x80\x99s Disclosure Requirements

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Having rejected the notion that the First Amendment requires Congress to treat so-called issue advocacy differently from express advocacy, we turn to plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 other concerns about the use of the term \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication\xe2\x80\x9d in amended FECA \xc2\xa7304\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure provisions. Under those provisions, whenever any person makes disbursements totaling more than $10,000 during any calendar year for the direct costs of producing and airing electioneering communications, he must file a statement with the FEC identifying the pertinent elections and all persons sharing the costs of the disbursements. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7434(f)(2)(A), (B), and (D) (Supp. 2003). If the disbursements are made from a corporation\xe2\x80\x99s or labor union\xe2\x80\x99s segregated account,[Footnote 79] or by a single individual who has collected contributions from others, the statement must identify all persons who contributed $1,000 or more to the account or the individual during the calendar year. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7434(f)(2)(E), (F). The statement must be filed within 24 hours of each \xe2\x80\x9cdisclosure date\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94a term defined to include the first date and all subsequent dates on which a person\xe2\x80\x99s aggregate undisclosed expenses for electioneering communications exceed $10,000 for that calendar year. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7434(f)(1), (2) and (4). Another subsection further provides that the execution of a contract to make a disbursement is itself treated as a disbursement for purposes of FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements. \xc2\xa7434(f)(5).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition to the failed argument that BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s amendments to FECA \xc2\xa7304 improperly extend to both express and issue advocacy, plaintiffs challenge amended FECA \xc2\xa7304\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements as unnecessarily (1) requiring disclosure of the names of persons who contributed $1,000 or more to the individual or group that paid for a communication, and (2) mandating disclosure of executory contracts for communications that have not yet aired. The District Court rejected the former submission but accepted the latter, finding invalid new FECA \xc2\xa7304(f)(5), which governs executory contracts. Relying on BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s severability provision,[Footnote 80] the court held that invalidation of the executory contracts subsection did not render the balance of BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s amendments to FECA \xc2\xa7304 unconstitutional. 251 F. Supp. 2d, at 242 (per curiam).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We agree with the District Court that the important state interests that prompted the Buckley Court to uphold FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements\xe2\x80\x94providing the electorate with information, deterring actual corruption and avoiding any appearance thereof, and gathering the data necessary to enforce more substantive electioneering restrictions\xe2\x80\x94apply in full to BCRA.[Footnote 81] Accordingly, Buckley amply supports application of FECA \xc2\xa7304\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements to the entire range of \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications.\xe2\x80\x9d As the authors of the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s per curiam opinion concluded after reviewing evidence concerning the use of purported \xe2\x80\x9cissue ads\xe2\x80\x9d to influence federal elections:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cThe factual record demonstrates that the abuse of the present law not only permits corporations and labor unions to fund broadcast advertisements designed to influence federal elections, but permits them to do so while concealing their identities from the public. BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure provisions require these organizations to reveal their identities so that the public is able to identify the source of the funding behind broadcast advertisements influencing certain elections. Plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 disdain for BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure pro-visions is nothing short of surprising. Plaintiffs chal-lenge BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on electioneering communications on the premise that they should be permitted to spend corporate and labor union general treasury funds in the sixty days before the federal elections on broadcast advertisements, which refer to federal candidates, because speech needs to be \xe2\x80\x98uninhibited, robust, and wide-open.\xe2\x80\x99 McConnell Br. at 44 (quoting New York Times Co. v. Sullivan, 376 U. S. 254, 270 (1964)). Curiously, Plaintiffs want to preserve the ability to run these advertisements while hiding behind dubious and misleading names like: \xe2\x80\x98The Coalition-Americans Working for Real Change\xe2\x80\x99 (funded by business organizations opposed to organized labor), \xe2\x80\x98Citizens for Better Medicare\xe2\x80\x99 (funded by the pharmaceutical industry), \xe2\x80\x98Republicans for Clean Air\xe2\x80\x99 (funded by brothers Charles and Sam Wyly). Findings \xc2\xb6 ;\xc2\xb6 ;44, 51, 52. Given these tactics, Plaintiffs never satisfactorily answer the question of how \xe2\x80\x98uninhibited, robust, and wide-open\xe2\x80\x99 speech can occur when organizations hide themselves from the scrutiny of the voting public. McConnell Br. at 44. Plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 argument for striking down BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure provisions does not reinforce the precious First Amendment values that Plaintiffs argue are trampled by BCRA, but ignores the competing First Amendment interests of individual citizens seeking to make informed choices in the political marketplace.\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 237.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The District Court was also correct that Buckley forecloses a facial attack on the new provision in \xc2\xa7304 that requires disclosure of the names of persons contributing $1,000 or more to segregated funds or individuals that spend more than $10,000 in a calendar year on electioneering communications. Like our earlier decision in NAACP v. Alabama ex rel. Patterson, 357 U. S. 449 (1958),[Footnote 82] Buckley recognized that compelled disclosures may impose an unconstitutional burden on the freedom to associate in support of a particular cause. Nevertheless, Buckley rejected the contention that FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements could not constitutionally be applied to minor parties and independent candidates because the Government\xe2\x80\x99s interest in obtaining information from such parties was minimal and the danger of infringing their rights substantial. In Buckley, unlike NAACP, we found no evidence that any party had been exposed to economic reprisals or physical threats as a result of the compelled disclosures. Buckley, 424 U. S., at 69\xe2\x80\x9370. We acknowledged that such a case might arise in the future, however, and addressed the standard of proof that would then apply:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cWe recognize that unduly strict requirements of proof could impose a heavy burden, but it does not follow that a blanket exemption for minor parties is necessary. Minor parties must be allowed sufficient flexibility in the proof of injury to assure a fair consideration of their claim. The evidence offered need show only a reasonable probability that the compelled disclosure of a party\xe2\x80\x99s contributors\xe2\x80\x99 names will subject them to threats, harassment, or reprisals from either Government officials or private parties.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 74.

\n

\nA few years later we used that standard to resolve a minor party\xe2\x80\x99s challenge to the constitutionality of the State of Ohio\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements. We held that the First Amendment prohibits States from compelling disclosures that would subject identified persons to \xe2\x80\x9cthreats, harassment, and reprisals,\xe2\x80\x9d and that the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s findings had established a \xe2\x80\x9creasonable probability\xe2\x80\x9d of such a result.[Footnote 83] Brown v. Socialist Workers \xe2\x80\x9974 Campaign Comm. (Ohio), 459 U. S. 87, 100 (1982).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In this litigation the District Court applied Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s evidentiary standard and found\xe2\x80\x94consistent with our conclusion in Buckley, and in contrast to that in Brown\xe2\x80\x94that the evidence did not establish the requisite \xe2\x80\x9creasonable probability\xe2\x80\x9d of harm to any plaintiff group or its members. The District Court noted that some parties had expressed such concerns, but it found a \xe2\x80\x9clack of specific evidence about the basis for these concerns.\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 247 (per curiam). We agree, but we note that, like our refusal to recognize a blanket exception for minor parties in Buckley, our rejection of plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 facial challenge to the requirement to disclose individual donors does not foreclose possible future challenges to particular applications of that requirement.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We also are unpersuaded by plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenge to new FECA \xc2\xa7304(f)(5), which requires disclosure of executory contracts for electioneering communications:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cContracts to disburse

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cFor purposes of this subsection, a person shall be treated as having made a disbursement if the person has executed a contract to make the disbursement.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434(f)(5) (Supp. 2003).

\n

\nIn our view, this provision serves an important purpose the District Court did not advance. BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s amendments to FECA \xc2\xa7304 mandate disclosure only if and when a person makes disbursements totaling more than $10,000 in any calendar year to pay for electioneering communications. Plaintiffs do not take issue with the use of a dollar amount, rather than the number or dates of the ads, to identify the time when a person paying for electioneering communications must make disclosures to the FEC. Nor do they question the need to make the contents of parties\xe2\x80\x99 disclosure statements available to curious voters in advance of elections. Given the relatively short time frames in which electioneering communications are made, the interest in assuring that disclosures are made promptly and in time to provide relevant information to voters is unquestionably significant. Yet fixing the deadline for filing disclosure statements based on the date when aggregate disbursements exceed $10,000 would open a significant loophole if advertisers were not required to disclose executory contracts. In the absence of that requirement, political supporters could avoid preelection disclosures concerning ads slated to run during the final week of a campaign simply by making a preelection downpayment of less than $10,000, with the balance payable after the election. Indeed, if the advertiser waited to pay that balance until the next calendar year then, as long as the balance did not itself exceed $10,000, the advertiser might avoid the disclosure requirements completely.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The record contains little evidence identifying any harm that might flow from the enforcement of \xc2\xa7304(f)(5)\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cadvance\xe2\x80\x9d disclosure requirement. The District Court speculated that disclosing information about contracts \xe2\x80\x9cthat have not been performed, and may never be performed, may lead to confusion and an unclear record upon which the public will evaluate the forces operating in the political marketplace.\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 241 (per curiam). Without evidence relating to the frequency of nonperformance of executed contracts, such speculation cannot outweigh the public interest in ensuring full disclosure before an election actually takes place. It is no doubt true that \xc2\xa7304(f)(5) will sometimes require the filing of disclosure statements in advance of the actual broadcast of an advertisement.[Footnote 84] But the same would be true in the absence of an advance disclosure requirement, if a television station insisted on advance payment for all of the ads covered by a contract. Thus, the possibility that amended \xc2\xa7304 may sometimes require disclosures prior to the airing of an ad is as much a function of the use of disbursements (rather than the date of an ad) to trigger the disclosure requirement as it is a function of \xc2\xa7304(f)(5)\xe2\x80\x99s treatment of executory contracts.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As the District Court observed, amended FECA \xc2\xa7304\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements are constitutional because they \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98d[o] not prevent anyone from speaking.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (quoting Brief for FEC in Opposition in No. 02\xe2\x80\x93582 et\xc2\xa0al. (DC), p.\xc2\xa0112). Moreover, the required disclosures \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98would not have to reveal the specific content of the advertisements, yet they would perform an important function in informing the public about various candidates\xe2\x80\x99 supporters before election day.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 241 (quoting Brief for FEC in Opposition, supra, at 112) (emphasis in original). Accordingly, we affirm the judgment of the District Court insofar as it upheld the disclosure requirements in amended FECA \xc2\xa7304 and rejected the facial attack on the provisions relating to donors of $1,000 or more, and reverse that judgment insofar as it invalidated FECA \xc2\xa7304(f)(5).

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7202\xe2\x80\x99s Treatment of \xe2\x80\x9cCoordinated Communications\xe2\x80\x9d as Contributions

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 202 of BCRA amends FECA \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(C) to provide that disbursements for \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication[s]\xe2\x80\x9d that are coordinated with a candidate or party will be treated as contributions to, and expenditures by, that candidate or party. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(a)(7)(C) (Supp. 2003).[Footnote 85] The amendment clarifies the scope of the preceding subsection, \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B), which states more generally that \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditures made by any person in cooperation, consultation, or concert, with, or at the request or suggestion of\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d a candidate or party will constitute contributions. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441a(a)(7)(B)(i)\xe2\x80\x93(ii). In Buckley we construed the statutory term \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditure\xe2\x80\x9d to reach only spending for express advocacy. 424 U. S., at 40\xe2\x80\x9343, and n. 52 (addressing 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7608(e)(1) (1970 ed., Supp. IV), which placed a $1,000 cap on expenditures \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98relative to a clearly identified candidate\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d). BCRA \xc2\xa7202 pre-empts a possible claim that \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B) is similarly limited, such that coordinated expenditures for communications that avoid express advocacy cannot be counted as contributions. As we explained above, see supra, at 83\xe2\x80\x9386, Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s narrow interpretation of the term \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditure\xe2\x80\x9d was not a constitutional limitation on Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to regulate federal elections. Accordingly, there is no reason why Congress may not treat coordinated disbursements for electioneering communications in the same way it treats all other coordinated expenditures. We affirm the judgment of the District Court insofar as it held that plaintiffs had advanced \xe2\x80\x9cno basis for finding Section 202 unconstitutional.\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 250.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7203\xe2\x80\x99s Prohibition of Corporate and Labor Disbursements for Electioneering Communications

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Since our decision in Buckley, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to prohibit corporations and unions from using funds in their treasuries to finance advertisements expressly advocating the election or defeat of candidates in federal elections has been firmly embedded in our law. The ability to form and administer separate segregated funds authorized by FECA \xc2\xa7316, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441b (main ed. and Supp. 2003), has provided corporations and unions with a constitutionally sufficient opportunity to engage in express advocacy. That has been this Court\xe2\x80\x99s unanimous view,[Footnote 86] and it is not challenged in this litigation.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 203 of BCRA amends FECA \xc2\xa7316(b)(2) to extend this rule, which previously applied only to express advocacy, to all \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications\xe2\x80\x9d covered by the definition of that term in amended FECA \xc2\xa7304(f)(3), discussed above. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441b(b)(2) (Supp. 2003).[Footnote 87] Thus, under BCRA, corporations and unions may not use their general treasury funds to finance electioneering communications, but they remain free to organize and administer segregated funds, or PACs, for that purpose. Because corporations can still fund electioneering communications with PAC money, it is \xe2\x80\x9csimply wrong\xe2\x80\x9d to view the provision as a \xe2\x80\x9ccomplete ban\xe2\x80\x9d on expression rather than a regulation. Beaumont, 539 U. S., at ___, ___ (slip op., at 15). As we explained in Beaumont:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cThe PAC option allows corporate political participation without the temptation to use corporate funds for political influence, quite possibly at odds with the sentiments of some shareholders or members, and it lets the government regulate campaign activity through registration and disclosure, see [2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.] \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7432\xe2\x80\x93434, without jeopardizing the associational rights of advocacy organizations\xe2\x80\x99 members.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at ___ (slip op., at 16) (citation omitted).

\n

\nSee also Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce, 494 U. S. 652, 658 (1990).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Rather than arguing that the prohibition on the use of general treasury funds is a complete ban that operates as a prior restraint, plaintiffs instead challenge the expanded regulation on the grounds that it is both overbroad and underinclusive. Our consideration of plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenge is informed by our earlier conclusion that the distinction between express advocacy and so-called issue advocacy is not constitutionally compelled. In that light, we must examine the degree to which BCRA burdens First Amendment expression and evaluate whether a compelling governmental interest justifies that burden. Id., at 657. The latter question\xe2\x80\x94whether the state interest is compelling\xe2\x80\x94is easily answered by our prior decisions regarding campaign finance regulation, which \xe2\x80\x9crepresent respect for the \xe2\x80\x98legislative judgment that the special characteristics of the corporate structure require particularly careful regulation.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Beaumont, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 8) (quoting National Right to Work, 459 U. S., at 209\xe2\x80\x93210). We have repeatedly sustained legislation aimed at \xe2\x80\x9cthe corrosive and distorting effects of immense aggregations of wealth that are accumulated with the help of the corporate form and that have little or no correlation to the public\xe2\x80\x99s support for the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d Austin, supra, at 660; see Beaumont, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 7\xe2\x80\x938); National Right to Work, supra, at 209\xe2\x80\x93210. Moreover, recent cases have recognized that certain restrictions on corporate electoral involvement permissibly hedge against \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98circumvention of [valid] contribution limits.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Beaumont, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 7) (quoting Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 456, and n.\xc2\xa018.)

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In light of our precedents, plaintiffs do not contest that the Government has a compelling interest in regulating advertisements that expressly advocate the election or defeat of a candidate for federal office. Nor do they contend that the speech involved in so-called issue advocacy is any more core political speech than are words of express advocacy. After all, \xe2\x80\x9cthe constitutional guarantee has its fullest and most urgent application precisely to the conduct of campaigns for political office,\xe2\x80\x9d Monitor Patriot Co. v. Roy, 401 U. S. 265, 272 (1971), and \xe2\x80\x9c[a]dvocacy of the election or defeat of candidates for federal office is no less entitled to protection under the First Amendment than the discussion of political policy generally or advocacy of the passage or defeat of legislation.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 48. Rather, plaintiffs argue that the justifications that adequately support the regulation of express advocacy do not apply to significant quantities of speech encompassed by the definition of electioneering communications.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This argument fails to the extent that the issue ads broadcast during the 30- and 60-day periods preceding federal primary and general elections are the functional equivalent of express advocacy. The justifications for the regulation of express advocacy apply equally to ads aired during those periods if the ads are intended to influence the voters\xe2\x80\x99 decisions and have that effect. The precise percentage of issue ads that clearly identified a candidate and were aired during those relatively brief preelection time spans but had no electioneering purpose is a matter of dispute between the parties and among the judges on the District Court. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 307\xe2\x80\x93312 (Henderson, J.); id., at 583\xe2\x80\x93587 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 796\xe2\x80\x93798 (Leon, J.). Nevertheless, the vast majority of ads clearly had such a purpose. Annenberg Report 13\xe2\x80\x9314; App. 1330\xe2\x80\x931348 (Krasno & Sorauf Expert Report); 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 573\xe2\x80\x93578 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 826\xe2\x80\x93827 (Leon, J.). Moreover, whatever the precise percentage may have been in the past, in the future corporations and unions may finance genuine issue ads during those time frames by simply avoiding any specific reference to federal candidates, or in doubtful cases by paying for the ad from a segregated fund.[Footnote 88]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We are therefore not persuaded that plaintiffs have carried their heavy burden of proving that amended FECA \xc2\xa7316(b)(2) is overbroad. See Broadrick v. Oklahoma, 413 U. S. 601, 613 (1973). Even if we assumed that BCRA will inhibit some constitutionally protected corporate and union speech, that assumption would not \xe2\x80\x9cjustify prohibiting all enforcement\xe2\x80\x9d of the law unless its application to protected speech is substantial, \xe2\x80\x9cnot only in an absolute sense, but also relative to the scope of the law\xe2\x80\x99s plainly legitimate applications.\xe2\x80\x9d Virginia v. Hicks, 539 U. S. ___, ___ (2003) (slip op., at 5\xe2\x80\x936). Far from establishing that BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s application to pure issue ads is substantial, either in an absolute sense or relative to its application to election-related advertising, the record strongly supports the contrary conclusion.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs also argue that FECA \xc2\xa7316(b)(2)\xe2\x80\x99s segregated-fund requirement for electioneering communications is underinclusive because it does not apply to advertising in the print media or on the Internet. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434(f)(3)(A) (Supp. 2003). The records developed in this litigation and by the Senate Committee adequately explain the reasons for this legislative choice. Congress found that corporations and unions used soft money to finance a virtual torrent of televised election-related ads during the periods immediately preceding federal elections, and that remedial legislation was needed to stanch that flow of money. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 569\xe2\x80\x93573 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 799 (Leon, J.); 3 1998 Senate Report 4465, 4474\xe2\x80\x934481; 5 id., at 7521\xe2\x80\x937525. As we held in Buckley, \xe2\x80\x9creform may take one step at a time, addressing itself to the phase of the problem which seems most acute to the legislative mind.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 105 (internal quotation marks and citations omitted). One might just as well argue that the electioneering communication definition is underinclusive because it leaves advertising 61 days in advance of an election entirely unregulated. The record amply justifies Congress\xe2\x80\x99 line drawing.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition to arguing that \xc2\xa7316(b)(2)\xe2\x80\x99s segregated-fund requirement is underinclusive, some plaintiffs contend that it unconstitutionally discriminates in favor of media companies. FECA \xc2\xa7304(f)(3)(B)(i) excludes from the definition of electioneering communications any \xe2\x80\x9ccommunication appearing in a news story, commentary, or editorial distributed through the facilities of any broadcasting station, unless such facilities are owned or controlled by any political party, political committee, or candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434(f)(3)(B)(i) (Supp. 2003). Plaintiffs argue this provision gives free rein to media companies to engage in speech without resort to PAC money. Section 304(f)(3)(B)(i)\xe2\x80\x99s effect, however, is much narrower than plaintiffs suggest. The provision excepts news items and commentary only; it does not afford carte blanche to media companies generally to ignore FECA\xe2\x80\x99s provisions. The statute\xe2\x80\x99s narrow exception is wholly consistent with First Amendment principles. \xe2\x80\x9cA valid distinction \xe2\x80\xa6 exists between corporations that are part of the media industry and other corporations that are not involved in the regular business of imparting news to the public.\xe2\x80\x9d Austin, 494 U. S., at 668. Numerous federal statutes have drawn this distinction to ensure that the law \xe2\x80\x9cdoes not hinder or prevent the institutional press from reporting on, and publishing editorials about, newsworthy events.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (citations omitted); see, e.g., 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7431(9)(B)(i) (exempting news stories, commentaries, and editorials from FECA\xe2\x80\x99s definition of \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditure\xe2\x80\x9d); 15 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71801\xe2\x80\x931804 (providing a limited antitrust exemption for newspapers); 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7315(a) (excepting newscasts, news interviews, and news documentaries from the requirement that broadcasters provide equal time to candidates for public office).[Footnote 89]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We affirm the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s judgment to the extent that it upheld the constitutionality of FECA \xc2\xa7316(b)(2); to the extent that it invalidated any part of \xc2\xa7316(b)(2), we reverse the judgment.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7204\xe2\x80\x99s Application to Nonprofit Corporations

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 204 of BCRA, which adds FECA \xc2\xa7316(c)(6), applies the prohibition on the use of general treasury funds to pay for electioneering communications to not-for-profit corporations.[Footnote 90] Prior to the enactment of BCRA, FECA required such corporations, like business corporations, to pay for their express advocacy from segregated funds rather than from their general treasuries. Our recent decision in Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Beaumont, 539 U. S. ___ (2003), confirmed that the requirement was valid except insofar as it applied to a sub-category of corporations described as \xe2\x80\x9cMCFL organizations,\xe2\x80\x9d as defined by our decision in MCFL, 479 U. S. 238 (1986).[Footnote 91] The constitutional objection to applying FECA\xe2\x80\x99s segregated-fund requirement to so-called MCFL organizations necessarily applies with equal force to FECA \xc2\xa7316(c)(6).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Our decision in MCFL related to a carefully defined category of entities. We identified three features of the organization at issue in that case that were central to our holding:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cFirst, it was formed for the express purpose of promoting political ideas, and cannot engage in business activities. If political fundraising events are expressly denominated as requests for contributions that will be used for political purposes, including direct expenditures, these events cannot be considered business activities. This ensures that political resources reflect political support. Second, it has no shareholders or other persons affiliated so as to have a claim on its assets or earnings. This ensures that persons connected with the organization will have no economic disincentive for disassociating with it if they disagree with its political activity. Third, MCFL was not established by a business corporation or a labor union, and it is its policy not to accept contributions from such entities. This prevents such corporations from serving as conduits for the type of direct spending that creates a threat to the political marketplace.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 264.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0That FECA \xc2\xa7316(c)(6) does not, on its face, exempt MCFL organizations from its prohibition is not a sufficient reason to invalidate the entire section. If a reasonable limiting construction \xe2\x80\x9chas been or could be placed on the challenged statute\xe2\x80\x9d to avoid constitutional concerns, we should embrace it. Broadrick, 413 U. S., at 613; Buckley, 424 U. S., at 44. Because our decision in the MCFL case was on the books for many years before BCRA was enacted, we presume that the legislators who drafted \xc2\xa7316(c)(6) were fully aware that the provision could not validly apply to MCFL-type entities. See Bowen v. Massachusetts, 487 U. S. 879, 896 (1988); Cannon v. University of Chicago, 441 U. S. 677, 696\xe2\x80\x93697 (1979). Indeed, the Government itself concedes that \xc2\xa7316(c)(6) does not apply to MCFL organizations. As so construed, the provision is plainly valid. See Austin, 494 U. S., at 661\xe2\x80\x93665 (holding that a segregated-fund requirement that did not explicitly carve out an MCFL exception could apply to a nonprofit corporation that did not qualify for MCFL status).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Accordingly, the judgment of the District Court upholding \xc2\xa7316(c)(6) as so limited is affirmed.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7212\xe2\x80\x99s Reporting Requirement for $1,000 Expenditures

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 212 of BCRA amends FECA \xc2\xa7304 to add a new disclosure requirement, FECA \xc2\xa7304(g), which applies to persons making independent expenditures of $1,000 or more during the 20-day period immediately preceding an election. Like FECA \xc2\xa7304(f)(5), discussed above, new \xc2\xa7304(g) treats the execution of a contract to make a disbursement as the functional equivalent of a payment for the goods or services covered by the contract.[Footnote 92] In challenging this provision, plaintiffs renew the argument we rejected in the context of \xc2\xa7304(f)(5): that they have a constitutional right to postpone any disclosure until after the performance of the services purchased by their expenditure.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The District Court held that the challenge to FECA \xc2\xa7304(g) was not ripe because the FEC has issued regulations \xe2\x80\x9cprovid[ing] Plaintiffs with the exact remedy they seek\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94that is, specifically declining to \xe2\x80\x9crequire disclosure of independent express advocacy expenditures prior to their \xe2\x80\x98publi[c] disseminat[ion].\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 251, and n.\xc2\xa085 (per curiam) (citing 68 Fed. Reg. 404, 452 (2003) (codified at 11 CFR \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7109.10(c), (d) (2003))). We are not certain that a regulation purporting to limit the range of circumstances in which a speech-burdening statute will be enforced can render nonjusticiable a facial challenge to the (concededly broader) underlying statute. Nevertheless, we need not separately address the constitutionality of \xc2\xa7304(g), for our ruling as to BCRA \xc2\xa7201, see supra, at 82\xe2\x80\x93\n89, renders the issue essentially moot.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7213\xe2\x80\x99s Requirement that Political Parties Choose Between Coordinated and Independent Expenditures After Nominating a Candidate

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 213 of BCRA amends FECA \xc2\xa7315(d)(4) to impose certain limits on party spending during the postnomination, preelection period.[Footnote 93] At first blush, the text of \xc2\xa7315(d)(4)(A) appears to require political parties to make a straightforward choice between using limited coordinated expenditures or unlimited independent expenditures to support their nominees. All three judges on the District Court concluded that the provision placed an unconstitutional burden on the parties\xe2\x80\x99 right to make unlimited independent expenditures. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 388 (Henderson, J.); id., at 650\xe2\x80\x93651 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.), id., at 805\xe2\x80\x93808 (Leon, J.). In the end, we agree with that conclusion but believe it important to identify certain complexities in the text of \xc2\xa7315(d)(4) that affect our analysis of the issue.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 315 of FECA sets forth various limitations on contributions and expenditures by individuals, political parties, and other groups. Section 315(a)(2) restricts \xe2\x80\x9ccontributions\xe2\x80\x9d by parties to $5,000 per candidate. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(a)(2). Because \xc2\xa7315(a)(7) treats expenditures that are coordinated with a candidate as contributions to that candidate, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441(a)(7) (Supp. 2003), the $5,000 limit also operates as a cap on parties\xe2\x80\x99 coordinated expenditures. Section 315(d), however, provides that, \xe2\x80\x9c[n]otwithstanding any other provision of law with respect to limitations on expenditures or limitations on contributions,\xe2\x80\x9d political parties may make \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditures\xe2\x80\x9d in support of their candidates under a formula keyed to the voting-age population of the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s home State or, in the case of a candidate for President, the voting-age population of the United States. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441a(d)(1)\xe2\x80\x93(3) (main ed. and Supp. 2003).[Footnote 94] In the year 2000, that formula permitted expenditures ranging from $33,780 to $67,650 for House of Representatives races, and from $67,650 to $1.6 million for Senate races. Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 439, n.\xc2\xa03. We held in Colorado I that parties have a constitutional right to make unlimited independent expenditures, and we invalidated \xc2\xa7315(d) to the extent that it restricted such expenditures. As a result of that decision, \xc2\xa7315(d) applies only to coordinated expenditures, replacing the $5,000 cap on contributions set out in \xc2\xa7315(a)(2) with the more generous limitations prescribed by \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7315(d)(1)\xe2\x80\x93(3). We sustained that limited application in Colorado II, supra.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 213 of BCRA amends \xc2\xa7315(d) by adding a new paragraph (4). New \xc2\xa7315(d)(4)(A) provides that, after a party nominates a candidate for federal office, it must choose between two spending options. Under the first option, a party that \xe2\x80\x9cmakes any independent expenditure (as defined in section [301(17)])\xe2\x80\x9d is thereby barred from making \xe2\x80\x9cany coordinated expenditure under this subsection.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(d)(4)(A)(i) (Supp. 2003). The phrase \xe2\x80\x9cthis subsection\xe2\x80\x9d is a reference to subsection (d) of \xc2\xa7315. Thus, the consequence of making an independent expenditure is not a complete prohibition of any coordinated expenditure: Although the party cannot take advantage of the increased spending limits under \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7315(d)(1)\xe2\x80\x93(3), it still may make up to $5,000 in coordinated expenditures under \xc2\xa7315(a)(2). As the difference between $5,000 and $1.6 million demonstrates, however, that is a significant cost to impose on the exercise of a constitutional right.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The second option is the converse of the first. It provides that a party that makes any coordinated expenditure \xe2\x80\x9cunder this subsection\xe2\x80\x9d (i.e., one that exceeds the ordinary $5,000 limit) cannot make \xe2\x80\x9cany independent expenditure (as defined in section [301(17)]) with respect to the candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(d)(4)(A)(ii). Section 301(17) defines \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98independent expenditure\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to mean a non-coordinated expenditure \xe2\x80\x9cexpressly advocating the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7431(17)(A).[Footnote 95] Therefore, as was true of the first option, the party\xe2\x80\x99s choice is not as stark as it initially appears: The consequence of the larger coordinated expenditure is not a complete prohibition of any independent expenditure, but the forfeiture of the right to make independent expenditures for express advocacy. As we explained in our discussion of the provisions relating to electioneering communications, supra, at 83\xe2\x80\x9387, express advocacy represents only a tiny fraction of the political communications made for the purpose of electing or defeating candidates during a campaign. Regardless of which option parties choose, they remain free to make independent expenditures for the vast majority of campaign ads that avoid the use of a few magic words.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, the coverage of new FECA \xc2\xa7315(d)(4) is much more limited than it initially appears. A party that wishes to spend more than $5,000 in coordination with its nominee is forced to forgo only the narrow category of independent expenditures that make use of magic words. But while the category of burdened speech is relatively small, it plainly is entitled to First Amendment protection. See Buckley, 424 U. S., at 44\xe2\x80\x9345, 48. Under \xc2\xa7315(d)(4), a political party\xe2\x80\x99s exercise of its constitutionally protected right to engage in \xe2\x80\x9ccore First Amendment expression,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 48, results in the loss of a valuable statutory benefit that has been available to parties for many years. To survive constitutional scrutiny, a provision that has such consequences must be supported by a meaningful governmental interest.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The interest in requiring political parties to avoid the use of magic words is not such an interest. We held in Buckley that a $1,000 cap on expenditures that applied only to express advocacy could not be justified as a means of avoiding circumvention of contribution limits or preventing corruption and the appearance of corruption because its restrictions could easily be evaded: \xe2\x80\x9cSo long as persons and groups eschew expenditures that in express terms advocate the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate, they are free to spend as much as they want to promote the candidate and his views.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 45. The same is true in this litigation. Any claim that a restriction on independent express advocacy serves a strong Government interest is belied by the overwhelming evidence that the line between express advocacy and other types of election-influencing expression is, for Congress\xe2\x80\x99 purposes, functionally meaningless. Indeed, Congress enacted the new \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication[s]\xe2\x80\x9d provisions precisely because it recognized that the express advocacy test was woefully inadequate at capturing communications designed to influence candidate elections. In light of that recognition, we are hard pressed to conclude that any meaningful purpose is served by \xc2\xa7315(d)(4)\xe2\x80\x99s burden on a party\xe2\x80\x99s right to engage independently in express advocacy.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government argues that \xc2\xa7315(d)(4) nevertheless is constitutional because it is not an outright ban (or cap) on independent expenditures, but rather offers parties a voluntary choice between a constitutional right and a statutory benefit. Whatever merit that argument might have in the abstract, it fails to account for new \xc2\xa7315(d)(4)(B), which provides:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cFor purposes of this paragraph, all political committees established and maintained by a national political party (including all congressional campaign committees) and all political committees established and maintained by a State political party (including any subordinate committee of a State committee) shall be considered to be a single political committee.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(d)(4)(B) (Supp. 2003).

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Given that provision, it simply is not the case that each party committee can make a voluntary and independent choice between exercising its right to engage in independent advocacy and taking advantage of the increased limits on coordinated spending under \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7315(d)(1)\xe2\x80\x93(3). Instead, the decision resides solely in the hands of the first mover, such that a local party committee can bind both the state and national parties to its chosen spending option.[Footnote 96] It is one thing to say that Congress may require a party committee to give up its right to make independent expenditures if it believes that it can accomplish more with coordinated expenditures. It is quite another thing, however, to say that the RNC must limit itself to $5,000 in coordinated expenditures in support of its presidential nominee if any state or local committee first makes an independent expenditure for an ad that uses magic words. That odd result undermines any claim that new \xc2\xa7315(d)(4) can withstand constitutional scrutiny simply because it is cast as a voluntary choice rather than an outright prohibition on independent expenditures.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The portion of the judgment of the District Court invalidating BCRA \xc2\xa7213 is affirmed.

\n

BCRA \xc2\xa7214\xe2\x80\x99s Changes in FECA\xe2\x80\x99s Provisions Covering Coordinated Expenditures

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Ever since our decision in Buckley, it has been settled that expenditures by a noncandidate that are \xe2\x80\x9ccontrolled by or coordinated with the candidate and his campaign\xe2\x80\x9d may be treated as indirect contributions subject to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s source and amount limitations. 424 U. S., at 46. Thus, FECA \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(i) long has provided that \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditures made by any person in cooperation, consultation, or concert, with, or at the request or suggestion of, a candidate, his authorized political committees, or their agents, shall be considered to be a contribution to such candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(a)(7)(B)(i) (Supp. 2003). Section 214(a) of BCRA creates a new FECA \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(ii) that applies the same rule to expenditures coordinated with \xe2\x80\x9ca national, State, or local committee of a political party.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(a)(7)(B)(ii).[Footnote 97] Sections 214(b) and (c) direct the FEC to repeal its current regulations[Footnote 98] and to promulgate new regulations dealing with \xe2\x80\x9ccoordinated communications\xe2\x80\x9d paid for by persons other than candidates or their parties. Subsection (c) provides that the new \xe2\x80\x9cregulations shall not require agreement or formal collaboration to establish coordination.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(a) note.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs do not dispute that Congress may apply the same coordination rules to parties as to candidates. They argue instead that new FECA \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(ii) and its implementing regulations are overbroad and unconstitutionally vague because they permit a finding of coordination even in the absence of an agreement. Plaintiffs point out that political supporters may be subjected to criminal liability if they exceed the contribution limits with expenditures that ultimately are deemed coordinated. Thus, they stress the importance of a clear definition of \xe2\x80\x9ccoordination\xe2\x80\x9d and argue any definition that does not hinge on the presence of an agreement cannot provide the \xe2\x80\x9cprecise guidance\xe2\x80\x9d that the First Amendment demands. Brief for Chamber of Commerce of the United States et\xc2\xa0al., Appellant in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931756, p. 48. As plaintiffs readily admit, that argument reaches beyond BCRA, calling into question FECA\xe2\x80\x99s pre-existing provisions governing expenditures coordinated with candidates.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We are not persuaded that the presence of an agreement marks the dividing line between expenditures that are coordinated\xe2\x80\x94and therefore may be regulated as indirect contributions\xe2\x80\x94and expenditures that truly are independent. We repeatedly have struck down limitations on expenditures \xe2\x80\x9cmade totally independently of the candidate and his campaign,\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 47, on the ground that such limitations \xe2\x80\x9cimpose far greater restraints on the freedom of speech and association\xe2\x80\x9d than do limits on contributions and coordinated expenditures, id., at 44, while \xe2\x80\x9cfail[ing] to serve any substantial governmental interest in stemming the reality or appearance of corruption in the electoral process,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 47\xe2\x80\x9348. See also Colorado I, 518 U. S., at 613\xe2\x80\x93614 (striking down limit on expenditure made by party officials prior to nomination of candidates and without any consultation with potential nominees). We explained in Buckley:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cUnlike contributions, \xe2\x80\xa6 independent expenditures may well provide little assistance to the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s campaign and indeed may prove counterproductive. The absence of prearrangement and coordination of an expenditure with the candidate or his agent not only undermines the value of the expenditure to the candidate, but also alleviates the danger that expenditures will be given as a quid pro quo for improper commitments from the candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 47.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Thus, the rationale for affording special protection to wholly independent expenditures has nothing to do with the absence of an agreement and everything to do with the functional consequences of different types of expenditures. Independent expenditures \xe2\x80\x9care poor sources of leverage for a spender because they might be duplicative or counterproductive from a candidate\xe2\x80\x99s point of view.\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 446. By contrast, expenditures made after a \xe2\x80\x9cwink or nod\xe2\x80\x9d often will be \xe2\x80\x9cas useful to the candidate as cash.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 442, 446. For that reason, Congress has always treated expenditures made \xe2\x80\x9cat the request or suggestion of\xe2\x80\x9d a candidate as coordinated.[Footnote 99] 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(a)(7)(B)(i) (Supp. 2003). A supporter easily could comply with a candidate\xe2\x80\x99s request or suggestion without first agreeing to do so, and the resulting expenditure would be \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98virtually indistinguishable from [a] simple contributio[n],\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado II, supra, at 444\xe2\x80\x93445. Therefore, we cannot agree with the submission that new FECA \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(ii) is overbroad because it permits a finding of coordination or cooperation notwithstanding the absence of a pre-existing agreement.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nor are we persuaded that the absence of an agreement requirement renders \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(ii) unconstitutionally vague. An agreement has never been required to support a finding of coordination with a candidate under \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(i), which refers to expenditures made \xe2\x80\x9cin cooperation, consultation, or concer[t] with, or at the request or suggestion of\xe2\x80\x9d a candidate. Congress used precisely the same language in new \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(ii) to address expenditures coordinated with parties. FECA\xe2\x80\x99s longstanding definition of coordination \xe2\x80\x9cdelineates its reach in words of common understanding.\xe2\x80\x9d Cameron v. Johnson, 390 U. S. 611, 616 (1968). Not surprisingly, therefore, the relevant statutory language has survived without constitutional challenge for almost three decades. Although that fact does not insulate the definition from constitutional scrutiny, it does undermine plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 claim that the language of \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(ii) is intolerably vague. Plaintiffs do not present any evidence that the definition has chilled political speech, whether between candidates and their supporters or by the supporters to the general public. See Reno v. American Civil Liberties Union, 521 U. S. 844, 874 (1997) (noting risk that vague statutes may chill protected expression). And, although plaintiffs speculate that the FEC could engage in intrusive and politically motivated investigations into alleged coordination, they do not even attempt to explain why an agreement requirement would solve that problem. Moreover, the only evidence plaintiffs have adduced regarding the enforcement of the coordination provision during its 27-year history concerns three investigations in the late 1990\xe2\x80\x99s into groups on different sides of the political aisle. Such meager evidence does not support the claim that \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(ii) will \xe2\x80\x9cfoster \xe2\x80\x98arbitrary and discriminatory application.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, supra, at 41, n. 48 (quoting Grayned v. City of Rockford, 408 U. S., at 108\xe2\x80\x93109). We conclude that FECA\xe2\x80\x99s definition of coordination gives \xe2\x80\x9cfair notice to those to whom [it] is directed,\xe2\x80\x9d American Communications Assn. v. Douds, 339 U. S. 382, 412 (1950), and is not unconstitutionally vague.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, portions of plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenge to BCRA \xc2\xa7214 focus on the regulations the FEC has promulgated under \xc2\xa7214(c). 11 CFR \xc2\xa7109.21 (2003). As the District Court explained, issues concerning the regulations are not appropriately raised in this facial challenge to BCRA, but must be pursued in a separate proceeding. Thus, we agree with the District Court that plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenge to \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7214(b) and (c) is not ripe to the extent that the alleged constitutional infirmities are found in the implementing regulations rather than the statute itself.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The portions of the District Court judgment rejecting plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 challenges to BCRA \xc2\xa7214 are affirmed.

\n

V

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Many years ago we observed that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o say that Congress is without power to pass appropriate legislation to safeguard \xe2\x80\xa6 an election from the improper use of money to influence the result is to deny to the nation in a vital particular the power of self protection.\xe2\x80\x9d Burroughs v. United States, 290 U. S., at 545. We abide by that conviction in considering Congress\xe2\x80\x99 most recent effort to confine the ill effects of aggregated wealth on our political system. We are under no illusion that BCRA will be the last congressional statement on the matter. Money, like water, will always find an outlet. What problems will arise, and how Congress will respond, are concerns for another day. In the main we uphold BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s two principal, complementary features: the control of soft money and the regulation of electioneering communications. Accordingly, we affirm in part and reverse in part the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s judgment with respect to Titles I and II.

\n

It is so ordered.

\n

*\xc2\xa0Justice Souter, Justice Ginsburg, and Justice Breyer join this opinion in its entirety.

\nFootnote 5

\xc2\xa0The Court of Appeals found:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cLarge contributions are intended to, and do, gain access to the elected official after the campaign for consideration of the contributor\xe2\x80\x99s particular concerns. Senator Mathias not only describes this but also the corollary, that the feeling that big contributors gain special treatment produces a reaction that the average American has no significant role in the political process.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 519 F.\xc2\xa02d, at 838 (footnotes omitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The court also noted:

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCongress found and the District Court confirmed that such contributions were often made for the purpose of furthering business or private interests by facilitating access to government officials or influencing governmental decisions, and that, conversely, elected officials have tended to afford special treatment to large contributors. See S. Rep. No. 93\xe2\x80\x93689, 93d Cong., 2d Sess. 4\xe2\x80\x935; Findings I, \xc2\xb6 ;\xc2\xb6 ;108, 110, 118, 170.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 838, n. 32.

\n

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe disclosures of illegal corporate contributions in 1972 included the testimony of executives that they were motivated by the perception that this was necessary as a \xe2\x80\x98calling card, something that would get us in the door and make our point of view heard,\xe2\x80\x99 Hearings before the Senate Select Comm. on Presidential Campaign Activities, 93d Cong., 1st Sess. 5442 (1973) (Ashland Oil Co.\xe2\x80\x94Orin Atkins, Chairman) or \xe2\x80\x98in response to pressure for fear of a competitive disadvantage that might result,\xe2\x80\x99 id. at 5495, 5514 (American Airlines\xe2\x80\x94George Spater, former chairman); see Findings I, \xc2\xb6 ;105. The record before Congress was replete with specific examples of improper attempts to obtain governmental favor in return for large campaign contributions. See Findings I, \xc2\xb6 ;\xc2\xb6 ;159\xe2\x80\x9364.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 839, n. 37.

\nFootnote 6

\xc2\xa0In 1977 the FEC promulgated a rule allowing parties to allocate their administrative expenses \xe2\x80\x9con a reasonable basis\xe2\x80\x9d between accounts containing funds raised in compliance with FECA and accounts containing nonfederal funds, including corporate and union donations. 11 CFR \xc2\xa7102.6(a)(2). In advisory opinions issued in 1978 and 1979, the FEC allowed parties similarly to allocate the costs of voter registration and get-out-the-vote drives between federal and nonfederal accounts. FEC Advisory Op. 1978\xe2\x80\x9310; FEC Advisory Op. 1979\xe2\x80\x9317. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 195\xe2\x80\x93197 (per curiam).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In 1990 the FEC clarified the phrase \xe2\x80\x9con a reasonable basis\xe2\x80\x9d by promulgating fixed allocation rates. 11 CFR \xc2\xa7106.5 (1991). The regulations required the Republican National Committee (RNC) and Democratic National Committee (DNC) to pay for at least 60% of mixed-purpose activities (65% in presidential election years) with funds from their federal accounts. \xc2\xa7106.5(b)(2). By contrast, the regulations required state and local committees to allocate similar expenditures based on the ratio of federal to nonfederal offices on the State\xe2\x80\x99s ballot, \xc2\xa7106.5(d)(1), which in practice meant that they could expend a substantially greater proportion of soft money than national parties to fund mixed-purpose activities affecting both federal and state elections. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 198\xe2\x80\x93199 (per curiam).

\nFootnote 39

\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy accuses us of engaging in a sleight of hand by conflating \xe2\x80\x9cunseemly corporate speech\xe2\x80\x9d with the speech of political parties and candidates, and then adverting to the \xe2\x80\x9ccorporate speech rationale as if it were the linchpin of the litigation.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 7 (opinion concurring in part and dissenting in part). This is incorrect. The principles set forth here and relied upon in assessing Title I are the same principles articulated in Buckley and its progeny that regulations of contributions to candidates, parties, and political committees are subject to less rigorous scrutiny than direct restraints on speech\xe2\x80\x94including \xe2\x80\x9cunseemly corporate speech.\xe2\x80\x9d

\nFootnote 40

\xc2\xa0Since our decision in Buckley, we have consistently applied less rigorous scrutiny to contribution restrictions aimed at the prevention of corruption and the appearance of corruption. See, e.g., 424 U. S., at 23\xe2\x80\x9336 (applying less rigorous scrutiny to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $1,000 limit on individual contributions to a candidate and FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $5,000 limit on PAC contributions to a candidate); id., at 38 (applying less rigorous scrutiny to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $25,000 aggregate yearly limit on contributions to candidates, political party committees, and political committees); California Medical Assn. v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 453 U. S. 182, 195\xe2\x80\x93196 (1981) (plurality opinion) (applying less rigorous scrutiny to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $5,000 limit on contributions to multicandidate political committees); National Right to Work, 459 U. S., at 208\xe2\x80\x93211 (applying less rigorous scrutiny to antisolicitation provision buttressing an otherwise valid contribution limit); Colorado II, 533 U. S. 431, 456 (2001) (applying less rigorous scrutiny to expenditures coordinated with a candidate); Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Beaumont, 539 U. S.___, ___ (2003) (slip op., at 14\xe2\x80\x9315) (applying less rigorous scrutiny to provisions intended to prevent circumvention of otherwise valid contribution limits).

\nFootnote 41

\xc2\xa0Indeed, Congress structured \xc2\xa7323(b) in such a way as to free individual, corporate, and union donations to state committees for nonfederal elections from federal source and amount restrictions.

\nFootnote 42

\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s contention that less rigorous scrutiny applies only to regulations burdening political association, rather than political speech, misreads Buckley. In Buckley, we recognized that contribution limits burden both protected speech and association, though they generally have more significant impacts on the latter. 424 U. S., at 20\xe2\x80\x9322. We nevertheless applied less rigorous scrutiny to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limits because neither burden was sufficiently weighty to overcome Congress\xe2\x80\x99 countervailing interest in protecting the integrity of the political process. See Nixon v. Shrink Missouri Government PAC, 528 U. S. 377, 388 (2000) (\xe2\x80\x9cWhile we did not [in Buckley] attempt to parse [the] distinctions between the speech and association standards of scrutiny for contribution limits, we did make it clear that those restrictions bore more heavily on the associational right than on [the] freedom to speak. We consequently proceeded on the understanding that a contribution limitation surviving a claim of associational abridgment would survive a speech challenge as well, and we held the standard satisfied by the contribution limits under review.\xe2\x80\x9d (citation omitted)). It is thus simply untrue in the campaign finance context that all \xe2\x80\x9cburdens on speech necessitate strict scrutiny review.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 29.

\nFootnote 43

\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy is no doubt correct that the associational burdens imposed by a particular piece of campaign-finance regulation may at times be so severe as to warrant strict scrutiny. Ibid. In light of our interpretation of \xc2\xa7323(a), however, see infra, at 46\xe2\x80\x9347, \xc2\xa7323 does not present such a case. As Justice Kennedy himself acknowledges, even \xe2\x80\x9csignificant interference\xe2\x80\x9d with \xe2\x80\x9cprotected rights of association\xe2\x80\x9d are subject to less rigorous scrutiny. Beaumont, 539 U. S., at _____ (slip op., at 15); see post, at 28. There is thus nothing inconsistent in our decision to account for the particular associational burdens imposed by \xc2\xa7323(a) when applying the appropriate level of scrutiny.

See J. Cantor, Congressional Research Service Report for Congress: Campaign Finance Legislation in the 101st Congress (1990) (9 bills seeking to limit the influence of soft money introduced); J. \nFootnote 45

\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy contends that the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s observation in Colorado I that large soft-money donations to a political party pose little threat of corruption \xe2\x80\x9cestablish[es] that\xe2\x80\x9d such contributions are not corrupting. \nFootnote 48

\xc2\xa0In addition to finding no support in our recent cases, see, e.g., Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 441 (defining corruption more broadly than quid pro quo arrangements); Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 389 (same), Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s contention that Buckley limits Congress to regulating contributions to a candidate ignores Buckley itself. There, we upheld FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $25,000 limit on aggregate yearly contributions to candidates, political committees, and party committees out of recognition that FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $1,000 limit on candidate contributions would be meaningless if individuals could instead make \xe2\x80\x9chuge contributions to the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s political party.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 38. Likewise, in California Medical Assn. v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 453 U. S. 182 (1981), we upheld FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $5,000 limit on contributions to multicandidate political committees. It is no answer to say that such limits were justified as a means of preventing individuals from using parties and political committees as pass-throughs to circumvent FECA\xe2\x80\x99s $1,000 limit on individual contributions to candidates. Given FECA\xe2\x80\x99s definition of \xe2\x80\x9ccontribution,\xe2\x80\x9d the $5,000 and $25,000 limits restricted not only the source and amount of funds available to parties and political committees to make candidate contributions, but also the source and amount of funds available to engage in express advocacy and numerous other noncoordinated expenditures. If indeed the First Amendment prohibited Congress from regulating contributions to fund the latter, the otherwise-easy-to-remedy exploitation of parties as pass-throughs (e.g., a strict limit on donations that could be used to fund candidate contributions) would have provided insufficient justification for such overbroad legislation.

\nFootnote 49

\xc2\xa0At another point, describing our \xe2\x80\x9cflawed reasoning,\xe2\x80\x9d Justice Kennedy seems to suggest that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 interest in regulating the appearance of corruption extends only to those contributions that actually \xe2\x80\x9ccreate \xe2\x80\xa6 corrupt donor favoritism among \xe2\x80\xa6 officeholders.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 16. This latter formulation would render Congress\xe2\x80\x99 interest in stemming the appearance of corruption indistinguishable from its interest in preventing actual corruption.

\nFootnote 50

\xc2\xa0In support of this claim, the political party plaintiffs assert that, in 2001, the RNC spent $15.6 million of nonfederal funds (30% of the nonfederal amount raised that year) on purely state and local election activity, including contributions to state and local candidates, transfers to state parties, and direct spending. See Tr. of Oral Arg. 102\xe2\x80\x93103 (statement of counsel Bobby R. Burchfield); 251 F. Supp. 2d, at 336\xe2\x80\x93337 (Henderson, J.); id., at 464\xe2\x80\x93465 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 830 (Leon, J.).

\nFootnote 51

\xc2\xa0The close relationship of federal officeholders and candidates to their parties answers not only The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s concerns about \xc2\xa7323(a), but also his fear that our analysis of \xc2\xa7323\xe2\x80\x99s remaining provisions bespeaks no limiting principle. Post, at 6\xe2\x80\x937 (dissenting opinion). As set forth in our discussion of those provisions, the record demonstrates close ties between federal officeholders and the state and local committees of their parties. That close relationship makes state and local parties effective conduits for donors desiring to corrupt federal candidates and officeholders. Thus, in upholding \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(b), (d), and (f), we rely not only on the fact that they regulate contributions used to fund activities influencing federal elections, but also that they regulate contributions to or at the behest of entities uniquely positioned to serve as conduits for corruption. We agree with The Chief Justice that Congress could not regulate financial contributions to political talk show hosts or newspaper editors on the sole basis that their activities conferred a benefit on the candidate. Post, at 7 (dissenting opinion).

\nFootnote 52

\xc2\xa0Plaintiffs claim that the option of soliciting hard money for state and local candidates is an illusory one, since several States prohibit state and local candidates from establishing multiple campaign accounts, which would preclude them from establishing separate accounts for federal funds. See Cal. Fair Pol. Practs. Comm\xe2\x80\x99n Advisory Op. A\xe2\x80\x9391\xe2\x80\x93448 (Dec. 16, 1991), 1991 WL 772902; Colo. Const., Art. XXVIII, \xc2\xa72(3); Iowa Code \xc2\xa756.5A (Supp. 2003); and Ohio Rev. Code Ann. \xc2\xa73517.10(J) (Anderson Supp. 2002). Plaintiffs maintain that \xc2\xa7323(a) combines with these state laws to make it impossible for state and local candidates to receive hard-money donations. But the challenge we are considering is a facial one, and on its face \xc2\xa7323(a) permits solicitations. The fact that a handful of States might interfere with the mechanism Congress has chosen for such solicitations is an argument that may be addressed in an as-applied challenge.

Burchfield, Partner, Covington & Burling).

The national parties know it; the candidates know it; the state and local parties know it. If state and local parties can use soft money for activities that affect federal elections, then the problem will not be solved at all. The same enormous incentives to raise the money will exist; the same large contributions by corporations, unions, and wealthy individuals will be made; the federal candidates who benefit from state party use of these funds will know exactly whom their benefactors are; the same degree of beholdenness and obligation will arise; the same distortions on the legislative process will occur; and the same public cynicism will erode the foundations of our democracy\xe2\x80\x94except it will all be worse in the public\xe2\x80\x99s mind because a perceived reform was undercut once again by a loophole that allows big money into the system.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 467 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.) \nFootnote 63

\xc2\xa0With respect to GOTV, voter identification, and other generic campaign activity, the FEC has interpreted \xc2\xa7323(b) to apply only to those activities conducted after the earliest filing deadline for access to the federal election ballot or, in States that do not conduct primaries, after January 1 of even-numbered years. 11 CFR \xc2\xa7100.24(a)(1) (2002).
