\nFootnote 64

\xc2\xa0We likewise reject the argument that \xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(iii) is unconstitutionally vague. The words \xe2\x80\x9cpromote,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9coppose,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cattack,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9csupport\xe2\x80\x9d clearly set forth the confines within which potential party speakers must act in order to avoid triggering the provision. These words \xe2\x80\x9cprovide explicit standards for those who apply them\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cgive the person of ordinary intelligence a reasonable opportunity to know what is prohibited.\xe2\x80\x9d Grayned v. City of Rockford, 408 U. S. 104, 108\xe2\x80\x93109 (1972). This is particularly the case here, since actions taken by political parties are presumed to be in connection with election campaigns. See Buckley, 424 U. S., at 79 (noting that a general requirement that political committees disclose their expenditures raised no vagueness problems because the term \xe2\x80\x9cpolitical committee\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cneed only encompass organizations that are under the control of a candidate or the major purpose of which is the nomination or election of a candidate\xe2\x80\x9d and thus a political committee\xe2\x80\x99s expenditures \xe2\x80\x9care, by definition, campaign related\xe2\x80\x9d). Furthermore, should plaintiffs feel that they need further guidance, they are able to seek advisory opinions for clarification, see 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7437f\xc2\xa0(a)(1), and thereby \xe2\x80\x9cremove any doubt there may be as to the meaning of the law,\xe2\x80\x9d Civil Service Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Letter Carriers, 413 U. S. 548, 580 (1973).

\xc2\xa0Section 323(e)(1)(B) tightly constrains the ability of federal candidates and officeholders to solicit or spend nonfederal money in connection with state or local elections. Contributions cannot exceed FECA\xe2\x80\x99s analogous hard-money contribution limits or come from prohibited sources. In effect, \xc2\xa7323(e)(1)(B) doubles the limits on what individuals can contribute to or at the behest of federal candidates and officeholders, while restricting the use of the additional funds to activities not related to federal elections. If the federal candidate or officeholder is also a candidate for state or local office, he or she may solicit, receive, and spend an unlimited amount of nonfederal money in connection with that election, subject only to state regulation and the requirement that such solicitation or expenditures refer only to the relevant state or local office. 2 U. \nFootnote 72

\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy faults our \xe2\x80\x9cunwillingness\xe2\x80\x9d to confront that \xe2\x80\x9cTitle I\xe2\x80\x99s entirety \xe2\x80\xa6 look[s] very much like an incumbency protection plan,\xe2\x80\x9d citing \xc2\xa7323(e), which provides officeholders and candidates with greater opportunities to solicit soft money than \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(a) and (d) permit party officers. Post, at 23\xe2\x80\x9324. But, \xc2\xa7323(e) applies to both officeholders and candidates and allows only minimally greater opportunities for solicitation out of regard for the fact that candidates and officeholders, unlike party officers, can never step out of their official roles. Supra, at 70\xe2\x80\x9371; 42 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(e). Any concern that Congress might opportunistically pass campaign-finance regulation for self-serving ends is taken into account by the applicable level of scrutiny. Congress must show concrete evidence that a particular type of financial transaction is corrupting or gives rise to the appearance of corruption and that the chosen means of regulation are closely drawn to address that real or apparent corruption. It has done so here. At bottom, Justice Kennedy has long disagreed with the basic holding of Buckley and its progeny that less rigorous scrutiny\xe2\x80\x94which shows a measure of deference to Congress in an area where it enjoys particular expertise\xe2\x80\x94applies to assess limits on campaign contributions. Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 465 (Thomas, J., dissenting) (joining Justice Thomas for the proposition that \xe2\x80\x9cBuckley should be overrruled\xe2\x80\x9d (citation omitted)); Shrink Missouri, 528 U. \nFootnote 87

\xc2\xa0The amendment is straightforward. Prior to BCRA, FECA \xc2\xa7316(a) made it \xe2\x80\x9cunlawful \xe2\x80\xa6 for any corporation whatever, or any labor organization, to make a contribution or expenditure in connection with\xe2\x80\x9d certain federal elections. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b(a) (2000 ed.). BCRA amends FECA \xc2\xa7316(b)(2)\xe2\x80\x99s definition of the term \xe2\x80\x9ccontribution or expenditure\xe2\x80\x9d to include \xe2\x80\x9cany applicable electioneering communication.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441b(b)(2) (Supp. 2003).

\nFootnote 88

\xc2\xa0As Justice Kennedy emphasizes in dissent, post, at 44\xe2\x80\x9345, we assume that the interests that justify the regulation of campaign speech might not apply to the regulation of genuine issue ads. The premise that apparently underlies Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s principal submission is a conclusion that the two categories of speech are nevertheless entitled to the same constitutional protection. If that is correct, Justice Kennedy must take issue with the basic holding in Buckley and, indeed, with our recognition in First Nat. Bank of Boston v. Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765 (1978), that unusually important interests underlie the regulation of corporations\xe2\x80\x99 campaign-related speech. In Bellotti we cited Buckley, among other cases, for the proposition that \xe2\x80\x9c[p]reserving the integrity of the electoral process, preventing corruption, and \xe2\x80\x98sustain[ing] the active, alert responsibility of the individual citizen in a democracy for the wise conduct of the government\xe2\x80\x99 are interests of the highest importance.\xe2\x80\x9d 435 U. S., at 788\xe2\x80\x93789 (citations and footnote omitted). \xe2\x80\x9cPreservation of the individual citizen\xe2\x80\x99s confidence in government,\xe2\x80\x9d we added, \xe2\x80\x9cis equally important.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 789. BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s fidelity to those imperatives sets it apart from the statute in Bellotti\xe2\x80\x94and, for that matter, from the Ohio statute banning the distribution of anonymous campaign literature, struck down in McIntyre v. Ohio Elections Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 514 U. S. 334 (1995).

\nFootnote 89

\xc2\xa0In a different but somewhat related argument, one set of plaintiffs contends that political campaigns and issue advocacy involve press activities, and that BCRA therefore interferes with speakers\xe2\x80\x99 rights under the Freedom of the Press Clause. U. S. Const., Amdt. 1. We affirm the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that this contention lacks merit.

\nFootnote 90

\xc2\xa0The statutory scheme is somewhat complex. In its provision dealing with \xe2\x80\x9cRules Relating to Electioneering Communications,\xe2\x80\x9d BCRA \xc2\xa7203(c)(2) (adding FECA \xc2\xa7316(c)(2)) makes a blanket exception for designated nonprofit organizations, which reads as follows:

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cException

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cNotwithstanding paragraph (1), the term \xe2\x80\x98applicable electioneering communication\xe2\x80\x99 does not include a communication by a section 501(c)(4) organization or a political organization (as defined in section 527(e)(1) of Title 26) made under section 434(f)(2)(E) or (F) of this title if the communication is paid for exclusively by funds provided directly by individuals who are United States citizens or nationals or lawfully admitted for permanent residence (as defined in section 1101(a)(20) of Title 8). For purposes of the preceding sentence, the term \xe2\x80\x98provided directly by individuals\xe2\x80\x99 does not include funds the source of which is an entity described in subsection (a) of this section.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441b(c)(2) (Supp. 2003).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7204, however, amends FECA \xc2\xa7316(c) to exclude \xe2\x80\x9ctargeted communications\xe2\x80\x9d from that exception. New FECA \xc2\xa7316(c)(6) states that the \xc2\xa7316(c)(2) exception \xe2\x80\x9cshall not apply in the case of a targeted communication that is made by an organization described\xe2\x80\x9d in \xc2\xa7316(b)(2). 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xb6 ;441b(c)(6)(A). Subparagraph (B) then defines the term \xe2\x80\x9ctargeted communication\xe2\x80\x9d for the purpose of the provision as including all electioneering communications. The parties and the judges on the District Court have assumed that amended FECA \xc2\xa7316(c)(6) completely canceled the exemption for nonprofit corporations set forth in \xc2\xa7316(c)(2). 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 804 (Leon, J.) (\xe2\x80\x9cSection 204 completely cancels out the exemption for all nonprofit corporations provided by Section 203\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 91

\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c[A] unanimous Court in National Right to Work did not think the regulatory burdens on PACs, including restrictions on their ability to solicit funds, rendered a PAC unconstitutional as an advocacy corporation\xe2\x80\x99s sole avenue for making political contributions. See 459 U. S., at 201\xe2\x80\x93202. There is no reason to think the burden on advocacy corporations is any greater today, or to reach a different conclusion here.\xe2\x80\x9d Beaumont, 539 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 16).

\nFootnote 92

S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434 (Supp. 2003).

\nFootnote 93

\xc2\xa0New FECA \xc2\xa7315(d)(4) reads as follows:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cIndependent versus coordinated expenditures by party

\n

\n\n \xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(A) In general

\n

\n

\n\n \xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(B) Application

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cFor purposes of this paragraph, all political committees established and maintained by a national political party (including all congressional campaign committees) and all political committees established and maintained by a State political party (including any subordinate committee of a State committee) shall be considered to be a single political committee.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(C) Transfers

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cA committee of a political party that makes coordinated expenditures under this subsection with respect to a candidate shall not, during an election cycle, transfer any funds to, assign authority to make coordinated expenditures under this subsection to, or receive a transfer of funds from, a committee of the political party that has made or intends to make an independent expenditure with respect to the candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(d)(4) (Supp. 2003).

\nFootnote 94

\xc2\xa0After exempting political parties from the general contribution and expenditure limitations of the statute, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(d)(1), FECA \xc2\xa7315(d) imposes the following substitute limitations on party spending:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(2) The national committee of a political party may not make any expenditure in connection with the general election campaign of any candidate for President of the United States who is affiliated with such party which exceeds an amount equal to 2 cents multiplied by the voting age population of the United States (as certified under subsection (e) of this section). \n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(3) The national committee of a political party, or a State committee of a political party, including any subordinate committee of a State committee, may not make any expenditure in connection with the general election campaign of a candidate for Federal office in a State who is affiliated with such party which exceeds\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\nFootnote 95

\xc2\xa0As amended by BCRA,\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa7301(17) provides:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cIndependent expenditure

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe term \xe2\x80\x98independent expenditure\xe2\x80\x99 means an expenditure by a person\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(B) that is not made in concert or cooperation with or at the request or suggestion of such candidate, the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s authorized political committee, or their agents, or a political party committee or its agents.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. \n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The version of the definition prior to its amendment by BCRA also included the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cexpressly advocating the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7431(17) (2000 ed.). That definition had been adopted in 1976, presumably to reflect the narrowing construction that the Court adopted in Buckley. Federal Election Campaign Act Amendments of 1976, 90 Stat. 475.

\nFootnote 96

\xc2\xa0Although the District Court and all the parties to this litigation endorse the interpretation set forth in the text, it is not clear that subparagraph (B) should be read so broadly: The reference to \xe2\x80\x9ca State\xe2\x80\x9d instead of \xe2\x80\x9cthe States\xe2\x80\x9d suggests that Congress meant to distinguish between committees associated with the party for each State (which would be grouped together by State, with each grouping treated as a single committee for purposes of the choice) and committees associated with a national party (which would likewise be grouped together and treated as a separate political committee). We need not resolve the interpretive puzzle, however, because even under the more limited reading a local party committee would be able to tie the hands of a state committee or other local committees in the same State.

\nFootnote 97

\xc2\xa0The italicized portion of the following partial quotation of FECA \xc2\xa7315(a)(7) was added by \xc2\xa7214 of BCRA:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cFor purposes of this subsection\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(A) contributions to a named candidate made to any political committee authorized by such candidate to accept contributions on his behalf shall be considered to be contributions made to such candidate;

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(B)(i) expenditures made by any person in cooperation, consultation, or concert, with, or at the request or suggestion of, a candidate, his authorized political committees, or their agents, shall be considered to be a contribution to such candidate;

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(ii) expenditures made by any person (other than a candidate or candidate\xe2\x80\x99s authorized committee) in cooperation, consultation, or concert with, or at the request or suggestion of, a national, State, or local committee of a political party, shall be considered to be contributions made to such party committee \xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(a)(7) (Supp. 2003).

\nFootnote 98

\xc2\xa0Pre-BCRA FEC regulations defined coordinated expenditures to include expenditures made \xe2\x80\x9c[a]t the request or suggestion of\xe2\x80\x9d a candidate or party; communications in which a candidate or party \xe2\x80\x9cexercised control or decision-making authority over the content, timing, location, mode, intended audience, volume of distribution, or frequency of placement\xe2\x80\x9d; and communications produced \xe2\x80\x9c[a]fter substantial discussion or negotiation\xe2\x80\x9d with a party or candidate, \xe2\x80\x9cthe result of which is collaboration or agreement.\xe2\x80\x9d 11 CFR \xc2\xa7100.23(c)(2) (2001).

\nFootnote 99

\n

on appeals from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[December 10, 2003]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to BCRA Title V.*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We consider here the constitutionality of \xc2\xa7504 of the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act of 2002 (BCRA), amending the Communications Act of 1934. That section requires broadcasters to keep publicly available records of politically related broadcasting requests. 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315(e) (Supp. 2003). The McConnell plaintiffs, who include the National Association of Broadcasters, argue that \xc2\xa7504 imposes onerous administrative burdens, lacks any offsetting justification, and consequently violates the First Amendment. For similar reasons, the three judges on the District Court found BCRA \xc2\xa7504 unconstitutional on its face. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 176, 186 (DC 2003) (per curiam) (case below). We disagree, and we reverse that determination.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7504\xe2\x80\x99s key requirements are the following:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(1) A \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d requirement calls for broadcasters to keep records of broadcast requests \xe2\x80\x9cmade by or on behalf of\xe2\x80\x9d any \xe2\x80\x9clegally qualified candidate for public office.\xe2\x80\x9d 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(A) (Supp. 2003).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(2) An \xe2\x80\x9celection message request\xe2\x80\x9d requirement calls for broadcasters to keep records of requests (made by anyone) to broadcast \xe2\x80\x9cmessage[s]\xe2\x80\x9d that refer either to a \xe2\x80\x9clegally qualified candidate\xe2\x80\x9d or to \xe2\x80\x9cany election to Federal office.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B)(i), (ii).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(3) An \xe2\x80\x9cissue request\xe2\x80\x9d requirement calls for broadcasters to keep records of requests (made by anyone) to broadcast \xe2\x80\x9cmessage[s]\xe2\x80\x9d related to a \xe2\x80\x9cnational legislative issue\nof public importance,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B)(iii), or otherwise relating to a \xe2\x80\x9cpolitical matter of national importance,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We shall consider each provision in turn.

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7504\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d requirements are virtually identical to those contained in a regulation that the Federal Communications Commission (FCC) promulgated as early as 1938 and which with slight modifications the FCC has maintained in effect ever since. 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1943 (2002); compare 3 Fed. Reg. 1692 (1938) (47 CFR \xc2\xa736a4); 13 Fed. Reg. 7486 (1948) (47 CFR \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa73.190(d), 3.290(d), 3.690(d)); 17 Fed. Reg. 4711 (1952) (47 CFR \xc2\xa73.590(d)); 19 Fed. Reg. 5949 (1954); 23 Fed. Reg. 7817 (1958); 28 Fed. Reg. 13593 (1963) (47 CFR \xc2\xa773.120(d));\n 43 Fed. Reg. 32795 (1978) (47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1940(d)); 57\n Fed. Reg. 210 (1992) (47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1943). See generally Brief in Opposition to Motion of Appellee National Association of Broadcasters for Summary Affirmance in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931676, pp.\xc2\xa09\xe2\x80\x9310 (hereinafter Brief Opposing Summary Affirmance).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In its current form the FCC regulation requires broadcast licensees to \xe2\x80\x9ckeep\xe2\x80\x9d a publicly available file \xe2\x80\x9cof all requests for broadcast time made by or on behalf of a candidate for public office,\xe2\x80\x9d along with a notation showing whether the request was granted, and (if granted) a history that includes \xe2\x80\x9cclasses of time,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9crates charged,\xe2\x80\x9d and when the \xe2\x80\x9cspots actually aired.\xe2\x80\x9d 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1943(a) (2002); \xc2\xa776.1701(a) (same for cable systems). These regulation-imposed requirements mirror the statutory requirements imposed by BCRA \xc2\xa7504 with minor differences which no one here challenges. Compare 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1943 with 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315(e)(2) (see Appendix, infra).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The McConnell plaintiffs argue that these requirements are \xe2\x80\x9cintolerabl[y]\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cburdensome and invasive.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Appellants/Cross-Appellees Senator Mitch McConnell et\xc2\xa0al. in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931674 et\xc2\xa0al., p. 74 (hereinafter Brief for McConnell Plaintiffs). But we do not see how that could be so. The FCC has consistently estimated that its \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d regulation imposes upon each licensee an additional administrative burden of six to seven hours of work per year. See 66 Fed. Reg. 37468 (2001); id., at 18090; 63 Fed. Reg. 26593 (1998); id., at 10379; 57 Fed. Reg. 18492 (1992); see also 66 Fed. Reg. 29963 (2001) (total annual burden of one hour per cable system). That burden means annual costs of a few hundred dollars at most, a microscopic amount compared to the many millions of dollars of revenue broadcasters receive from candidates who wish to advertise.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Perhaps for this reason, broadcasters in the past did not strongly oppose the regulation or its extension. Cf., e.g., 17 Fed. Reg. 4711 (1952) (\xe2\x80\x9cNo comments adverse to the adoption of the proposed rule have been received\xe2\x80\x9d); 43 Fed. Reg. 32794 (1978) (no adverse comments). Indeed in 1992, \xe2\x80\x9cCBS\xe2\x80\x9d itself \xe2\x80\x9csuggest[ed]\xe2\x80\x9d that the candidate file \xe2\x80\x9cinclude a record of all requests for time.\xe2\x80\x9d 57 Fed. Reg. 206 (1992); cf. 63 Fed. Reg. 49493 (1998) (FCC \xe2\x80\x9cnot persuaded that the current retention period [two years] is overly burdensome to licensees\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In any event, as the FCC wrote in an analogous context, broadcaster recordkeeping requirements \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98simply run with the territory.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 40 Fed. Reg. 18398 (1975). Broadcasters must keep and make publicly available numerous records. See 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.3526 (2002) (general description of select recordkeeping requirements for commercial stations); see also \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa773.1202, 73.3526(e)(9)(i) (retention of all \xe2\x80\x9cwritten comments and suggestions [including letters and e-mail] received from the public regarding operation of the station\xe2\x80\x9d for three years); \xc2\xa773.1212(e) (sponsorship identification records, including the identification of a sponsoring entity\xe2\x80\x99s executive officers and board-level members when sponsoring \xe2\x80\x9cpolitical matter or matter involving the discussion of a controversial issue of public importance\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa773.1840 (retention of station logs); \xc2\xa773.1942 (candidate broadcast records); \xc2\xa773.2080 (equal employment oppor-\ntunities records); \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa773.3526(e)(11)(i), (e)(12) (\xe2\x80\x9clist of\n programs that have provided the station\xe2\x80\x99s most significant treatment of community issues during the preceding three month period,\xe2\x80\x9d including \xe2\x80\x9cbrief narrative describing [the issues, and] time, date, duration, and title\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa773.3526(e)(11)(ii), (iii) (reports of children\xe2\x80\x99s program, and retention of records sufficient to substantiate \xe2\x80\x9ccompliance with the commercial limits on children\xe2\x80\x99s programming\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa773.3613(a) (network affiliation contracts); \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa773.3613(b), 73.3615, 73.3526(e)(5) (ownership-related reports); \xc2\xa773.3613(c) (\xe2\x80\x9c[m]anagement consultant agreements\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa773.3613(d) (\xe2\x80\x9c[t]ime brokerage agreements\xe2\x80\x9d). Compared to these longstanding recordkeeping requirements, an additional six to seven hours is a small drop in a very large bucket.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The McConnell plaintiffs also claim that the \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate requests\xe2\x80\x9d requirement fails significantly to further any important governmental interest. Brief for McConnell Plaintiffs 74. But, again, we cannot agree. The FCC has pointed out that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]hese records are necessary to permit political candidates and others to verify that licensees have complied with their obligations relating to use of their facilities by candidates for political office\xe2\x80\x9d pursuant to the \xe2\x80\x9cequal time\xe2\x80\x9d provision of 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7315(a). 63 Fed. Reg. 49493 (1998). They also help the FCC determine whether broadcasters have violated their obligation to sell candidates time at the \xe2\x80\x9clowest unit charge.\xe2\x80\x9d 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7315(b). As reinforced by BCRA, the \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d requirements will help the FCC, the Federal Election Commission, and \xe2\x80\x9cthe public to evaluate whether broadcasters are processing [candidate] requests in an evenhanded fashion,\xe2\x80\x9d Brief Opposing Summary Affirmance 9, thereby helping to assure broadcasting fairness. 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7315(a); Red Lion Broadcasting Co. v. FCC, 395 U. S. 367, 390 (1969). They will help make the public aware of how much money candidates may be prepared to spend on broadcast messages. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434 (main ed. and Supp. 2003); see ante, at 87\xe2\x80\x9393 (joint opinion of Stevens and O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, JJ.) (hereinafter joint opinion). And they will provide an independently compiled set of data for purposes of verifying candidates\xe2\x80\x99 compliance with the disclosure requirements and source limitations of BCRA and the Federal Election Campaign Act of 1971. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434; cf. Adventure Communications, Inc. v. Kentucky Registry of Election Finance, 191 F.\xc2\xa03d 429, 433 (CA4 1999) (candidate compliance verification); 63 Fed. Reg. 49493 (1998) (FCC finding record retention provision provides public with \xe2\x80\x9cnecessary and adequate access\xe2\x80\x9d).\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We note, too, that the FCC\xe2\x80\x99s regulatory authority is broad. Red Lion, supra, at 380 (\xe2\x80\x9cbroad\xe2\x80\x9d mandate to assure broadcasters operate in public interest); National Broadcasting Co. v. United States, 319 U. S. 190, 219 (1943) (same). And we have previously found broad governmental authority for agency information demands from regulated entities. Compare United States v. Morton Salt Co., 338 U. S. 632, 642\xe2\x80\x93643 (1950); Oklahoma Press Publishing Co. v. Walling, 327 U. S. 186, 209 (1946); Donovan v. Lone Steer, Inc., 464 U. S. 408, 414\xe2\x80\x93415 (1984). \xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice suggests that the Government has not made these particular claims. But it has\xe2\x80\x94though succinctly\xe2\x80\x94for it has cross-referenced the relevant regulatory rules. Compare post, at 12\xe2\x80\x9313 (opinion of Rehnquist, C. J.), with Brief Opposing Summary Affirmance; Brief for McConnell Plaintiffs 73\xe2\x80\x9374; Brief for FEC et\xc2\xa0al. in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931674 et\xc2\xa0al., pp. 132\xe2\x80\x93133. And succinctness through cross-reference was necessary given our procedural requirement that the Government set forth in a 140-page brief all its arguments concerning each of the 20 BCRA provisions here under contest. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 186\xe2\x80\x93188.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, given the Government\xe2\x80\x99s reference to the 65-year-old FCC regulation and the related considerations we have mentioned, we cannot accept the argument that the constitutionality of the \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d provision lacks evidentiary support. The challengers have made no attempt to explain away the FCC\xe2\x80\x99s own contrary conclusions and the mass of evidence in related FCC records and proceedings. E.g., 57 Fed. Reg. 189 (1992); cf. supra, at 4\xe2\x80\x935; ante, at 117\xe2\x80\x93118 (joint opinion) (upholding BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s coordination provision based, in part, on prior experience under similar provision). Because we cannot, on the present record, find the longstanding FCC regulation unconstitutional, we likewise cannot strike down the \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d provision in BCRA \xc2\xa7504; for the latter simply embodies the regulation in a statute, thereby blocking any agency attempt to repeal it.

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7504\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9celection message request\xe2\x80\x9d requirements call for broadcasters to keep records of requests (made by any member of the public) to broadcast a \xe2\x80\x9cmessage\xe2\x80\x9d about \xe2\x80\x9ca legally qualified candidate\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cany election to Federal office.\xe2\x80\x9d 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B)(i), (ii) (Supp. 2003). Although these requirements are somewhat broader than the \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d requirement, they serve much the same purposes. A candidate\xe2\x80\x99s supporters or opponents account for many of the requests to broadcast \xe2\x80\x9cmessage[s]\xe2\x80\x9d about a \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate.\xe2\x80\x9d Requests to broadcast messages about an \xe2\x80\x9celection\xe2\x80\x9d may include messages that favor one candidate or another, along with other messages that may be more neutral.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Given the nature of many of the messages, recordkeeping can help both the regulatory agencies and the public evaluate broadcasting fairness, and determine the amount of money that individuals or groups, supporters or opponents, intend to spend to help elect a particular candidate. Cf. ante, at 100\xe2\x80\x93101 (joint opinion) (upholding stringent restrictions on all election-time advertising that refers to a candidate because such advertising will often convey message of support or opposition). Insofar as the request is to broadcast neutral material about a candidate or election, the disclosure can help the FCC carry out other statutory functions, for example, determining whether a broadcasting station is fulfilling its licensing obligation to broadcast material important to the community and the public. 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7315(a) (\xe2\x80\x9cobligation \xe2\x80\xa6 to afford reasonable opportunity for the discussion of conflicting views on issues of public importance\xe2\x80\x9d); 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1910 (2002); \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa773.3526(e)(11)(i), (e)(12) (recordkeeping requirements for issues important to the community).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For reasons previously discussed, supra, at 4\xe2\x80\x935, and on the basis of the material presented, we cannot say that these requirements will impose disproportionate administrative burdens. They ask the broadcaster to keep information about the disposition of the request, and information identifying the individual or company requesting the broadcast time (name, address, contact information, or, if the requester is not an individual, the names of company officials). 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315(e)(2) (Supp. 2003). Insofar as the \xe2\x80\x9crequest\xe2\x80\x9d is made by a candidate\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9csupporters,\xe2\x80\x9d the \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d regulation apparently already requires broadcasters to keep such records. 43 Fed. Reg. 32794 (1978). Regardless, the information should prove readily available, for the individual requesting a broadcast must provide it to the broadcaster should the broadcaster accept the request. 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1212(e) (2002). And as we have previously pointed out, the recordkeeping requirements do not reach significantly beyond other FCC\nrecordkeeping rules, for example, those requiring broadcasting licensees to keep material showing com-\npliance with their license-related promises to broad-\ncast material on issues of public importance. See, e.g., \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa773.3526(e)(11)(i), (e)(12) (recordkeeping requirements for issues important to the community); supra, at 4\xe2\x80\x935 (collecting regulations); Office of Communication of United Church of Christ v. FCC, 707 F.\xc2\xa02d 1413, 1421\xe2\x80\x931422 (CADC 1983) (describing FCC rules, in force during 1960\xe2\x80\x931981, that required nonentertainment programming in 14 specific areas and mandated publicly available records detailing date, time, source, and description to substantiate compliance). If, as we have held, the \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d requirements are constitutional, supra, at 7, the \xe2\x80\x9celection message\xe2\x80\x9d requirements, which serve similar governmental interests and impose only a small incremental burden, must be constitutional as well.

\n

IV

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The \xe2\x80\x9cissue request\xe2\x80\x9d requirements call for broadcasters to keep records of requests (made by any member of the public) to broadcast \xe2\x80\x9cmessage[s]\xe2\x80\x9d about \xe2\x80\x9ca national legislative issue of public importance\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cany political matter of national importance.\xe2\x80\x9d 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B), (e)(1)(B)(iii) (Supp. 2003). These recordkeeping requirements seem likely to help the FCC determine whether broadcasters are carrying out their \xe2\x80\x9cobligations to afford reasonable opportunity for the discussion of conflicting views on issues of public importance,\xe2\x80\x9d 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1910 (2002), and whether broadcasters are too heavily favoring entertainment, and discriminating against broadcasts devoted to public affairs, see ibid.; 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C \xc2\xa7315(a); Red Lion, 395 U. S., at 380.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The McConnell plaintiffs claim that the statutory language\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cpolitical matter of national importance\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cnational legislative issue of public importance\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94is unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. Brief for McConnell Plaintiffs 74\xe2\x80\x9375. But that language is no more general than the language that Congress has used to impose other obligations upon broadcasters. Compare 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B) (Supp. 2003) (\xe2\x80\x9cpolitical matter of national importance\xe2\x80\x9d) and \xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B)(iii) (\xe2\x80\x9cnational legislative issue of public importance\xe2\x80\x9d) (both added by BCRA \xc2\xa7504), with 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7315(a) (\xe2\x80\x9cobligation \xe2\x80\xa6 to operate in the public interest\xe2\x80\x9d and to afford reasonable opportunity for discussion of \xe2\x80\x9cissues of public importance\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa7317(a)(2) (FCC disclosure requirements relating to any \xe2\x80\x9cpolitical program\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cdiscussion of any controversial issue\xe2\x80\x9d); cf. 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1212(e) (2002) (\xe2\x80\x9cpolitical matter or \xe2\x80\xa6 a controversial issue of public importance\xe2\x80\x9d); and 9 Fed. Reg. 14734 (1944) (\xe2\x80\x9cpublic controversial issues\xe2\x80\x9d); ante, at 117\xe2\x80\x93118 (joint opinion) (noting that the experience under longstanding regulations undermines claims of chilling effect). And that language is also roughly comparable to other language in BCRA that we uphold today. E.g., ante, at 61\xe2\x80\x9362, and n.\xc2\xa064 (joint opinion) (upholding 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7431(20)(A)(iii) (Supp. 2003) (\xe2\x80\x9cpublic communication that refers to a clearly identified candidate for Federal office \xe2\x80\xa6 and that promotes or supports a candidate for that office, or attacks or opposes a candidate for that office\xe2\x80\x9d)), ante, at 117\xe2\x80\x93118 (upholding 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441a(a)(7)(B)(ii) (Supp. 2003) (counting as coordinated disbursements that are made \xe2\x80\x9cin cooperation, consultation, or concert with, or at the request or suggestion of [a political party]\xe2\x80\x9d) against challenge and noting that an \xe2\x80\x9cagreement\xe2\x80\x9d is not necessary for precision).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Whether these requirements impose disproportionate administrative burdens is more difficult to say. On the one hand, the burdens are likely less heavy than many that other FCC regulations have imposed, for example, the burden of keeping and disclosing \xe2\x80\x9c[a]ll written comments and suggestions\xe2\x80\x9d received from the public, including every e-mail. 47 CFR \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa773.1202, 73.3526(e)(9) (2002); see also supra, at 4\xe2\x80\x935. On the other hand, the burdens are likely heavier than those imposed by BCRA \xc2\xa7504\xe2\x80\x99s other provisions, previously discussed.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The regulatory burden, in practice, will depend on how the FCC interprets and applies this provision. The FCC has adequate legal authority to write regulations that may limit, and make more specific, the provision\xe2\x80\x99s potential linguistic reach. 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7315(d). It has often ameliorated regulatory burdens by interpretation in the past, and there is no reason to believe it will not do so here. See 14 FCC Rcd. 4653, \xc2\xb6 ;25 (1999) (relaxing the recordkeeping requirements in respect to cable systems that serve fewer than 5,000 subscribers); 14 FCC Rcd. 11121, \xc2\xb6 ;\xc2\xb6 ;20\xe2\x80\x9322 (1999) (requiring candidates to inspect the political file at a station rather than requiring licensees to send out photocopies of the files to candidates upon telephone request). The parties remain free to challenge the provisions, as interpreted by the FCC in regulations, or as otherwise applied. Any such challenge will likely provide greater information about the provisions\xe2\x80\x99 justifications and administrative burdens. Without that additional information, we cannot now say that the burdens are so great, or the justifications so minimal, as to warrant finding the provisions unconstitutional on their face.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The McConnell plaintiffs and The Chief Justice make one final claim. They say that the \xe2\x80\x9cissue request\xe2\x80\x9d requirement will force them to disclose information that will reveal their political strategies to opponents, perhaps prior to a broadcast. See post, at 14\xe2\x80\x9315 (dissenting opinion). We are willing to assume that the Constitution includes some form of protection against premature disclosure of campaign strategy\xe2\x80\x94though, given the First Amendment interest in free and open discussion of campaign issues, we make this assumption purely for argument\xe2\x80\x99s sake. Nonetheless, even on that assumption we do not see how BCRA \xc2\xa7504 can be unconstitutional on its face.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For one thing, the statute requires disclosure of names, addresses, and the fact of a request; it does not require disclosure of substantive campaign content. See 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315(e)(2) (Supp. 2003). For another, the statutory words \xe2\x80\x9cas soon as possible,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7315(e)(3), would seem to permit FCC disclosure-timing rules that would avoid any premature disclosure that the Constitution itself would forbid. Further, the plaintiffs do not point to\xe2\x80\x94and our own research cannot find\xe2\x80\x94any specific indication of such a \xe2\x80\x9cstrategy-disclosure\xe2\x80\x9d problem arising during the past 65 years in respect to the existing FCC \xe2\x80\x9ccandidate request\xe2\x80\x9d requirement, where the strategic problem might be expected to be more acute. Finally, we today reject an analogous facial attack\xe2\x80\x94premised on speculations of \xe2\x80\x9cadvance disclosure\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94on a similar BCRA provision. See ante, at 94 (joint opinion). Thus, the \xe2\x80\x9cstrategy disclosure\xe2\x80\x9d argument does not show that BCRA \xc2\xa7504 is unconstitutional on its face, but the plaintiffs remain free to raise this argument when \xc2\xa7504 is applied.

\n

V

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice makes two important arguments in response to those we have set forth. First, he says that we \xe2\x80\x9capproac[h] \xc2\xa7504 almost exclusively from the perspective of the broadcast licensees, ignoring the interests of candidates and other purchasers, whose speech and association rights are affected.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 11 (dissenting opinion). The Chief Justice is certainly correct in emphasizing the importance of the speech interests of candidates and other potential speakers, but we have not ignored their First Amendment \xe2\x80\x9cperspective.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To the contrary, we have discussed the speakers\xe2\x80\x99 interests together with the broadcasters\xe2\x80\x99 interests because the two sets of interests substantially overlap. For example, the speakers\xe2\x80\x99 vagueness argument is no different from the broadcasters\xe2\x80\x99, and it fails for the same reasons, e.g., the fact that BCRA \xc2\xa7504\xe2\x80\x99s language is just as definite and precise as other language that we today uphold. See supra, at 10.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We have separately discussed the one and only speech-related claim advanced on behalf of candidates (or other speakers) that differs from the claims set forth by the broadcasters. See supra, at 11\xe2\x80\x9312. This is the claim that the statute\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements will require candidates to reveal their political strategies to opponents. We just said, and we now repeat, that BCRA \xc2\xa7504 can be applied, in a significant number of cases, without requiring any such political-strategy disclosure\xe2\x80\x94either because disclosure in many cases will not create any such risk or because the FCC may promulgate rules requiring disclosure only after any such risk disappears, or both.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Moreover, candidates (or other speakers) whom \xc2\xa7504 affects adversely in this way (or in other ways) remain free to challenge the lawfulness of FCC implementing regulations and to challenge the constitutionality of \xc2\xa7504 as applied. To find that the speech-related interests of candidates and others may be vindicated in an as-applied challenge is not to \xe2\x80\x9cignor[e]\xe2\x80\x9d those interests.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, The Chief Justice says that \xe2\x80\x9cthe Government, in its brief, proffers no interest whatever to support \xc2\xa7504 as a whole,\xe2\x80\x9d adding that the existence of \xe2\x80\x9cpre-existing unchallenged agency regulations imposing similar disclosure requirements\xe2\x80\x9d cannot \xe2\x80\x9ccompel the conclusion that \xc2\xa7504 is constitutional,\xe2\x80\x9d nor somehow \xe2\x80\x9crelieve the Government of its burden of advancing a constitutionally sufficient justification for \xc2\xa7504.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 12\xe2\x80\x9313 (dissenting opinion).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Again The Chief Justice is correct in saying that the mere existence of similar FCC regulation-imposed requirements\xe2\x80\x94even if unchallenged for at least 65 years\xe2\x80\x94cannot prove that those requirements are constitutional. But the existence of those regulations means that we must read beyond the briefs in this case before holding those requirements unconstitutional. Before evaluating the relevant burdens and justifications, we must at least become acquainted with the FCC\xe2\x80\x99s own view of the matter. We must follow the Government\xe2\x80\x99s regulation-related references to the relevant regulatory records, related FCC regulatory conclusions, and the FCC\xe2\x80\x99s enforcement experience. We must take into account, for example, the likelihood that the reason there is \xe2\x80\x9cnothing in the record that indicates licensees have treated purchasers unfairly,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 13 (Rehnquist, C.\xc2\xa0J., dissenting), is that for many decades similar FCC regulations have made that unfair treatment unlawful. And, if we are to avoid disrupting related agency law, we must evaluate what we find in agency records and related experience before holding this similar statutory provision unconstitutional on its face.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even a superficial examination of those relevant agency materials reveals strong supporting justifications, and a lack of significant administrative burdens. And any additional burden that the statute, viewed facially, imposes upon interests protected by the First Amendment seems slight compared to the strong enforcement-related interests that it serves. Given the FCC regulations and their history, the statutory requirements must survive a facial attack under any potentially applicable First Amendment standard, including that of heightened scrutiny.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0That is why the regulations are relevant. That is why the brevity of the Government\xe2\x80\x99s discussion here cannot be determinative. That is why we fear that The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s contrary view would lead us into an unfortunate\xe2\x80\x94and at present unjustified\xe2\x80\x94revolution in communications law. And that is why we disagree with his dissent.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The portion of the judgment of the District Court invalidating BCRA \xc2\xa7504 is reversed.

\n

\nIt is so ordered.

\n

APPENDIX TO OPINION OF THE COURT

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Title 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315(e) (Supp. 2003), as amended by BCRA \xc2\xa7504, provides:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cPolitical record

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(1) In general

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cA licensee shall maintain, and make available for public inspection, a complete record of a request to purchase broadcast time that\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(A) is made by or on behalf of a legally qualified candidate for public office; or

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(B) communicates a message relating to any political matter of national importance, including\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(i) a legally qualified candidate;

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(ii) any election to Federal office; or

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(iii) a national legislative issue of public importance.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(2) Contents of record

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cA record maintained under paragraph (1) shall contain information regarding\xe2\x80\x94

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(A) whether the request to purchase broadcast time is accepted or rejected by the licensee;

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(B) the rate charged for the broadcast time;

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(C) the date and time on which the communication is aired;

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(D) the class of time that is purchased;

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(E) the name of the candidate to which the communication refers and the office to which the candidate is seeking election, the election to which the communication refers, or the issue to which the communication refers (as applicable);

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(F) in the case of a request made by, or on behalf of, a candidate, the name of the candidate, the authorized committee of the candidate, and the treasurer of such committee; and

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(G) in the case of any other request, the name of the person purchasing the time, the name, address, and phone number of a contact person for such person, and a list of the chief executive officers or members of the executive committee or of the board of directors of such person.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(3) Time to maintain file

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe information required under this subsection shall be placed in a political file as soon as possible and shall be retained by the licensee for a period of not less than 2 years.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\nTitle 47 CFR \xc2\xa773.1943 (2002) provides:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cPolitical file.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(a) Every licensee shall keep and permit public inspection of a complete and orderly record (political file) of all requests for broadcast time made by or on behalf of a candidate for public office, together with an appropriate notation showing the disposition made by the licensee of such requests, and the charges made, if any, if the request is granted. The \xe2\x80\x98disposition\xe2\x80\x99 includes the schedule of time purchased, when spots actually aired, the rates charged, and the classes of time purchased.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(b) When free time is provided for use by or on behalf of candidates, a record of the free time provided shall be placed in the political file.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c(c) All records required by this paragraph shall be placed in the political file as soon as possible and\nshall be retained for a period of two years. As soon\nas possible means immediately absent unusual\ncircumstances.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

*Justice Stevens, Justice O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, Justice Souter, and Justice Ginsburg join this opinion in its entirety.

\n\n

on appeals from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[December 10, 2003]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Rehnquist, dissenting with respect to BCRA Titles I and V.*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Although I join Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s opinion in full, I write separately to highlight my disagreement with the Court on Title I of the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act of 2002 (BCRA), 116 Stat. 81, and to dissent from the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion upholding \xc2\xa7504 of Title V.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The issue presented by Title I is not, as the Court implies, whether Congress can permissibly regulate campaign contributions to candidates, de facto or otherwise, or seek to eliminate corruption in the political process. Rather, the issue is whether Congress can permissibly regulate much speech that has no plausible connection to candidate contributions or corruption to achieve those goals. Under our precedent, restrictions on political contributions implicate important First Amendment values and are constitutional only if they are \xe2\x80\x9cclosely drawn\xe2\x80\x9d to reduce the corruption of federal candidates or the appearance of corruption. Buckley v. Valeo, 424 U. S. 1, 26\xe2\x80\x93\n27 (1976) (per curiam). Yet, the Court glosses over the breadth of the restrictions, characterizing Title I of BCRA as \xe2\x80\x9cdo[ing] little more that regulat[ing] the ability of wealthy individuals, corporations, and unions to contribute large sums of money to influence federal elections, federal candidates, and federal officeholders.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 28 (joint opinion of Stevens and O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, JJ.). Because, in reality, Title I is much broader than the Court allows, regulating a good deal of speech that does not have the potential to corrupt federal candidates and officeholders, I dissent.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The lynchpin of Title I, new FECA \xc2\xa7323(a), prohibits national political party committees from \xe2\x80\x9csolicit[ing],\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9creceiv[ing],\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cdirect[ing] to another person,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cspend[ing]\xe2\x80\x9d any funds not subject to federal regulation, even if those funds are used for nonelection related activities. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(a)(1) (Supp. 2003). The Court concludes that such a restriction is justified because under FECA, \xe2\x80\x9cdonors have been free to contribute substantial sums of soft money to the national parties, which the parties can spend for the specific purpose of influencing a particular candidate\xe2\x80\x99s federal election.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 36. Accordingly, \xe2\x80\x9c[i]t is not only plausible, but likely, that candidates would feel grateful for such donations and that donors would seek to exploit that gratitude.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. But the Court misses the point. Certainly \xe2\x80\x9cinfusions of money into [candidates\xe2\x80\x99] campaigns,\xe2\x80\x9d Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. National Conservative Political Action Comm., 470 U. S. 480, 497 (1985), can be regulated, but \xc2\xa7323(a) does not regulate only donations given to influence a particular federal election; it regulates all donations to national political committees, no matter the use to which the funds are put.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court attempts to sidestep the unprecedented breadth of this regulation by stating that the \xe2\x80\x9cclose relationship between federal officeholders and the national parties\xe2\x80\x9d makes all donations to the national parties \xe2\x80\x9csuspect.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 45. But a close association with others, especially in the realm of political speech, is not a surrogate for corruption; it is one of our most treasured First Amendment rights. See California Democratic Party v. Jones, 530 U. S. 567, 574 (2000); Eu v. San Francisco County Democratic Central Comm., 489 U. S. 214, 225 (1989); Tashjian v. Republican Party of Conn., 479 U. S. 208, 214 (1986). The Court\xe2\x80\x99s willingness to impute corruption on the basis of a relationship greatly infringes associational rights and expands Congress\xe2\x80\x99 ability to regulate political speech. And there is nothing in the Court\xe2\x80\x99s analysis that limits congressional regulation to national political parties. In fact, the Court relies in part on this closeness rationale to regulate nonprofit organizations. Ante, at 47\xe2\x80\x9348, n.\xc2\xa051. Who knows what association will be deemed too close to federal officeholders next. When a donation to an organization has no potential to corrupt a federal officeholder, the relationship between the officeholder and the organization is simply irrelevant.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court fails to recognize that the national political parties are exemplars of political speech at all levels of government, in addition to effective fundraisers for federal candidates and officeholders. For sure, national political party committees exist in large part to elect federal candidates, but as a majority of the District Court found, they also promote coordinated political messages and participate in public policy debates unrelated to federal elections, promote, even in off-year elections, state and local candidates and seek to influence policy at those levels, and increase public participation in the electoral process. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 176, 334\xe2\x80\x93337 (DC 2003) (per curiam) (Henderson, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part); id., at 820\xe2\x80\x93821 (Leon, J.). Indeed, some national political parties exist primarily for the purpose of expressing ideas and generating debate. App. 185\xe2\x80\x93186 (declaration of Stephen L. Dasbach et al. \xc2\xb6 ;11 (describing Libertarian Party)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As these activities illustrate, political parties often foster speech crucial to a healthy democracy, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 820 (Leon, J.), and fulfill the need for like-minded individuals to ban together and promote a political philosophy, see Jones, supra, at 574; Eu, supra, at 225. When political parties engage in pure political speech that has little or no potential to corrupt their federal candidates and officeholders, the government cannot constitutionally burden their speech any more than it could burden the speech of individuals engaging in these same activities. E.g., National Conservative Political Action Comm., supra, at 496\xe2\x80\x93497; Citizens Against Rent Control/Coalition for Fair Housing v. Berkeley, 454 U. S. 290, 297\xe2\x80\x93298 (1981); Buckley, 424 U. S., at 27. Notwithstanding the Court\xe2\x80\x99s citation to the numerous abuses of FECA, under any definition of \xe2\x80\x9cexacting scrutiny,\xe2\x80\x9d the means chosen by Congress, restricting all donations to national parties no matter the purpose for which they are given or are used, are not \xe2\x80\x9cclosely drawn to avoid unnecessary abridgment of associational freedoms,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 25.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s overinclusiveness is not limited to national political parties. To prevent the circumvention of the ban on the national parties\xe2\x80\x99 use of nonfederal funds, BCRA extensively regulates state parties, primarily state elections, and state candidates. For example, new FECA \xc2\xa7323(b), by reference to new FECA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7301(20)(A)(i)\xe2\x80\x93(ii), prohibits state parties from using nonfederal funds[Footnote 1] for general partybuilding activities such as voter registration, voter identification, and get out the vote for state candidates even if federal candidates are not mentioned. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441i(b), 431(20)(A)(i)\xe2\x80\x93(ii) (Supp. 2003). New FECA \xc2\xa7323(d) prohibits state and local political party committees, like their national counterparts, from soliciting and donating \xe2\x80\x9cany funds\xe2\x80\x9d to nonprofit organizations such as the National Rifle Association or the National Association for the Advancement of Colored People (NAACP). See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(d). And, new FECA \xc2\xa7323(f) requires a state gubernatorial candidate to abide by federal funding restrictions when airing a television ad that tells voters that, if elected, he would oppose the President\xe2\x80\x99s policy of increased oil and gas exploration within the State because it would harm the environment. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7441i(f), 431(20)(A)(iii) (regulating \xe2\x80\x9cpublic communication[s] that refe[r] to a clearly identified candidate for Federal office (regardless of whether a candidate for State or local office is also mentioned or identified) and that \xe2\x80\xa6 attacks or opposes a candidate for that office\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Although these provisions are more focused on activities that may affect federal elections, there is scant evidence in the record to indicate that federal candidates or officeholders are corrupted or would appear corrupted by donations for these activities. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 403, 407, 416, 422 (Henderson, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part); id., at 779\xe2\x80\x93780, 791 (Leon, J.); see also Colorado Republican Federal Campaign Comm. v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 518 U. S. 604, 616 (1996) (plurality opinion) (noting that \xe2\x80\x9cthe opportunity for corruption posed by [nonfederal contributions for state elections, get-out-the-vote, and voter registration activities] is, at best, attenuated\xe2\x80\x9d). Nonetheless, the Court concludes that because these activities benefit federal candidates and officeholders, see ante, at 59 or prevent the circumvention of pre-existing or contemporaneously enacted restrictions,[Footnote 2] see ante, at 57, 67, 71, 78, it must defer to the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98predictive judgments of Congress,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 57 (quoting Turner Broadcasting System, Inc. v. FCC, 512 U. S. 622, 665 (1994)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Yet the Court cannot truly mean what it says. Newspaper editorials and political talk shows benefit federal candidates and officeholders every bit as much as a generic voter registration drive conducted by a state party; there is little doubt that the endorsement of a major newspaper affects federal elections, and federal candidates and officeholders are surely \xe2\x80\x9cgrateful,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 60, for positive media coverage. I doubt, however, the Court would seriously contend that we must defer to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 judgment if it chose to reduce the influence of political endorsements in federal elections.[Footnote 3] See Miami Herald Publishing Co. v. Tornillo, 418 U. S. 241, 247, 250 (1974) (holding unconstitutional a state law that required newspapers to provide \xe2\x80\x9cright to reply\xe2\x80\x9d to any candidate who was personally or professionally assailed in order to eliminate the \xe2\x80\x9cabuses of bias and manipulative reportage\xe2\x80\x9d by the press).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is also true that any circumvention rationale ultimately must rest on the circumvention itself leading to the corruption of federal candidates and officeholders. See Buckley, 424 U. S., at 38 (upholding restrictions on funds donated to national political parties \xe2\x80\x9cfor the purpose of influencing any election for a Federal office\xe2\x80\x9d because they were prophylactic measures designed \xe2\x80\x9cto prevent evasion\xe2\x80\x9d of the contribution limit on candidates). All political speech that is not sifted through federal regulation circumvents the regulatory scheme to some degree or another, and thus by the Court\xe2\x80\x99s standard would be a \xe2\x80\x9cloophole\xe2\x80\x9d in the current system.[Footnote 4] Unless the Court would uphold federal regulation of all funding of political speech, a rationale dependent on circumvention alone will not do. By untethering its inquiry from corruption or the appearance of corruption, the Court has removed the touchstone of our campaign finance precedent and has failed to replace it with any logical limiting principle.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0But such an untethering is necessary to the Court\xe2\x80\x99s analysis. Only by using amorphous language to conclude a federal interest, however vaguely defined, exists can the Court avoid the obvious fact that new FECA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7323(a), (b), (d), and (f\xc2\xa0) are vastly overinclusive. Any campaign finance law aimed at reducing corruption will almost surely affect federal elections or prohibit the circumvention of federal law, and if broad enough, most laws will generally reduce some appearance of corruption. Indeed, it is precisely because broad laws are likely to nominally further a legitimate interest that we require Congress to tailor its restrictions; requiring all federal candidates to self-finance their campaigns would surely reduce the appearance of donor corruption, but it would hardly be constitutional. In allowing Congress to rely on general principles such as affecting a federal election or prohibiting the circumvention of existing law, the Court all but eliminates the \xe2\x80\x9cclosely drawn\xe2\x80\x9d tailoring requirement and meaningful judicial review.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0No doubt Congress was convinced by the many abuses of the current system that something in this area must be done. Its response, however, was too blunt. Many of the abuses described by the Court involve donations that were made for the \xe2\x80\x9cpurpose of influencing a federal election,\xe2\x80\x9d and thus are already regulated. See Buckley, supra. Congress could have sought to have the existing restrictions enforced or to enact other restrictions that are \xe2\x80\x9cclosely drawn\xe2\x80\x9d to its legitimate concerns. But it should not be able to broadly restrict political speech in the fashion it has chosen. Today\xe2\x80\x99s decision, by not requiring tailored restrictions, has significantly reduced the protection for political speech having little or nothing to do with corruption or the appearance of corruption.

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7504 amends \xc2\xa7315 of the Communications Act to require broadcast licensees to maintain and disclose records of any request to purchase broadcast time that \xe2\x80\x9cis made by or on behalf of a legally qualified candidate for public office\xe2\x80\x9d or that \xe2\x80\x9ccommunicates a message relating to any political matter of national importance,\xe2\x80\x9d including communications relating to \xe2\x80\x9ca legally qualified candidate,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cany election to Federal office,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9ca national legislative issue of public importance.\xe2\x80\x9d BCRA \xc2\xa7504; 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7315(e)(1) (Supp. 2003).[Footnote 5] This section differs from other BCRA disclosure sections because it requires broadcast licensees to disclose requests to purchase broadcast time rather than requiring purchasers to disclose their disbursements for broadcast time. See, e.g., BCRA \xc2\xa7201. The Court concludes that \xc2\xa7504 \xe2\x80\x9cmust survive a facial attack under any potentially applicable First Amendment standard, including that of heightened scrutiny.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 15 (opinion of Breyer, J.). I disagree.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This section is deficient because of the absence of a sufficient governmental interest to justify disclosure of mere requests to purchase broadcast time, as well as purchases themselves. The Court approaches \xc2\xa7504 almost exclusively from the perspective of the broadcast licensees, ignoring the interests of candidates and other purchasers, whose speech and association rights are affected by \xc2\xa7504. See, e.g., ante, at 5 (noting that broadcasters are subject to numerous recordkeeping requirements); ante, at 7 (opining that this Court has recognized \xe2\x80\x9cbroad governmental authority for agency information demands from regulated entities\xe2\x80\x9d); ante, at 8\xe2\x80\x939 (\xe2\x80\x9c[W]e cannot say that these requirements will impose disproportionate administrative burdens\xe2\x80\x9d). An approach that simply focuses on whether the administrative burden is justifiable is untenable. Because \xc2\xa7504 impinges on core First Amendment rights, it is subject to a more demanding test than mere rational-basis review. The Court applies the latter by asking essentially whether there is any conceivable reason to support \xc2\xa7504. See ante, at 8 (discussing the ways in which the disclosure \xe2\x80\x9ccan help\xe2\x80\x9d the FCC and the public); ante, at 10 (noting that the \xe2\x80\x9crecordkeeping requirements seem likely to help the FCC\xe2\x80\x9d enforce the fairness doctrine).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Required disclosure provisions that deter constitutionally protected association and speech rights are subject to heightened scrutiny. See Buckley, 424 U. S., at 64. When applying heightened scrutiny, we first ask whether the Government has asserted an interest sufficient to justify the disclosure of requests to purchase broadcast time. Ibid.; see ante, at 89 (joint opinion of Stevens and O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, JJ.) (concluding that the important state interests the Buckley Court held justified FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements apply to BCRA \xc2\xa7201\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirement). But the Government, in its brief, proffers no interest whatever to support \xc2\xa7504 as a whole.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Contrary to the Court\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion, ante, at 7 (opinion of Breyer, J.), the Government\xe2\x80\x99s brief does not succinctly present interests sufficient to support \xc2\xa7504. The two paragraphs that the Court relies on provide the following:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cAs explained in the government\xe2\x80\x99s brief in opposition to the motion for summary affirmance on this issue filed by plaintiff National Association of Broadcasters (NAB), longstanding FCC regulations impose disclosure requirements with respect to the sponsorship of broadcast matter \xe2\x80\x98involving the discussion of a controversial issue of public importance.\xe2\x80\x99 47 C.\xc2\xa0F.\xc2\xa0R. 73.1212(d) and (e) (2002); see 47 C.\xc2\xa0F.\xc2\xa0R. 76.1701(d) (2002) (same standard used in disclosure regulation governing cablecasting). By enabling viewers and listeners to identify the persons actually responsible for communications aimed at a mass audience, those regulations assist the public in evaluating the message transmitted. See Bellotti, 435 U. S. at 792 n.\xc2\xa032 (\xe2\x80\x98Identification of the source of advertising may be required \xe2\x80\xa6 so that the people will be able to evaluate the arguments to which they are being subjected.\xe2\x80\x99).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe range of information required to be disclosed under BCRA \xc2\xa7504 is comparable to the disclosures mandated by pre-existing FCC rules. Compare 47 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. 315(e)(2)(G) (added by BCRA \xc2\xa7504), with 47 C.\xc2\xa0F.\xc2\xa0R. 73.1212(e) and 76.1701(d) (2002). Plaintiffs do not attempt to show that BCRA \xc2\xa7504\xe2\x80\x99s requirements are more onerous than the FCC\xe2\x80\x99s longstanding rules, nor do they contend that the pre-existing agency regulations are themselves unconstitutional. See generally 02\xe2\x80\x931676 Gov\xe2\x80\x99t Br. in Opp. to Mot. of NAB for Summ. Aff. 4\xe2\x80\x939. Because BCRA \xc2\xa7504 is essentially a codification of established and unchallenged regulatory requirements, plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x99 First Amendment claim should be rejected.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for FEC et\xc2\xa0al. in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931674 et\xc2\xa0al., pp.\xc2\xa0132\xe2\x80\x93133; ante, at 7.

\n

\nWhile these paragraphs attempt to set forth a justification for the new Communications Act \xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B), discussed below, I fail to see any justification for BCRA \xc2\xa7504 in its entirety. Nor do I find persuasive the Court\xe2\x80\x99s and the Government\xe2\x80\x99s argument that pre-existing unchallenged agency regulations imposing similar disclosure requirements compel the conclusion that \xc2\xa7504 is constitutional and somehow relieve the Government of its burden of advancing a constitutionally sufficient justification for \xc2\xa7504.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At oral argument, the Government counsel indicated that one of the interests supporting \xc2\xa7504 in its entirety stems from the fairness doctrine, Tr. of Oral Arg. 192, which in general imposes an obligation on licensees to devote a \xe2\x80\x9creasonable percentage\xe2\x80\x9d of broadcast time to issues of public importance in a way that reflects opposing views. See Red Lion Broadcasting Co. v. FCC, 395 U. S. 367 (1969). Assuming, arguendo, this latter-day assertion should be considered, I think the District Court correctly noted that there is nothing in the record that indicates licensees have treated purchasers unfairly. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 812 (Leon, J.). In addition, this interest seems wholly unconnected to the central purpose of BCRA, and it is not at all similar to the governmental interests\nin Buckley that we found to be \xe2\x80\x9csufficiently important\nto outweigh the possibility of infringement,\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 66.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As to the disclosure requirements involving \xe2\x80\x9cany political matter of national importance\xe2\x80\x9d under the new Communications Act \xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(B), the Government suggests that the disclosure enables viewers to evaluate the message transmitted.[Footnote 6] First, insofar as BCRA \xc2\xa7504 requires reporting of \xe2\x80\x9crequest[s for] broadcast time\xe2\x80\x9d as well as actual broadcasts, it is not supported by this goal. Requests that do not mature into actual purchases will have no viewers, but the information may allow competitors or adversaries to obtain information regarding organizational or political strategies of purchasers. Second, even as to broadcasts themselves, in this noncandidate-related context, this goal is a far cry from the Government interests endorsed in Buckley, which were limited to evaluating and preventing corruption of federal candidates. Ibid.; see also McIntyre v. Ohio Elections Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 514 U. S. 334, 354 (1995).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As to disclosure requirements with respect to candidates under the new Communications Act \xc2\xa7315(e)(1)(A), BCRA \xc2\xa7504 significantly overlaps with \xc2\xa7201, which is today also upheld by this Court, ante, at 87\xe2\x80\x9395 (joint opinion of Stevens and O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, JJ.), and requires purchasers of \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications\xe2\x80\x9d to disclose a wide array of information, including the amount of each disbursement and the elections to which electioneering communications pertain. While I recognize that there is this overlap, \xc2\xa7504 imposes a different burden on the purchaser\xe2\x80\x99s First Amendment rights: as noted above, \xc2\xa7201 is limited to purchasers\xe2\x80\x99 disclosure of disbursements for electioneering communications, whereas \xc2\xa7504 requires broadcast licensees\xe2\x80\x99 disclosure of requests for broadcast time by purchasers. Not only are the purchasers\xe2\x80\x99 requests, which may never result in an actual advertisement, subject to the disclosure requirements, but \xc2\xa7504 will undoubtedly result in increased costs of communication because the licensees will shift the costs of the onerous disclosure and recordkeeping requirements to purchasers. The Government fails to offer a reason for the separate burden and apparent overlap.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government cannot justify, and for that matter, has not attempted to justify, its requirement that \xe2\x80\x9crequest[s for] broadcast\xe2\x80\x9d time be publicized. On the record before this Court, I cannot even speculate as to a governmental interest that would allow me to conclude that the disclosure of \xe2\x80\x9crequests\xe2\x80\x9d should be upheld. Such disclosure risks, inter alia, allowing candidates and political groups the opportunity to ferret out a purchaser\xe2\x80\x99s political strategy and, ultimately, unduly burdens the First Amendment freedoms of purchasers.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Absent some showing of a Government interest served by \xc2\xa7504 and in light of the breadth of disclosure of \xe2\x80\x9crequests,\xe2\x80\x9d I must conclude that \xc2\xa7504 fails to satisfy First Amendment scrutiny.

\n

*\xc2\xa0Justice Scalia and Justice Kennedy join this opinion in its\nentirety.

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Stevens, dissenting with respect to \xc2\xa7305.*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice, writing for the Court, concludes that the McConnell plaintiffs lack standing to challenge \xc2\xa7305 of BCRA because Senator McConnell cannot be affected by the provision until \xe2\x80\x9c45 days before the Republican primary election in 2008.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 4. I am not persuaded that Article III\xe2\x80\x99s case-or-controversy requirement imposes such a strict temporal limit on our jurisdiction. By asserting that he has run attack ads in the past, that he plans to run such ads in his next campaign, and that \xc2\xa7305 will adversely affect his campaign strategy, Senator McConnell has identified a \xe2\x80\x9cconcrete,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98distinct,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98actual\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d injury, Whitmore v. Arkansas, 495 U. S. 149, 155 (1990). That the injury is distant in time does not make it illusory.

\n
