\nCHAMBER OF COMMERCE OF THE UNITED STATES, et\xc2\xa0al., APPELLANTS

\n

\n02\xe2\x80\x931756\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0v.

\n

\nFEDERAL ELECTION COMMISSION, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on appeals from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[December 10, 2003]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas, concurring with respect to BCRA Titles III and IV, except for BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7311 and 318, concurring in the result with respect to BCRA \xc2\xa7318, concurring in the judgment in part and dissenting in part with respect to BCRA Title II, and dissenting with respect to BCRA Titles I, V, and \xc2\xa7311.*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The First Amendment provides that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress shall make no law \xe2\x80\xa6 abridging the freedom of speech.\xe2\x80\x9d Nevertheless, the Court today upholds what can only be described as the most significant abridgment of the freedoms of speech and association since the Civil War. With breathtaking scope, the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act of 2002 (BCRA), directly targets and constricts core political speech, the \xe2\x80\x9cprimary object of First Amendment protection.\xe2\x80\x9d Nixon v. Shrink Missouri Government PAC, 528 U. S. 377, 410\xe2\x80\x93411 (2000) (Thomas, J., dissenting). Because \xe2\x80\x9cthe First Amendment \xe2\x80\x99has its fullest and most urgent application\xe2\x80\x99 to speech uttered during a campaign for political office,\xe2\x80\x9d Eu v. San Francisco County Democratic Central Comm., 489 U. S. 214, 223 (1989) (quoting Monitor Patriot Co. v. Roy, 401 U. S. 265, 272 (1971)), our duty is to approach these restrictions \xe2\x80\x9cwith the utmost skepticism\xe2\x80\x9d and subject them to the \xe2\x80\x9cstrictest scrutiny.\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, supra, at 412 (Thomas, J., dissenting).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In response to this assault on the free exchange of ideas and with only the slightest consideration of the appropriate standard of review or of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s traditional role of protecting First Amendment freedoms, the Court has placed its imprimatur on these unprecedented restrictions. The very \xe2\x80\x9cpurpose of the First Amendment [is] to preserve an uninhibited marketplace of ideas in which truth will ultimately prevail.\xe2\x80\x9d Red Lion Broadcasting Co. v. FCC, 395 U. S. 367, 390 (1969). Yet today the fundamental principle that \xe2\x80\x9cthe best test of truth is the power of the thought to get itself accepted in the competition of the market,\xe2\x80\x9d Abrams v. United States, 250 U. S. 616, 630 (1919) (Holmes, J., dissenting), is cast aside in the purported service of preventing \xe2\x80\x9ccorruption,\xe2\x80\x9d or the mere \xe2\x80\x9cappearance of corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley v. Valeo, 424 U. S. 1, 26 (1976) (per curiam). Apparently, the marketplace of ideas is to be fully open only to defamers, New York Times Co. v. Sullivan, 376 U. S. 254 (1964); nude dancers, Barnes v. Glen Theatre, Inc., 501 U. S. 560 (1991) (plurality opinion); pornographers, Ashcroft v. Free Speech Coalition, 535 U. S. 234 (2002); flag burners, United States v. Eichman, 496 U. S. 310 (1990); and cross burners, Virginia v. Black, 538 U. S. ___ (2003).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Because I cannot agree with the treatment given by Justice Stevens\xe2\x80\x99 and Justice O\xe2\x80\x99Connor\xe2\x80\x99s opinion (hereinafter joint opinion) to speech that is \xe2\x80\x9cindispensable to the effective and intelligent use of the processes of popular government to shape the destiny of modern industrial society,\xe2\x80\x9d Thornhill v. Alabama, 310 U. S. 88, 103 (1940), I respectfully dissent. I also dissent from Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s opinion upholding BCRA \xc2\xa7504. I join The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s opinion in regards to BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7304, 305, 307, 316, 319, and 403(b); concur in the result as to \xc2\xa7318; and dissent from the opinion as to \xc2\xa7311. I also fully agree with Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s discussion of \xc2\xa7213 and join that portion of his opinion. Post, at 37\xe2\x80\x9338.

\n

I

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c[C]ampaign finance laws are subject to strict scrutiny,\xe2\x80\x9d Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Beaumont, 539 U. S. ___, ___ (2003) (slip op., at 1) (Thomas, J., dissenting), and thus Title I must satisfy that demanding standard even if it were (incorrectly) conceived of as nothing more than a contribution limitation. The defendants do not even attempt to defend Title I under this standard, and for good reason: The various restrictions imposed by Title I are much less narrowly tailored to target only corrupting or problematic donations than even the contribution limits in Shrink Missouri. See 528 U. S., at 427\xe2\x80\x93430 (Thomas, J., dissenting); see also Colorado Republican Federal Campaign Comm. v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 518 U. S. 604, 641\xe2\x80\x93644 (1996) (Thomas, J., dissenting) (Colorado I). And, as I have previously noted, it is unclear why \xe2\x80\x9c[b]ribery laws [that] bar precisely the quid pro quo arrangements that are targeted here\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cdisclosure laws\xe2\x80\x9d are not \xe2\x80\x9cless restrictive means of addressing [the Government\xe2\x80\x99s] interest in curtailing corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, supra, at 428.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The joint opinion not only continues the errors of Buckley v. Valeo, by applying a low level of scrutiny to contribution ceilings, but also builds upon these errors by expanding the anticircumvention rationale beyond reason. Admittedly, exploitation of an anticircumvention concept has a long pedigree, going back at least to Buckley itself. Buckley upheld a $1,000 contribution ceiling as a way to combat both the \xe2\x80\x9cactuality and appearance of corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 26. The challengers in Buckley contended both that bribery laws represented \xe2\x80\x9ca less restrictive means of dealing with \xe2\x80\x98proven and suspected quid pro quo arrangements,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 27, and that the $1,000 contribution ceiling was overbroad as \xe2\x80\x9cmost large contributors do not seek improper influence over a candidate\xe2\x80\x99s position or an officeholder\xe2\x80\x99s action,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 29. The Court rejected the first argument on the grounds that \xe2\x80\x9claws making criminal the giving and taking of bribes deal with only the most blatant and specific attempts of those with money to influence governmental action,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 27\xe2\x80\x9328, and rejected the second on the grounds that \xe2\x80\x9cit [is] difficult to isolate suspect contributions,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 30.[Footnote 1] But a broadly drawn bribery law[Footnote 2] would cover even subtle and general attempts to influence government officials corruptly, eliminating the Court\xe2\x80\x99s first concern. And, an effective bribery law would deter actual quid pro quos and would, in all likelihood, eliminate any appearance of corruption in the system.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Hence, at root, the Buckley Court was concerned that bribery laws could not be effectively enforced to prevent quid pro quos between donors and officeholders, and the only rational reading of Buckley is that it approved the $1,000 contribution ceiling on this ground. The Court then, however, having at least in part concluded that individual contribution ceilings were necessary to prevent easy evasion of bribery laws, proceeded to uphold a separate contribution limitation, using, as the only justification, the \xe2\x80\x9cprevent[ion] [of] evasion of the $1,000 contribution limitation.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 38. The need to prevent circumvention of a limitation that was itself an anticircumvention measure led to the upholding of another significant restriction on individuals\xe2\x80\x99 freedom of speech.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The joint opinion now repeats this process. New Federal Election Campaign Act of 1971 (FECA) \xc2\xa7323(a), 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7441i(a) (Supp. 2003), is intended to prevent easy circumvention of the (now) $2,000 contribution ceiling. The joint opinion even recognizes this, relying heavily on evidence that, for instance, \xe2\x80\x9ccandidates and donors alike have in fact exploited the soft-money loophole, the former to increase their prospects of election and the latter to create debt on the part of officeholders, with the national parties serving as willing intermediaries.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 36. The joint opinion upholds \xc2\xa7323(a), in part, on the grounds that it had become too easy to circumvent the $2,000 cap by using the national parties as go-betweens.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0And the remaining provisions of new FECA \xc2\xa7323 are upheld mostly as measures preventing circumvention of other contribution limits, including \xc2\xa7323(a), ante, at 55\xe2\x80\x9357 (\xc2\xa7323(b)); ante, at 66\xe2\x80\x9369 (\xc2\xa7323(d)); ante, at 75 (\xc2\xa7323(e)); ante, at 77\xe2\x80\x9378 (\xc2\xa7323(f)), which, as I have already explained, is a second-order anticircumvention measure. The joint opinion\xe2\x80\x99s handling of \xc2\xa7323(f) is perhaps most telling, as it upholds \xc2\xa7323(f) only because of \xe2\x80\x9cCongress\xe2\x80\x99 eminently reasonable prediction that \xe2\x80\xa6 state and local candidates and officeholders will become the next conduits for the soft-money funding of sham issue advertising.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 78 (emphasis added). That is, this Court upholds a third-order anticircumvention measure based on Congress\xe2\x80\x99 anticipation of circumvention of these second-order anticircumvention measures that might possibly, at some point in the future, pose some problem.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is not difficult to see where this leads. Every law has limits, and there will always be behavior not covered by the law but at its edges; behavior easily characterized as \xe2\x80\x9ccircumventing\xe2\x80\x9d the law\xe2\x80\x99s prohibition. Hence, speech regulation will again expand to cover new forms of \xe2\x80\x9ccircumvention,\xe2\x80\x9d only to spur supposed circumvention of the new regulations, and so forth. Rather than permit this never-ending and self-justifying process, I would require that the Government explain why proposed speech restrictions are needed in light of actual Government interests, and, in particular, why the bribery laws are not sufficient.

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0But Title I falls even on the joint opinion\xe2\x80\x99s terms. This Court has held that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he quantum of empirical evidence needed to satisfy heightened judicial scrutiny of legislative judgments will vary up or down with the novelty and plausibility of the justification raised.\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 391. And three Members of today\xe2\x80\x99s majority have observed that \xe2\x80\x9cthe opportunity for corruption\xe2\x80\x9d presented by \xe2\x80\x9c[u]nregulated \xe2\x80\x98soft money\xe2\x80\x99 contributions\xe2\x80\x9d is \xe2\x80\x9cat best, attenuated.\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado I, 518 U. S., at 616 (opinion of Breyer, J., joined by O\xe2\x80\x99Connor and Souter, JJ.). Such an observation is quite clearly correct. A donation to a political party is a clumsy method by which to influence a candidate, as the party is free to spend the donation however it sees fit, and could easily spend the money as to provide no help to the candidate. And, a soft-money donation to a party will be of even less benefit to a candidate, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause of legal restrictions on how the money may be spent.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for FEC et\xc2\xa0al. in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931674 et\xc2\xa0al., p.\xc2\xa043. It follows that the defendants bear an especially heavy empirical burden in justifying Title I.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The evidence cited by the joint opinion does not meet this standard and would barely suffice for anything more than rational-basis review. The first category of the joint opinion\xe2\x80\x99s evidence is evidence that \xe2\x80\x9cfederal officeholders have commonly asked donors to make soft-money donations to national and state committees solely in order to assist federal campaigns, including the officeholder\xe2\x80\x99s own.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 36 (internal quotation marks omitted). But to the extent that donors and federal officeholders have collaborated so that donors could give donations to a national party committee \xe2\x80\x9cfor the purpose of influencing any election for Federal office,\xe2\x80\x9d the alleged soft-money donation is in actuality a regular \xe2\x80\x9ccontribution\xe2\x80\x9d as already defined and regulated by FECA. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7431(8)(A)(i). Neither the joint opinion nor the defendants present evidence that enforcement of pre-BCRA law has proved to be impossible, ineffective, or even particularly difficult.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The second category is evidence that \xe2\x80\x9clobbyists, CEOs, and wealthy individuals\xe2\x80\x9d have \xe2\x80\x9cdonat[ed] substantial sums of soft money to national committees not on ideological grounds, but for the express purpose of securing influence over federal officials.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 37. Even if true (and the cited evidence consists of nothing more than vague allegations of wrongdoing), it is unclear why existing bribery laws could not address this problem. Again, neither the joint opinion nor the defendants point to evidence that the enforcement of bribery laws has been or would be ineffective. If the problem has been clear and widespread, as the joint opinion suggests, I would expect that convictions, or at least prosecutions, would be more frequent.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The third category is evidence characterized by the joint opinion as \xe2\x80\x9cconnect[ing] soft money to manipulations of the legislative calendar, leading to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 failure to enact, among other things, generic drug legislation, tort reform, and tobacco legislation.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 40. But the evidence for this is no stronger than the evidence that there has been actual vote buying or vote switching for soft money. The joint opinion\xe2\x80\x99s citations to the record do not stand for the propositions that they claim. For instance, the McCain declaration does not provide any evidence of any exchange of legislative action for donations of any kind (hard or soft).[Footnote 3] Neither do the Simpson or Simon declarations, with perhaps one exception effectively addressed by Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s opinion.[Footnote 4] See post, at 18\xe2\x80\x9319. In fact, the findings by two of the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s judges confirm that the evidence of any quid pro quo corruption is exceedingly weak, if not nonexistent. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 176, 349\xe2\x80\x93352 (DC 2003) (Henderson, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part); id., at 851\xe2\x80\x93853 (Leon, J.). The evidence cited by the joint opinion is properly described as \xe2\x80\x9cat best, [the Members of Congress\xe2\x80\x99] personal conjecture regarding the impact of soft money donations on the voting practices of their present and former colleagues.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 852 (Leon, J.).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The joint opinion also places a substantial amount of weight on the fact that \xe2\x80\x9cin 1996 and 2000, more than half of the top 50 soft-money donors gave substantial sums to both major national parties,\xe2\x80\x9d and suggests that this fact \xe2\x80\x9cleav[es] room for no other conclusion but that these donors were seeking influence, or avoiding retaliation, rather than promoting any particular ideology.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 38 (emphasis in original). But that is not necessarily the case. The two major parties are not perfect ideological opposites, and supporters or opponents of certain policies or ideas might find substantial overlap between the two parties. If donors feel that both major parties are in general agreement over an issue of importance to them, it is unremarkable that such donors show support for both parties. This commonsense explanation surely belies the joint opinion\xe2\x80\x99s too-hasty conclusion drawn from a relatively innocent fact.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court today finds such sparse evidence sufficient. This cannot be held to satisfy even the \xe2\x80\x9crelatively complaisant review\xe2\x80\x9d of Beaumont, 539 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 14), unless, as it appears, the Court intends to abdicate entirely its role.[Footnote 5]

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court is not content with \xe2\x80\x9cbalanc[ing] away First Amendment freedoms,\xe2\x80\x9d Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 410 (Thomas, J., dissenting), in the context of the restrictions imposed by Title I, which could arguably (if wrongly) be thought to be mere contribution limits. The Court also, in upholding virtually all of Title II, proceeds to do the same for limitations on expenditures, which constitute \xe2\x80\x9cpolitical expression \xe2\x80\x98at the core of our electoral process and of the First Amendment freedoms,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 39 (quoting Williams v. Rhodes, 393 U. S. 23, 32 (1968)). Today\xe2\x80\x99s holding continues a disturbing trend: the steady decrease in the level of scrutiny applied to restrictions on core political speech. See Buckley, supra, at 16 (First Amendment requires \xe2\x80\x9cexacting scrutiny\xe2\x80\x9d); Shrink Missouri, supra, at 387 (applying \xe2\x80\x9cBuckley\xe2\x80\x99s standard of scrutiny\xe2\x80\x9d); Beaumont, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 14) (referencing \xe2\x80\x9crelatively complaisant review\xe2\x80\x9d).[Footnote 6] Although this trend is most obvious in the review of contribution limits, it has now reached what even this Court today would presumably recognize as a direct restriction on core political speech: limitations on independent expenditures.

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Of course, by accepting Congress\xe2\x80\x99 expansion of what constitutes \xe2\x80\x9ccoordination\xe2\x80\x9d for purposes of treating expenditures as limitations, the Court can pretend that it is, in fact, still only restricting primarily \xe2\x80\x9ccontributions.\xe2\x80\x9d I need not say much about this illusion. I have already discussed how the language used in new FECA \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B)(ii) is, even under Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s framework, overly broad and restricts fully protected speech. See Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Colorado Republican Federal Campaign Comm., 533 U. S. 431, 467\xe2\x80\x93468 (2001) (Thomas, J., dissenting) (Colorado II). The particular language used, \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditures made by any person \xe2\x80\xa6 in cooperation, consultation, or concert with, or at the request or suggestion of, a national, State, or local committee of a political party,\xe2\x80\x9d BCRA \xc2\xa7214(a)(2), captures expenditures with \xe2\x80\x9cno constitutional difference\xe2\x80\x9d from \xe2\x80\x9ca purely independent one.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 468 (Thomas, J., dissenting).[Footnote 7] And new FECA \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(C), although using the neutral term \xe2\x80\x9ccoordinated,\xe2\x80\x9d certainly has the purpose of \xe2\x80\x9cclarif[ying] the scope of the preceding subsection, \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B),\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 95 (joint opinion), and thus should be read to be as expansive as the overly broad language in \xc2\xa7315(a)(7)(B). Hence, it too is unconstitutional.

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As for \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7203 and 204, the Court rests its decision on another vast expansion of the First Amendment framework described in Buckley, this time of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s, rather than Congress\xe2\x80\x99, own making. In Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce, 494 U. S. 652, 659\xe2\x80\x93660 (1990), the Court recognized a \xe2\x80\x9cdifferent type of corruption\xe2\x80\x9d from the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98financial quid pro quo\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d: the \xe2\x80\x9ccorrosive and distorting effects of immense aggregations of wealth that are accumulated with the help of the corporate form and that have little or no correlation to the public\xe2\x80\x99s support for the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d The only effect, however, that the \xe2\x80\x9cimmense aggregations\xe2\x80\x9d of wealth will have (in the context of independent expenditures) on an election is that they might be used to fund communications to convince voters to select certain candidates over others. In other words, the \xe2\x80\x9ccorrosive and distorting effects\xe2\x80\x9d described in Austin are that corporations, on behalf of their shareholders, will be able to convince voters of the correctness of their ideas. Apparently, winning in the marketplace of ideas is no longer a sign that \xe2\x80\x9cthe ultimate good\xe2\x80\x9d has been \xe2\x80\x9creached by free trade in ideas,\xe2\x80\x9d or that the speaker has survived \xe2\x80\x9cthe best test of truth\xe2\x80\x9d by having \xe2\x80\x9cthe thought \xe2\x80\xa6 get itself accepted in the competition of the market.\xe2\x80\x9d Abrams, 250 U. S., at 630 (Holmes, J., dissenting). It is now evidence of \xe2\x80\x9ccorruption.\xe2\x80\x9d This conclusion is antithetical to everything for which the First Amendment stands. See, e.g., First Nat. Bank of Boston v. Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765, 790 (1978) (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he fact that advocacy may persuade the electorate is hardly a reason to suppress it\xe2\x80\x9d); Kingsley Int\xe2\x80\x99l Pictures Corp. v. Regents of Univ. of N. Y., 360 U. S. 684, 689 (1959) (\xe2\x80\x9c[I]n the realm of ideas [the Constitution] protects expression which is eloquent no less than that which is unconvincing\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Because Austin\xe2\x80\x99s definition of \xe2\x80\x9ccorruption\xe2\x80\x9d is incompatible with the First Amendment, I would overturn Austin and hold that the potential for corporations and unions to influence voters, via independent expenditures aimed at convincing these voters to adopt particular views, is not a form of corruption justifying any state regulation or suppression. Without Austin\xe2\x80\x99s peculiar variation of \xe2\x80\x9ccorruption,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7203 and 204 are supported by no compelling government interest. The joint opinion does not even argue that these provisions address quid pro quo corruption.[Footnote 8] And the shareholder protection rationale is equally unavailing. The \xe2\x80\x9cshareholder invests in a corporation of his own volition and is free to withdraw his investment at any time and for any reason,\xe2\x80\x9d Bellotti, supra, at 794, n.\xc2\xa034. Hence, no compelling interest can be found in protecting minority shareholders from the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s use of its general treasury, especially where, in other contexts, \xe2\x80\x9cequally important and controversial corporate decisions are made by management or by a predetermined percentage of the shareholders.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

C

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I must now address an issue on which I differ from all of my colleagues: the disclosure provisions in BCRA \xc2\xa7201, now contained in new FECA \xc2\xa7304(f). The \xe2\x80\x9chistorical evidence indicates that Founding-era Americans opposed attempts to require that anonymous authors reveal their identities on the ground that forced disclosure violated the \xe2\x80\x98freedom of the press.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d McIntyre v. Ohio Elections Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 514 U. S. 334, 361 (1995) (Thomas, J., concurring).[Footnote 9] Indeed, this Court has explicitly recognized that \xe2\x80\x9cthe interest in having anonymous works enter the marketplace of ideas unquestionably outweighs any public interest in requiring disclosure as a condition of entry,\xe2\x80\x9d and thus that \xe2\x80\x9can author\xe2\x80\x99s decision to remain anonymous \xe2\x80\xa6 is an aspect of the freedom of speech protected by the First Amendment.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 342. The Court now backs away from this principle, allowing the established right to anonymous speech to be stripped away based on the flimsiest of justifications.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The only plausible interest asserted by the defendants to justify the disclosure provisions is the interest in providing \xe2\x80\x9cinformation\xe2\x80\x9d about the speaker to the public. But we have already held that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he simple interest in providing voters with additional relevant information does not justify a state requirement that a writer make statements or disclosures she would otherwise omit.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 348. Of course, Buckley upheld the disclosure requirement on expenditures for communications using words of express advocacy based on this informational interest. 424 U. S., at 81. And admittedly, McIntyre purported to distinguish Buckley. McIntyre, supra, at 355\xe2\x80\x93356. But the two ways McIntyre distinguished Buckley\xe2\x80\x94one, that the disclosure of \xe2\x80\x9can expenditure and its use, without more, reveals far less information [than a forced identification of the author of a pamphlet,]\xe2\x80\x9d 514 U. S., at 355; and two, that in candidate elections, the \xe2\x80\x9cGovernment can identify a compelling state interest in avoiding the corruption that might result from campaign expenditures,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 356\xe2\x80\x94are inherently implausible. The first is simply wrong. The revelation of one\xe2\x80\x99s political expenditures for independent communications about candidates can be just as revealing as the revelation of one\xe2\x80\x99s name on a pamphlet for a noncandidate election. See also id., at 384 (Scalia, J., dissenting). The second was outright rejected in Buckley itself, where the Court concluded that independent expenditures did not create any substantial risk of real or apparent corruption. 424 U. S., at 47. Hence, the only reading of McIntyre that remains consistent with the principles it contains is that it overturned Buckley to the extent that Buckley upheld a disclosure requirement solely based on the governmental interest in providing information to the voters.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The right to anonymous speech cannot be abridged based on the interests asserted by the defendants. I would thus hold that the disclosure requirements of BCRA \xc2\xa7201 are unconstitutional. Because of this conclusion, the so-called advance disclosure requirement of \xc2\xa7201 necessarily falls as well.[Footnote 10]

\n

D

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I have long maintained that Buckley was incorrectly decided and should be overturned. See Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 465; Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 410; Colorado I, 518 U. S., at 640. But, most of Title II should still be held unconstitutional even under the Buckley framework. Under Buckley and Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Massachusetts Citizens for Life, Inc., 479 U. S. 238 (1986) (MCFL), it is, or at least was, clear that any regulation of political speech beyond communications using words of express advocacy is unconstitutional. Hence, even under the joint opinion\xe2\x80\x99s framework, most of Title II is unconstitutional, as both the \xe2\x80\x9cprimary definition\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cbackup definition\xe2\x80\x9d of \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications\xe2\x80\x9d cover a significant number of communications that do not use words of express advocacy. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.\xc2\xa0A. \xc2\xa7434(f)(3)(A) (Supp. 2003).[Footnote 11]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In Buckley, the Court was presented with the ambiguous language \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98any expenditure \xe2\x80\xa6 relative to a clearly identified candidate.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 41. The Court noted that the \xe2\x80\x9cuse of so indefinite a phrase as \xe2\x80\x98relative to\xe2\x80\x99 a candidate fails to clearly mark the boundary between permissible and impermissible speech.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Hence, the Court read the phrase to mean \xe2\x80\x9cadvocating the election or defeat of a candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 42 (internal quotation marks omitted). But this construction did not complete the vagueness inquiry. As the Court observed:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he distinction between discussion of issues and candidates and advocacy of election or defeat of candidates may often dissolve in practical application. Candidates, especially incumbents, are intimately tied to public issues involving legislative proposals and governmental actions. Not only do candidates campaign on the basis of their positions on various public issues, but campaigns themselves generate issues of public interest.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\nThe Court then recognized that the constitutional issues raised by the provision \xe2\x80\x9ccan be avoided only by reading \xc2\xa7608(e)(1) as limited to communications that include explicit words of advocacy of election or defeat of a candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 43.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The joint opinion argues that Buckley adopted this narrow reading only to avoid addressing a constitutional question. \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he concept of express advocacy and the concomitant class of magic words were born of an effort to avoid constitutional infirmities,\xe2\x80\x9d concludes the joint opinion after examining the language of Buckley. Ante, at 85. This ignores the fact that the Court then struck down the expenditure limitation precisely because it was too narrow:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cThe exacting interpretation of the statutory language necessary to avoid unconstitutional vagueness thus undermines the limitation\xe2\x80\x99s effectiveness as a loophole-closing provision by facilitating circumvention by those seeking to exert improper influence upon a candidate or officeholder. It would naively underestimate the ingenuity and resourcefulness of persons and groups desiring to buy influence to believe that they would have much difficulty devising expenditures that skirted the restriction on express advocacy of election or defeat but nevertheless benefited the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s campaign. Yet no substantial societal interest would be served by a loophole-closing provision designed to check corruption that permitted unscrupulous persons and organizations to expend unlimited sums of money in order to obtain improper influence over candidates for elective office.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 45.

\n

\nFar from saving the provision from constitutional doubt, the Court read the provision in such a way as to guarantee its unconstitutionality. If there were some possibility that regulation of communications without words of express advocacy were constitutional, the provision would have to have been read to include these communications, and the constitutional question addressed head on.[Footnote 12] Indeed, the exceedingly narrow reading of the relevant language in Buckley is far from mandated by the text; it is, in fact, a highly strained reading. \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[A]ny expenditure \xe2\x80\xa6 relative to a clearly identified candidate,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 41, would be better read to cover, for instance, any expenditure for an advertisement aired close to an election that is \xe2\x80\x9cintended to influence the voters\xe2\x80\x99 decisions and ha[s] that effect,\xe2\x80\x9d a standard apparently endorsed by the joint opinion as being sufficiently \xe2\x80\x9cequivalent\xe2\x80\x9d to express advocacy to justify its regulation. Ante, at 99\xe2\x80\x93100. By deliberately adopting a strained and narrow reading of the statutory text and then striking down the provision in question for being too narrow, the Court made clear that regulation of nonexpress advocacy was strictly forbidden.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This reading is confirmed by other portions of Buckley and by other cases. For instance, in limiting FECA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure provisions to expenditures involving express advocacy, the Court noted that it gave such a narrowing interpretation \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o insure that the reach of [the disclosure provision] is not impermissibly broad.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 80 (emphasis added). If overbreadth were a concern in limiting the scope of a disclosure provision, it surely was equally a concern in the limitation of an actual cap on expenditures. And, in MCFL, the Court arguably eliminated any ambiguity remaining in Buckley when it explicitly stated that the narrowing interpretations taken in Buckley were necessary \xe2\x80\x9cin order to avoid problems of overbreadth.\xe2\x80\x9d MCFL, 479 U. S., at 248. The joint opinion\xe2\x80\x99s attempt to explain away MCFL\xe2\x80\x99s uncomfortable language is unpersuasive. The joint opinion emphasizes that the MCFL Court \xe2\x80\x9cheld that a \xe2\x80\x98similar construction\xe2\x80\x99 must apply to the expenditure limitation,\xe2\x80\x9d as if that somehow proved its point. Ante, at 85, n.\xc2\xa076 (emphasis in original). The fact that the MCFL Court said this does not establish anything, of course; adopting a narrow construction of a statute \xe2\x80\x9cin order to avoid problems of overbreadth,\xe2\x80\x9d 479 U. S., at 248, is perfectly consistent with a holding that, lacking the narrowing construction, the statute would be overly broad, i.e., unconstitutional.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The defendants\xe2\x80\x99 principal argument in response is that

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cit would be bizarre to conclude that the Constitution permits Congress to prohibit the use of corporate or union general treasury funds for electioneering advertisements, but that the only standard that it can constitutionally use (express advocacy) is one that misses the vast majority (88.6 percent) of advertisements that candidates themselves use for electioneering.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for FEC et\xc2\xa0al. in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931674 et\xc2\xa0al., p.\xc2\xa0103 (emphasis in original).

\n

\nThe joint opinion echoes this, stating that the express advocacy line \xe2\x80\x9ccannot be squared with our longstanding recognition that the presence or absence of magic words cannot meaningfully distinguish electioneering speech from a true issue ad.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 86. First, the presence of the \xe2\x80\x9cmagic words\xe2\x80\x9d does differentiate in a meaningful way between categories of speech. Speech containing the \xe2\x80\x9cmagic words\xe2\x80\x9d is \xe2\x80\x9cunambiguously campaign related,\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, supra, at 81, while speech without these words is not. Second, it is far from bizarre to suggest that (potentially regulable) speech that is in practice impossible to differentiate from fully protected speech must be fully protected. It is, rather, part and parcel of First Amendment first principles. See, e.g., Free Speech Coalition, 535 U. S., at 255 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe Government may not suppress lawful speech as the means to suppress unlawful speech. Protected speech does not become unprotected merely because it resembles the latter. The Constitution requires the reverse\xe2\x80\x9d). In fact, First Amendment protection was extended to that fundamental category of artistic and entertaining speech not for its own sake, but only because it was indistinguishable, practically, from speech intended to inform. See Joseph Burstyn, Inc. v. Wilson, 343 U. S. 495, 501 (1952); Winters v. New York, 333 U. S. 507, 510 (1948) (rejecting suggestion that \xe2\x80\x9cthe constitutional protection for a free press applies only to the exposition of ideas\xe2\x80\x9d as the \xe2\x80\x9cline between the informing and the entertaining is too elusive for the protection of that basic right,\xe2\x80\x9d noting that \xe2\x80\x9c[w]hat is one man\xe2\x80\x99s amusement, teaches another\xe2\x80\x99s doctrine\xe2\x80\x9d). This principle clearly played a significant role in Buckley itself, see 424 U. S., at 42 (after noting that \xe2\x80\x9cthe distinction between discussion of issues and candidates and advocacy of election or defeat of candidates may often dissolve in practical application,\xe2\x80\x9d holding that the \xe2\x80\x9cexpress advocacy\xe2\x80\x9d standard must be adopted as the interpretation of the relevant language in FECA). The express-advocacy line was drawn to ensure the protection of the \xe2\x80\x9cdiscussion of issues and candidates,\xe2\x80\x9d not out of some strange obsession of the Court to create meaningless lines. And the joint opinion misses the point when it notes that \xe2\x80\x9cBuckley\xe2\x80\x99s express advocacy line, in short, has not aided the legislative effort to combat real or apparent corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 86\xe2\x80\x9387. Buckley did not draw this line solely to aid in combating real or apparent corruption, but rather also to ensure the protection of speech unrelated to election campaigns.[Footnote 13]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Nor is this to say that speech with words of express advocacy is somehow less protected, as the joint opinion claims. Ante, at 99. The Court in Buckley recognized an informational interest that justified the imposition of a disclosure requirement on campaign-related speech. See 424 U. S., at 81. This interest is not implicated with regard to speech that is unrelated to an election campaign. Hence, it would be unconstitutional to impose such a disclosure requirement on non-election-related speech. And, as \xe2\x80\x9cthe distinction between discussion of issues and candidates \xe2\x80\xa6 may often dissolve in practical application,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 42, the only way to prevent the unjustified burdening of nonelection speech is to impose the regulation only on speech that is \xe2\x80\x9cunambiguously campaign related,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 81, i.e., speech using words of express advocacy. Hence, speech that uses words of express advocacy is protected under the same standard, strict scrutiny, as all other forms of speech. The only difference is that, under Buckley, there is a governmental interest supporting some regulation of those using words of express advocacy not present in other forms of speech.

\n

* * *

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The chilling endpoint of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning is not difficult to foresee: outright regulation of the press. None of the rationales offered by the defendants, and none of the reasoning employed by the Court, exempts the press. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is so because of the difficulty, and perhaps impossibility, of distinguishing, either as a matter of fact or constitutional law, media corporations from [nonmedia] corporations.\xe2\x80\x9d Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 796 (Burger, C.\xc2\xa0J., concurring). Media companies can run procandidate editorials as easily as nonmedia corporations can pay for advertisements. Candidates can be just as grateful to media companies as they can be to corporations and unions. In terms of \xe2\x80\x9cthe corrosive and distorting effects\xe2\x80\x9d of wealth accumulated by corporations that has \xe2\x80\x9clittle or no correlation to the public\xe2\x80\x99s support for the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political ideas,\xe2\x80\x9d Austin, 494 U. S., at 660, there is no distinction between a media corporation and a nonmedia corporation.[Footnote 14] Media corporations are influential. There is little doubt that the editorials and commentary they run can affect elections. Nor is there any doubt that media companies often wish to influence elections. One would think that the New York Times fervently hopes that its endorsement of Presidential candidates will actually influence people. What is to stop a future Congress from determining that the press is \xe2\x80\x9ctoo influential,\xe2\x80\x9d and that the \xe2\x80\x9cappearance of corruption\xe2\x80\x9d is significant when media organizations endorse candidates or run \xe2\x80\x9cslanted\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cbiased\xe2\x80\x9d news stories in favor of candidates or parties? Or, even easier, what is to stop a future Congress from concluding that the availability of unregulated media corporations creates a loophole that allows for easy \xe2\x80\x9ccircumvention\xe2\x80\x9d of the limitations of the current campaign finance laws?[Footnote 15]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Indeed, I believe that longstanding and heretofore unchallenged opinions such as Miami Herald Publishing Co. v. Tornillo, 418 U. S. 241 (1974), are in peril. There, the Court noted that \xe2\x80\x9c[c]hains of newspapers, national newspapers, national wire and news services, and one-newspaper towns, are the dominant features of a press that has become noncompetitive and enormously powerful and influential in its capacity to manipulate popular opinion and change the course of events.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 249. Despite expressing some sympathy for those arguing for a legally created \xe2\x80\x9cright of access\xe2\x80\x9d to encourage diversity in viewpoints in the media, the Court struck down such laws, noting that these laws acted both to suppress speech and to \xe2\x80\x9cintru[de] into the function of editors\xe2\x80\x9d by interfering with \xe2\x80\x9cthe exercise of editorial control and judgment.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 257\xe2\x80\x93258. Now, supporters of such laws need only argue that the press\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9ccapacity to manipulate popular opinion\xe2\x80\x9d gives rise to an \xe2\x80\x9cappearance of corruption,\xe2\x80\x9d especially when this capacity is used to promote a particular candidate or party. After drumming up some evidence,[Footnote 16] laws regulating media outlets in their issuance of editorials would be upheld under the joint opinion\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning (a result considered so beyond the pale in Miami Herald Publishing that the Court there used it as a reductio ad absurdum against the right-of-access law being addressed, see id., at 256). Nor is there anything in the joint opinion that would prevent Congress from imposing the Fairness Doctrine, not just on radio and television broadcasters, but on the entire media. See Red Lion Broadcasting, 395 U. S., at 369 (defining the \xe2\x80\x9cfairness doctrine\xe2\x80\x9d as a \xe2\x80\x9crequirement that discussion of public issues be presented \xe2\x80\xa6 and that each side of those issues must be given fair coverage\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Hence, \xe2\x80\x9cthe freedom of the press,\xe2\x80\x9d described as \xe2\x80\x9cone of the greatest bulwarks of liberty,\xe2\x80\x9d 1 J. Elliot, Debates on the Federal Constitution 335 (___ ed. 1876) (declaration of Rhode Island upon the ratification of the Constitution),[Footnote 17] could be next on the chopping block. Although today\xe2\x80\x99s opinion does not expressly strip the press of First Amendment protection, there is no principle of law or logic that would prevent the application of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning in that setting. The press now operates at the whim of Congress.

\n

*\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Scalia joins Parts I, II\xe2\x80\x93A, and II\xe2\x80\x93B of this opinion.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0The Court also rejected an overbreadth challenge, reasoning that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress was justified in concluding that the interest in safeguarding against the appearance of impropriety requires that the opportunity for abuse inherent in the process of raising large monetary contributions be eliminated.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 30. But this justification was inextricably intertwined with the Court\xe2\x80\x99s concern over the difficulty of isolating suspect contributions. If it were easy to isolate suspect contributions, and if bribery laws could be quickly and effectively enforced, then there would be no \xe2\x80\x9copportunity for abuse inherent in the process,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., and hence no need for an otherwise overbroad contribution ceiling.

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0Arguably, the current antibribery statute, 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7201, is broad enough to cover the unspecified other \xe2\x80\x9cattempts \xe2\x80\xa6 to influence governmental action\xe2\x80\x9d that the Buckley Court seemed worried about. 424 U. S., at 28.

\nFootnote 3

\xc2\xa0Indeed, the principal contents of Senator McCain\xe2\x80\x99s declaration are his complaints that several bills he supported were defeated. The Senator also suggests, without evidence, that there had been some connection between the defeat of his favored policy outcomes and certain soft-money donors. See, e.g., App. 393\xe2\x80\x93394, \xc2\xb6 ;10 (declaration of Sen. John McCain \xc2\xb6 ;10) (noting Democratic \xe2\x80\x9cparliamentary procedural device\xe2\x80\x9d used to block one of Senator McCain\xe2\x80\x99s proposed amendments to the Sarbanes-Oxley corporate governance bill). The possibility that his favored policy outcomes lost due to lack of public support, or because the opponents of the amendment honestly believed it would do harm to the public, does not appear to be addressed.

\xc2\xa0Because there is not an iota of evidence supporting the Government\xe2\x80\x99s asserted interests in BCRA \xc2\xa7318, I concur in the Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that this provision is unconstitutional.

\xc2\xa0The joint opinion continues yet another disturbing trend: the application of a complaisant level of scrutiny under the guise of \xe2\x80\x9cstrict scrutiny.\xe2\x80\x9d See Grutter v. Bollinger, 539 U. S. ___ (2003).

\nFootnote 7

\xc2\xa0This is doubly so now that the Court has decided that there is no constitutional need for the showing even of an \xe2\x80\x9cagreement\xe2\x80\x9d in order to transform an expenditure into a \xe2\x80\x9ccoordinated expenditur[e]\xe2\x80\x9d and hence into a contribution for FECA purposes. Ante, at 115\xe2\x80\x93117 (joint opinion).

\nFootnote 8

\xc2\xa0The National Rifle Association (NRA) plaintiffs compellingly state that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]s a measure designed to prevent official corruption, of either the quid pro quo or the \xe2\x80\x98gratitude\xe2\x80\x99 variety, Title II \xe2\x80\xa6 makes no more sense than a bribery statute requiring corporations to pay for their bribes using funds from PACs.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Appellants NRA et\xc2\xa0al. in No. 02\xe2\x80\x931675, pp.\xc2\xa024\xe2\x80\x9325. \nFootnote 9

\xc2\xa0The fact that the Founders located the right to anonymous speech in the \xe2\x80\x9cfreedom of the press\xe2\x80\x9d is of no moment, as \xe2\x80\x9cit makes little difference in terms of our analysis, which seeks to determine only whether the First Amendment, as originally understood, protects anonymous writing.\xe2\x80\x9d McIntyre, 514 U. S., at 360 (Thomas, J., concurring).

\nFootnote 10

\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7212(a) is also unconstitutional. Although the plaintiffs only challenge the advance disclosure requirement of \xc2\xa7212(a), by requiring disclosure of communications using express advocacy, the entire reporting requirement is unconstitutional for the same reasons that \xc2\xa7201 is unconstitutional. Consequently, it follows that the advance disclosure provision is unconstitutional.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7311 and 504 also violate the First Amendment. By requiring any television or radio advertisement that satisfies the definition of \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication\xe2\x80\x9d to include the identity of the sponsor, and even a \xe2\x80\x9cfull-screen view of a representative of the political committee or other person making the statement\xe2\x80\x9d in the case of a television advertisement, new FECA \xc2\xa7318, \xc2\xa7311 is a virtual carbon copy of the law at issue in McIntyre v. Ohio Elections Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 514 U. S. 334 (1995) (the only difference being the irrelevant distinction between a printed pamphlet and a television or radio advertisement). And \xc2\xa7504 not only has the precise flaws of \xc2\xa7201, but also sweeps broadly as well, covering any \xe2\x80\x9cmessage relating to any political matter of national importance, including \xe2\x80\xa6 a national legislative issue of public importance.\xe2\x80\x9d Hence, both \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7311 and 504 should be struck down.

\nFootnote 11

\xc2\xa0The Court, in upholding most of its provisions by concluding that the \xe2\x80\x9cexpress advocacy\xe2\x80\x9d limitation derived by Buckley is not a constitutionally mandated line, has, in one blow, overturned every Court of Appeals that has addressed this question (except, perhaps, one). See Clifton v. FEC, 114 F.\xc2\xa03d 1309, 1312 (CA1 1997); Vermont Right to Life Comm., Inc. v. Sorrell, 221 F.\xc2\xa03d 376, 387 (CA2 2000); FEC v. Christian Action Network, Inc., 110 F.\xc2\xa03d 1049, 1064 (CA4 1997); Chamber of Commerce v. Moore, 288 F.\xc2\xa03d 187, 193 (CA5 2000); Iowa Right to Life Comm., Inc. v. Williams, 187 F.\xc2\xa03d 963, 968\xe2\x80\x93970 (CA8 1999); Citizens for Responsible Govt. State Political Action Comm. v. Davidson, 236 F.\xc2\xa03d 1174, 1187 (CA10 2000). The one possible exception is the Ninth Circuit. See FEC v. Furgatch, 807 F.\xc2\xa02d 857, 862\xe2\x80\x93863 (1987).

\nFootnote 12

\xc2\xa0After all, the constitutional avoidance doctrine counsels us to adopt constructions of statutes to \xe2\x80\x9cavoid decision of constitutional questions,\xe2\x80\x9d not to deliberately create constitutional questions. United States v. Thirty&nbhyph;seven Photographs, 402 U. S. 363, 373 (1971); see also United States ex rel. Attorney General v. Delaware & Hudson Co., 213 U. S. 366, 408 (1909).

\nFootnote 13

\xc2\xa0This very case is an excellent example of why such a bright-line rule is necessary. The Court, having \xe2\x80\x9crejected the notion that the First Amendment requires Congress to treat so-called issue advocacy differently from express advocacy,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 87\xe2\x80\x9388, proceeds to uphold significant new restrictions on speech that is, in every sense of the word, pure issue-related speech. The Court abandons the bright-line rule, and now subjects political speech of virtually any kind to the risk of regulation by Congress.

\nFootnote 14

\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Burger presciently commented on precisely this point in First Nat. Bank of Boston v. Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765, 796\xe2\x80\x93797 (1978) (citations omitted):

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn terms of \xe2\x80\x98unfair advantage in the political process\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98corporate domination of the electoral process,\xe2\x80\x99 it could be argued that such media conglomerates as I describe pose a much more realistic threat to valid interests than do appellants and similar entities not regularly concerned with shaping popular opinion on public issues. See Miami Herald Publishing Co. v. Tornillo, [418 U. S. 241 (1974)]. In Tornillo, for example, we noted the serious contentions advanced that a result of the growth of modern media empires \xe2\x80\x98has been to place in a few hands the power to inform the American people and shape public opinion.\xe2\x80\x99 418 U. S., at 250.\xe2\x80\x9d
